THE RED PAGE.
J. B. STEPHENS: SOME
LETTERS.
SOME years ago I spent a long, delightful
evening with Brunton Stephens in his Brisbane
home. It was the first, the last, the time
that 1 saw him at leisure ; for in chance meetings
he was usually too much occupied with office work to be
able to disengage his thought. Yet in that evening we
were knit closely together, and for the rest of his life he
honoured me with a correspondence critical, admonitory,
always kindly. Our friendly intercourse was never
broken —not even when, in the course of a commentator’s
work, his Convict Once was called to unsparing- judg-
ment. He himself, he said, had passed through “ mental
turbulence” to “hard-won serenity,” but he would not
admit that honest difference of opinion, however
decisively expressed, could ever shadow one’s personal
or literary relationship with others.
In half-a-dozen years, indeed, I knew him to lose his
serenity twice only, and for a moment only. Once, when
he thought F. Kenna misrepresented his attitude
to Literature, and particularly to Poetry. Then he
flamed into unexpected violence of epithets—withdrawn,
one after the other, as soon as he had posted his com-
plaint, in a curious succession of telegrams and letters.
The other time, wherr Tennyson’s poetical divinity was
called in question. Stephens felt far more keenly for
Tennyson than for Burris, than for himself. His own
work he valued little ; Burns he valued highly ; but
Tennyson he valued with the very highest.
One of Brunton Stephens’s best qualities was
his mental alertness, his spiritual youth. At sixty years
and more he preserved a boyish enthusiasm for the fine
thing in literature. His enthusiasm was qualified, but
not impaired, by unusually good judgment. He had
been a companion of the best writers in several languages,
and his synthesis of impressions had flowered into a taste
always refined, always reasonable. As with the rest of us,
his habit of mind coloured his verdicts ; yet only in the
case of Tennyson did he show impatience when they were
called in question. Tennyson was woven into the inmost
fibres of his life.
And he was as eager for the New as he was delighted
with the Old. To the end he swam in the fresh current of
letters, returning now and then to the firm banks of
the past. He was one of the first to welcome Stephen
Phillips ; the next week he was deep in Brantome ; just
before his death he wrote to me that he was “ obsessed ”
with Dante.
I have been living in Dante’s mind for a long time.
During the last month of my long illness of last winter
[l9ol] I took up Dante (original Italian, of course), and
ever since then I have been entirely obsessed with
Dante. I have gone slowly through the Inferno three
times and the Purgatoriv once, and am now wrestling
with the ParadisO, which contains more obscure pas-
sages than the other two put together. I used to think
there must be a lot of affectation about the extravagant
praises of Dantean scholars and critics, but I novv find
that “ the half had not been told me.”
Jt
To Stephens’s sweet and kindly nature all who
knew him can testify. He was especially the friend of
young Australian writers. He lifted them all, he relished
them-all, and if he did not sometimes say all he thought
about them—well, as he said, he did not sit in the critic’s
chair. He was ready to help and encourage : blame
could come from others. With all his appreciation of
form, he was keenly alive to force, emotion, fire. “ A
good line, a good verse, a good poem,” he told me,
“ affects me physically. I feel little wiggles down my
spine.” In letters of 1897 he repeats this and other
personalia—
I am singularly unable to give a reason for my likes
and dislikes in poetry. I know by the physical tingle
it communicates to me that I have come in contact
with genuine poetry The few things of Boake’s 1
have seen struck me as showing a strong and original
personality. Had he lived, I think he would have put
all our noses out of joint.. ..
I have to break this off in order to get my chest and
back rubbed with hartshorn liniment. But I don’t
know what good that will do to an illness which is
clearly established in my mind as the result of a long
course of severely hygienic living.. ..
As to that missing packet, I am quite clear that I
posted it. Even if all the other details were hazy, my
delighted surprise (I suppose you are aware I’m a
Scotchman) at finding upon weighing it that it would
be twopence less than I expected will always remain
indelibly impressed on my memory.. ..
Do you know I have a very faint belief in there being
as a rule any particularly close connection between the
man and the writer. In reading Jowett’s Life the
other day, I was pleased to find my own scepticism on
that point corroborated by his—if that is a proper way
of expressing myself. I think, moreover, that the
environment theory, like the theory of heredity, has
been run to death. Taine put me quite out of conceit
with both. As regards my own case, I really do not
think—all modesty apart—that the question of the
* connection between the two capacities is worth the
trouble of investigating.. Won’t it be enough to deal
with the poems? I deliver them over to your mercy
entirely. Work your wickedest will on them. I’ve
always been expecting to be found-out some day. Show
up the impostor. Nail him to the Red Page like a
vermin on a barn-door. But oh ! spare the poor spin-
dle-shanked nudity beneath the singing robes, which
he only uses for the express purpose of hiding his
shame.
J*
When Henley’s criticism of Burns appeared,
Stephens wrote—
lam still pondering your Burns. Very umviPing to
give in. And even granting all, is he the less a force,
and didn’t the force come from him, original-, native
to him? Balls are commonplace articles—so much so
that you don’t think of calling the ball an invention at
all. It is the powder that is the grand invention.
Burns was the powder that gave the ball its force. That
is the sort of line my ideas are taking. You see I
can’t give up my Burns ; though, to be honest, I never
ranked him with the first order of genius.
Later, he recurred to the subject—
About Burns. The indictment is no doubt very strong
But I am still seeking to know how far Burns pre-
tended to originality in the matter of these old
vamped-up songs. Of course, the settling of that
point would not alter the fact of Burns being not so
great a writer as one had previously believed. But
there still remains Burns the great inspiring Force—
and that is something unquestionably big. Anyway, I
am not so much shocked as many Scotchmen would be,
as I never ranked Burns in first order of great poets—
still less of great minds.
*
An article based on Prof. Sully’s Children’s
Ways interested Stephens. He debated the csnclusions
keenly—
Do not your remarks seem to favour the general
impression that complexity of braia necessarily
implies loss of vital force ? Does it even mean
dispersion of force ? Is it not simply that the complex
mind has the power of applying its totality of vital
force at a given time in any one of a much greater
variety of directions than was open to the simple
undeveloped mind ? If force is to be measured by its
results, is not this measurement all in favour of the
complex mind, both individually and racially?
Another point. The theatre audience and the music-
hall audience. Doesn’t the music-hall audience dis-
pense so readily with scenery simply because the per-
formances are mainly single songs that don’t want
scenery? Wouldn’t the self-same audience demand
scenery if they went, as they do, to a theatre to hear a
play ?
By the way. Isn’t it Louis Stevenson who some-
where contends that it is because children have less
imagination than grown-up people that they require
tangible dolls, tangible broomsticks (for horses), actual
sofas and chairs (for railway trains)—all this to assist
their feeble imaginations—whereas the grown-up imagi-
nation is so self-sufficing that a written description of
the things gives all the actuality wanted for pleasure.
It was a re-statement of Tennyson’s position
in literature that drew forth Stephens's warmest pro-
test. From his post of literary dilettante and genial
critic he was roused instantly to become champion and
advocate. He defended Tennyson as he would have
defended a personal friend.
I have just been reading your article on Tennyson..
All the same, I read it with considerable pain. To my
mind, when you have reduced the subject of your
discourse to a prig and a word-artificer, Tennyson
is still a long way from being accounted for. For my
own part, I care very little about whether Tennyson
was a prig or not, nor does it seem to me to matter
much what were his relations with the Queen. That
Tennyson was endowed beyond all except a select
few of the world’s poets with “the wisdom and the
faculty divine,” that he saw clearly, saw steadily, and
kept sacredly in sight “ the light that never was on sea
or land,” and that his immense power as an
expresser was essentially the outcome of vivid
and wide vision and clear and strong feeling, I believe
as thoroughly as I believe that I can read English.
What do I care about his social and political opinions I
I believe that the most rabid Tory may be as
richly endowed with the vision and the faculty afore-
said as the most advanced social democrat. With me
the consideration of the difference between the aristo-
cratic and democratic mind disappears altogether
before the consideration of Nature, inner and outer,
and the revelation thereof in human expression. That
is the plane on which I meet my Tennyson, and he has
to lift me up a long way (but with his strong arms he
does it with ease) before I can sit and breathe with him
there.
What of his effect on—not merely the average reader
who makes popularity—but on the most gifted minds
of his generation, and, as far as can be judged by the
present literary outcome, on the most gifted minds of
the present generation also ? Those who disagree with
his social opinions may try as they like, but they can
never get rid of Tennyson as a great force in the educa-
tion which means the evolution of the modern mind.
He is incorporated into the language and therefore into
the thought of the race. At every turn of the corner
in their reading his disparagers will meet him even
though they do not recognise him, and even the cham-
pions of advanced social views will champion them
all the better because Tennyson has been mixed with
their nourishment. The man who has not made him-
self familiar with Tennyson is at a great disadvantage
compared with the man who has. I don’t say “ com-
pared with the man by whom Tennyson is unappreciated
or wrongly appreciated,” because that man, if he knows
his Tennyson as y o u do, is as much beholden to him
as a large contributor to his intellectual and spiritual
fortunes as if he had not misconceived his benefactor.
You favourably contrast Browning with Tennyson as
regards—shall I say their theological outlook. ‘ I have
an immense admiration for Browning, hut I do not
accept either of them as an authority on theological
doctrine. But that does not interfere with my rever-
ential attitude towards both of them. I may be duly
grateful to a wealthy benefactor who showers sovereigns
on me, and yet not care a doit about his special theory
as to the matrix of gold. That is how I feel towards
Browning and Tennyson as theologians. As pqets, I
must say I have a higher appreciation of Tennyson. ’ I
prefer even a small kosmos (not that Tennyson is small)
to a huge chaos (not that Browning is all chaos, though
about half of him is slag). There is more revelation of
power and beauty in a single planetary system than in
a universe of chaos. Browning, I think, had a greater
wealth of ideas and a more impulsive mental activity
than Tennyson, but Tennyson’s deeper sense of the
sacredness of expression makes all the difference
between them as consecrated hards. But by expression
I mean something very different from mere word-
artifice. I believe with Masson that Shakespeare’s true
place in the history of the human mind is that of the
“greatest expresser” of human thought and experi-
ence, but that is something infinitely greater than
being the greatest of word-artificers.
I have not proved my point, which is that Tennyson
is great—a great poet—one of the very few great poets.
But you will observe also that I have not au mpted to
prove it. This is not a vindication of Tennvson. To
me the very idea of Tennyson requiring vindication is
extraordinary.
I am giving you my opinions freely, though opposed
to yours, because I am quite sure that, like myself, you
do not consider identity of views essential to friendship
or to mutual appreciation ...
I must now conclude these few lines hoping you are
well, and that even unto you the heavens may yet be
opened that you may see Tennyson sitting at the right
hand of Sh Mil I mean, Virgil.
WHAT RELIGION HAS HONE
My blood gets hot when I think what religion
has done to men. I cannot think of it without grief and
horror. I could not tell the long tragedy of shame, and
blood, and cowardice, and torture. I have not the
eloquence, nor the memory, nor the knowledge, to do
justice to those black annals of ignorance and crime. I
do not think I can hold my pity and anger within the
scope of measured language if I try to tell one tithe of
what I know—although my knowledge is so limited.
How many hundreds of piillions of innocent children’s
lives have been blighted with terror by religion ? How
many sweet natures have been soured, and how many
bright spirits have been broken, by the dread of hell ?
How many cruel wars have been waged upon religious
grounds, wars in which millions and millions of" brave
men have been slain, in which millions and millions of
women and children have been outraged and mur-
dered in the name of gods who never existed ? If
you want pictures of these savage butcheries and
devilish tortures, read your Bible ! Read the Psalms
and the Chronicles, and the prophets. If you need
historic proofs of what religion has been to thousands
of millions of human beings throughout life’s troubled
story, read the histories of the Christian and Mohamme-
dan Churches. Read of the Spanish Inquisition ; read of
the Bartholomew massacres ; read of the witchcraft
trials ; read of the martyred Jews, Christians, Pagans
heathen, Moslems ; read of the persecution of the men
of science and of reason in all ages by ignorant and black-
hearted priests. If you want internal evidence of the
gloom, the cowardice, the folly which have disfigured
nearly all the religions of the world, read the miserable
gibbenrigs and cursings of the mental malformations
which, before science let in daylight on a world of
savagery and despair, passed current as works of theologv
“ Think for ourselves ! ” By thunder, it is only withi'n
the past century or so that we could think for ourselves
what religion meant to us or to others, without danger
of being murdered or tortured in the name of Christ "or
Mahomet. But now we can think, if we can lay aside our
theological crutches and let our minds step boldly for-
ward, now we can think what religion has meant and
what it does mean. Yes, I admit that religion even in
the black past, has meant golden dreams and soft delu-
sions to many poor creatures who, in a world of Christ’s
followers, had no friend and no comfort to turn to on this
side of the grave. I do not claim that murder, hatred
rapine, and terror of hell and the Pope comprise the
whole truth about religion. Amongst much crime it has
done some good. But the balance is against it a million-
fold.—Robert Blatchford, in The Clarion.
BOOKFELLOW'S MIXTURE

invents wof to describe Work Of Fiction ; wofsmith, the
maker of wofs . . . . “ Abandon hope all ye who en t e r
(incorrectly) here,” is quoted by Pulsford as what shoufd
he written over the Federal Custom-House . . . Methuen

J.H. : Can’t see it. Certainly, we ’re all for honesty and
purity in politics ; but even an honest politician has no
business to bore you with paraded virtue. Ashton
is only a small man, but his ostensible Conscience would
be several sizes too big for George Washington. “Me
and my Conscience” end by Incoming tedious. They
irritate us in the way that Puritanism irritated England,
in the way that Aristides irritated the citizen who cast
his oyster-shell for banishment because he was sick of the

No sense in quoting the science of fifty years ago against

“So Henry Lawson is dead.” E. C. Buley,
who claims to be “a young Australian,” affirms it in
Lond. To-Day of March 25, and adds a lengthy obituary
statement of qualified regret. “ Had he lived, he might
some day have realised the glowing promise of his first
book. But he is dead ” —E, C. Buley has killed him !
Gum-Tree’s whitmaniac chant after the dis-
closures of Baker and Smith, of N.S.W. Technological
Museum:—
One must pardon something to the epigrammatist ; and
that is not quite fair. Norton’s paper is a useful vehicle
for a great deal of matter which the “ respectable ” press
will not carry ; and while so many people believe that
her well is a good place to drown Truth in, one must not
complain if when she is lugged out she drips and looks
muddy.
“The factor in the situation which outweighs
all others is that, while South Africa is emphatically a
white man’s country, practically all manual labour is
done by the natives. This situation is a direct conse-
quence of well-known historic and ethnological causes,
but it is pregnant with grave evils for the moral and the
material prosperity of the society where it exists. If
British South Africa is ever to develop her natural re-
sources to the full, and to take her proper rank among
the great self-governing communities of the empire, she
must draw to her shores a large white population before
the economic framework of her institutions permanently
hardens upon the wrong lines. This end can be achieved
only by a gradual, but complete, change in the character
of the supply of labour. If the land is to become in the
true sense a white man’s country, it must become a
country of white labour, and it can only become a country
of white labour by establishing and maintaining a standard
of wages and living high enough to induce the labourer of
European blood to make it his permanent home. To the
attainment of this object the existence of a native popu-
lation, outnumbering the present white population of a
million by five or six to one, constitutes a serious
obstacle.”
And whence do you think we take this excerpt?
From Lond. Times—the paper which encouraged its
representative, Floral Shaw, to advocate the creation of a
servile community in Australia!
Amy Bemardy, in an Amer. magazine, denys
that Marion Crawford’s Italian novels truthfully repre-
sent Italian life. “It certainly is unquestionably true
that Air. Crawford’s Italy is entirely unlike the real
thing ; that while some particulars and descriptions are
good, the entire organisation of his novels is hopelessly
melodramatic, and falls hopelessly short of its ambitious
aim; that no Italian princess would ever think of
doing, and much less do, what the princess does in
Taquisara ; that never in Italy will people get married
as they do in A Roman Singer, at a moment’s
notice ; that no Roman princes will be choked to dej-th,
or princesses poisoned, brothers murdered, or deeds
duplicated and signatures forged quite so easily and
naturally as such things seem to happen in the novels of
Mr. Crawford.” And so on. Miss Bernardy, herself
Anglo-Italian, relates how an Italian editor refused her
review of Crawford’s works because it blamed his blun-
ders too weakly. “He is artificial, false, passe,” said the
indignant editor, “ even to the names of his characters—
‘Corona Saracinesca’ and ‘ Duca d’Astrardente,’ for
instance. The Italian language has never tolerated such
screeching sounds.” “ Ouida,” alas, falls similarly under
the Italian ban. “ Why this good lady will keep' bewail-
ing evils that never have existed save in her imagination,
and why she will persist in making herself miserable
upon her own conception of what Italy is and what she
should be, is the one thing that Italians cannot be made
to understand.” As for Hall Caine ! —but enough . . .

The A ims and Objects of a Literature Society, by
H. G. Turner (Echo Publishing: Co., Melb.) is the third of
Melb. “Australian Literature Society’s” interesting
pamphlets. H. G. Turner, as most people know, has
waved a useful flag in front of the local literary battle;
but he was born too early to assimilate modern notions of
literature, and his enthusiasm is more admirable than his
judgment. Still: a pleasant little paper, with a plethora
of loose expressions. One sentence flashes out as a
motto for the schism-rent “Australian Literature
Society,” if it want a motto—
We... like to get together and talk, especially about
things we are not very familiar with.
The more you study that sentence, in regard to H.G.
Turner, and in regard to “The Aust. Lit. Society,” and in
regard to Australia, the more you will find in it. in
fact, it has not been made prominent enough, try
again :
Even now... but that must serve.
And...“ conscience ” (the instinct of order, the
instinct of justice) rises and asks, Is that fair, is that
just? What is there on the other side? Well, there are
Turner and the A. L. S. on the other side, with parapher-
nalia and propaganda and a set of polished ideals, lheir
whoop for Brallaghan will balance any outcry on uena
O’Callaghan. By all means let them whoop. There isn t
much in literary societies of dilettanti: they plav pretti)
and create nothing—not even standards, toe At ■
society is better than its class : it gives us pampme ,
and the pamphlets are readable. And as a focus for scat-
tered aims, an advertisement for current hteratu .
a spur to the purchasing public, it has a eia
upon affection. The working tribe of Australian writers
wants as much encouragement and advertisem _
as—as it deserves. Even H. G. Turner does not a .
praise all the wrong people. Sometimes you can
him doubting if Art really did stop short in the
rated court of the Empress Josephine, or thereabout
True, he tells us that, in the first Literary Society n«
connected with—
We decided that Longfellow was a greater poet
Tennyson ... most unforgivable of all, we n
Martin Farquhar Tupper on to a pedestal as one or
first-class poets of the Victorian era.
And... “on revient toujours k ses premiers amours.

NEWLY PUBLISHED
Dewdrop Danby, by Harold Hansell (2s. 6d.) is
a small book produced in attractive form by the’Ex-
change Press, Melb. (The machine impression is not
good, however.) The contents are thirteen short sketches
which the publisher’s commendatory circular “guaran-
tees ” to be “quaint, humourous, and interesting, and
totally different to anything else ever written or’pub-
lished in Australia.” Consequently we regret to find
them mild littie, neat little yarns of the average weekly
quality, undistinguished and impotent.
May number of N.S. W. Agricultural Gazette
contains Govt, inspector A. A. Dunnicliffe’s illustrated
report of N. A. Gatenby’s sheep-feeding experiment at
Jt-malong. In defiance of the proof of China, India, and
America, and in the teeth of the drought losses, there
are still Australian blockheads who deride the profit of
irrigation. To bring these to greater shame, N. A.
Gatenby, of Jemalongstation,near Forbes,N.S.W.,proved
that on 22k acres of lucerne, irrigated from the Lachlan,
he could keep alive 75 sheep to J,he acre, and improve
their condition. The experiment was watched during
several months by the Govt, inspector, who says that
I am quite satisfied from my experience, confirmed
at Jemalong, that from one acre of good lucerne fifty
sheep per annum may be primely fattened for Home-
bush market; and in saying that I am keeping well
within the mark.
Sellbridge and Go., an Australian story, by
Joyce Vincent (J. C. MacCartie ; 3s. 6d.), is an example
of a class of Action that will presently find a profitable
sale in Australia, as population increases. It is a read-
able tale of life in a Victorian town, not brilliant, not
dull, with many truthful pictures of character done in
“Ada Cambridge’s” bright, homely manner, and with
unexpected little turns of plot and comment that are
purely Australian and pleasantly fillip interest. If this
be a first. book, it bolds distinctive promise ; and we hope
that, even in the face of insufficient remuneration, the
author will continue to write. Her (?) work is much more
agreeable to read than the average foreign novel.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Spuds Went Up.
I am getting near to fifty,
And I’ve seen a varied life ;
I’ve had times of fun and frolic.
And I’ve had a share of strife.
I have known some sudden changes
From the depths of hopeless woe
To the sunny heights of pleasure
With Good Fortune’s tide at flow—
But in all my life’s transitions,
Drinking Woe’s or Pleasure’s cup,
Ne’er have known such pure elation
As I felt when spuds went up.
It was back in days of boyhood,
When we all worked on the farm,
When we shared each other’s pleasure
And we felt each other’s harm.
Through our district crops had suffered
From disease and blight and drought,
And for most of us all prospt cts
And all hope had given out.
Crop succeeding crop had failed us—
Next, we thought, ’twas Ruin’s turn—
And the spuds that year we planted
In a hopeless unconcern.
-A 11 the neighbors planted early,
And we planted early, too,
For we reckoned that the March rains
Might come down and pull us through.
But our hopes were disappointed,
All the month it didn’t rain;
So, for other crops, our neighbours
Went and ploughed theirs out again.
As for us, we’d staked our fortunes—
We no longer hoped or feared—
We had grimly vowed to leave it
If those ’taturs ne’er appeared !
We’d begun to plan our movements
In the future, when the rain
Came in time to save our ’taturs,
And it cheered us up again !
Then it poured like all Gehenna,
And the frost was late that year—
And the crop was out of danger
Ere the first of it came near.
And when we had started digging, •
Oh ! it filled full Pleasure’s cup
When the Old Man came from market
With the news that “ Spuds was up ! "
If you’ve dug ’em in mid-winter,
When the frost is on the ground,
And each blessed spud you pick up
Is with inud encrusted round ;
In the morn with fingers aching,
While you ’re sweating at the brow,
You will know it isn’t pleasant,
But we didn’t make a row ;
For we’d something then to work for,
And scarce stopped for bite and sup
While the daylight lingered with us—
We had spuds, and spuds were up !
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.
Benching.
I am beueher at a sawmill,-cuttin’ firewood blocks
for town,
And the wages that I’m getting aren’t grand ;
But a fellow can’t go picking when his loot is
“ melted down ” ;
He’s got to collar anything at hand.
The bench is rather crazy, slung together anyway,
And it wobbles when the engine’s going strong ;
And the belt flies off the pulley, and I’m worried
all the day,
For one thing or another'3 mostly wrong.
And the lumper slings the billets with a wallop
on the bench—
Though I curse him—and it lurches and it rocks !
There—he’s jammed my blessed fingers ; Oh ! his
bloomin’ neck I’ll wrench
When I’m finished with this biinded tiuck of
blocks.
“ The engine is a new one,” says the boss, “or
nearly so ; ”
But these second-hand affairs are seldom good,
And I’ve had to overhaul it, and I drive it; so, I
know
That in places it is neatly packed with wood.
Bub I’ve got to keep it going—open valve, at
fifty-five,
And the trucker has to watch and keep it stokec ;
And if we’re short of water ere the next lot can
arrive,
We chance it—let her rip and keep her poked.
The plugs won’t always hold her; they will get
afire some day,
And then—the thought my blessed system shocks !
But I seldom dwell upon it, and I let her pound
away,
While the circular is tearing through the blocks.
Then the splitters come a-growling ’bout the ends
I throw aside,
And curse because I say : “ Well, chop 'em
square.”
But the boss says: “Never heed ’em, keep the
margin pretty wide;
Y’ want the ends for stoking : let ’em swear.”
So with driving and with swearing, and the belt
a-slipping too,
And the water-carter loafing at the creek,
And with benching and with oiling I’ve enough
and more to do
(The salary ain’t very much per week).
I’ve got to grin and do it—run the whole con-
founded show—
At a rate that sweating records simply knocks ;
And the pace ! why, by comparison a lightning
streak is slow—
When I’m keeping orders filled for firewood
blocks.
VIC.
BEN SUN.

Acute Dyspepsia.
“Clements Tonic Restored
all my Strength and Vigor.”
The Case of N|rs WATKINS.
(by our reporter.)
There is a period in every person’s life,
particularly when in sorrow, that the sym-
pathy of a friend in need is greatly appre-
ciated, and although it is difficult to con-
jecture how many hearts may be gladdened
by the news appearing below, it is safe to
remark that Mrs Rachael Watkins, of
Grosvenor-street, Woollahra, was actuated
by the best of motives in saying :
“My health was in such an unsatisfae-
tory state some time ago that I could nob
enjoy life. Every day I got up with a
splitting headache which made me feel sad
and wretched from morning till night, and,
owing to the taste I had, I felt so sick that
I could not touch any breakfast. My tongue
was dry and my throat was so parched that
I was constantly drinking water, yet the
thirst from which I suffered still remained
Sometim°s midday went by and still I did
not feel hungry, Out as sure as I did take a
little food I paid dea ! ly for it afterwards. ’’
“ In what way, Mrs. Watkins ? ” enquired
the pressman.
“I used to feel ill all over me, especially
if there happened to be a little salt in my
food, which had the effect of making my
mouth and throat so much inflamed that it
was painful for me to swallow anything;
but the stomach seemed to be the worst
part, as it was distended with wind
which came from the food which had not
digested, and the cramping pains inside
were simply horrible. Windy spasms often
caused me to get up in the middle of the
night and walk the floor in agony, for it
was more than I could do to lie there quietly
and suffer such frightful torture. What-
ever I had eaten always repeated, but it
would not come up, much as I would have
liked it to, as then I would have felt a
little easier.”
“ How long had you suffered in this
way ? ”
“ I was very ill for twelve months.
Everything went wrong, even my ehest
being painful with a heavy sensation which
almost stifled me. Many a time I tried to
vomit, thinking that if I could only do so I
would be much relieved, but the lump in
my chest seemed to be a fixture, and fre-
quently made me feel as if I were taking
my last breath, the trouble in breathing
was so great. I became so terrified that I
might suffocate whilst in bed that I used to
prop myself up with pillows, and, notwith-
standing the fact that I always felt weak
and worn out, I could not sleep when I did
retire, being very restless till about two or
three o’clock in the morning. When I
ought to have been asleep was the only
part of the twenty-four hours that I seemed
to liven up a bit, but it was very unnatural
for me to be so fidgety, and my system was
drained of all its vital force on that account.
If I did fall asleep I began dreaming, and
when I woke up I tried to recollect what I
hid been dreaming about, but my memory
was so defective that I could not recall any-
thing to mind. Scarcely an hour passed
without some annoyance being caused by
sharp pains between my shoulders, and es-
pecially under my left shoulder-blade, and
my legs ached so much that after standing a
little while the aches developed into dis
tinct pains. Across my loins the aches
were really terrible. I use 1 to think they
came through sitting down so mu h, but I
found when I walked the pains were still
more severe The least effort on my part
resulted in giddiness coming on, so I had to
be very careful how I went about, particu-
larly as my eyes were often so affected that
I could scarcely see out of them. If I
stooped to pick up anything from the floor
I felt half-dazed for a while, my head reel-
ing awfully, and small black specks dancing
all round. Not only did I lose all my
strength, hut my nervous system was quite
unstrung.”
“I believe you consulted a doctor, Mrs.
Watkins. W hat did he say your complaint
was ? ”
“My doctor said I was suffering from
acute indigestion and he treated me accord-
ingly, but to my intense dismay I could
see I had no hope of recovering under his
treatment, as the medicines he prescribed
did not have any good effect. In my sor-
rowful condition I lost heart completely. I
did not know what to do, especially as I had
got so much more feeble that on many oc-
casions I had to lie do wn in a state of ex-
haustion the whole of the day. It looked
to me as though my remaining days were
to be spent in misery, from which there was
no escape ; but I thought I ought to try
everything at my disposal, so I started doc-
toring myself with adverti ed medicines,
and when I had taken enough of them to
base an opinion upon, I was forced to con-
clude that my afflictions were incurable.
As those me iicines were also useless I
thought, in my desperation, that everything
else was the same, but a lady friend spoke
so favourably about Clements Tonic that I
could not help buying some.”
“ Did you find it acting as your friend
said it would ? ”
“Yes, thank God ! her advice was not
misleading, for Clements Tonic had precisely
the effect which I was told it would have,
although my condition was so low that it
took a little time to make headway. Once
Clements Tonic got my maladies on the
move, however, it shifted them with great
despatch. I started by picking up my ap-
petite and getting an occasional sound
night’s rest; then, when my head was free
from pain, I slept tranquilly right through
every night, and the aches left my limbs by
degrees. I considered this a great amount
of success for Clements Tonic to achieve,
taking into account the repeated failures I
had met with, so I got more of it as it was
required, and I shall never regret that I
stuck steadfastly to the same remedy, to
which there was nothing to compare for so
restoring my internal organs to a healthy
state that my food digested properly. My
vision became clear, the distention of and
pains in my stomach did not occur again,
and the stifling sensations left my chest.
To put it briefly, I was relieved of every
pain which had made my life a martyrdom,
besides having my nervousness quite
abolished, and you may d pend I was de-
voutly thankful to Clements Tonic for bring-
ing b.ick all my strength and vigor. In
every re-pect I was like a new woman, for
I had suffered from rheumatism close on
five years, and Clements Tonic was the only
medicine which gave me relief. My feet
and nearly all the joints in my body had
been assailed by severe rheumatic pains, but
Clements Tonic found its way to those parts
an I gradually diminished my tortures. In
all cases of sickness I recommend to my
friends the use of Clements Tonic, and you
may publish these remarks in any way you
desire. ”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I. Rachael Watkins, of Grosvenor-street,
Woollahra, Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that 1
have carefully read the annexed document,
consisting of four folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to four, and that it contains and is a true
and faithful account of my iliness and cure by
Clements Tonic ; and also contains my full permis-
sion to publish in any way my statements— which
I give voluntarily, without receiving any pay-
ment ; and 1 make this solemn declaration con-
scientiouslv believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Waverley, this twelfth
day of April, one thousand nine hundred and two,
before me,
J. L DALE, J.P.

SATURDAY, MAY 16, 1903.
The Tight for Union.
Benttsm has had its natural, its inevitable
result in Victoria. The State which made
dishonesty in its relations with its employees
a principle, and brutal bluster a settled method,
is punished now by the paralysis of its railway
service. The loss is the community's, and the
blame is largely the community’s, for it was the
community that deliberately placed Irvine and
Bent in power, and though the community gave
to those men no warrant whatever to pursue the
policy they have done in railway matters, and
neither Bent nor Irvine dared even to hint at
that policy when asking the public for its
suffrages, still a State which puts a person
like Bent in a position of trust and responsibility
must be held responsible for the consequences.
That there will be many innocent sufferers is a
fact for which regret must be felt, though
Bent and Irvine, if their actions count for
anything, feel none. That regret may sug-
gest to some that it is a pity the issue
should have been put to the stern decision of
industrial war, that a little more patience under
insult and ignominy was to be expected from the
railway men, in the hope that time would bring
redress of their wrongs. But such a thought will
not exist long in the mind of any man possessing
the instinct of freedom. There are occasions
which demand that all thoughts of expediency, all
calculations of gain and loss, shall be cast aside,
and a stand made at any price for principle.
When what is sacred in human thought—and
there is nothing more sacred than the
instinct of liberty—is attacked, then revolt
becomes a duty. {And I thought that your
decrees were not so strong as to overstep the
established, unwritten laws of Heaven ” was the
unanswerable argument of Antigone, charged
with breaking a human edict because it conflicted
with the higher authority of fraternal duty. With
a people or a class oppressed, called upon to
surrender liberty, which is the essential of self-
respecting life, all arguments as to the conse-
quences of revolt—the sufferings entailed, the
harm to the guiltless, the loss to the weak and the
helpless—shrivel away. A people must fight if
threatened by an invader, even though children
will starve, and wives perish, and innocent blood
flow. A class must revolt if threatened with
deprivation of its liberty, however great the loss
and the suffering. Revolt brings huge evils in its
train. But submission brings worse. When the
point is reached at which tolerance is cowardice
and patience servility, protest is the duty of free
men.
• • * •
That point was reached in Victoria before the
railway employees took the desperate step of
going on strike, thus cutting one of the great
arteries of the State. If it had not been, a strike
would have been a wicked and malignant act,
fairly comparable for atrociousness with the pre-
ceding actions of the Irvine Government, But
it is not possible, after a just examination
of the facts, to avoid the conclusion that the
Bent - Irvine Government was resolved on
forcing a strike, and that the men gave in to
the Government’s wish for a strike only when to
refuse longer to do so was to sacrifice all self-
respect. The men, it should be noted, struck on
no sordid or paltry grievance. When the Govern-
ment reduced their wage-fund to the extent or
stopping all the legally-due increments, they sub-
mitted. When practically robbed of their fran-
chise the men submitted, allowing the specious
fictions advanced in support of that outrage on
manhood suffrage to pass. When the Bent-
levxne Government contrived the mean swindle
of depriving the railway workers of one day’s pay
per week, whilst exacting from them practically
the same amount of work —in some cases a larger
amount —they submitted. It was only when their
tyrannical masters proceeded to outrage, wantonly
and purposelessly, their natural liberties and their
manhood, that the men at last took action. The
demand that they should formally separate them-
selves from the fraternity of Labor, to which they
had been long linked, was from every point of
view, a causeless degradation. It saved no money,
it assisted no scheme of discipline. Its object was
humiliation, and humiliation only. The demand
was the invention of a petty, malignant, surly
tyrant and persecutor. The men were asked to
trample upon what they considered dear, and spit
at what they held in respect. _ To demand of a
free man that he shall publicly repudiate a
religious belief which he has cherished is
never made except by a mean and paltry
tyrant, for such a repudiation can have no
real effect on his views, and can only be de-
manded as a means of degrading and mortifying
him. Similarly, the demand that the railway
workers in Victoria should withdraw the affili-
ation of their unions with the Trades Hall could
have been made with no practical or seemly pur-
pose. Such affiliation was but the external symbol
of a belief in the fraternity of Labor; to cast it
off would not have affected the belief. If that
fraternal feeling exposed the railways to the
danger of their employees being drawn into a
strike without having a grievance of their own and
merely out of sympathy with some other union, it
was a matter of absolutely no importance whether
the men affiliated with the Trades Hall or
not. The affiliation could not force or induce
them to go out on strike without their
full consent ; without the affiliation they
could, if they chose, enter just as easily
upon a sympathetic strike. The railway men
saw clearly that the Bent-Irvine purpose was to
outrage their sentiments, to pillory them as poor-
spirited slavish creatures, and to publicly trample
upon their liberties; and they objected—as Aus-
tralian miners objected at Eureka, as Australian
citizens objected at Circular Quay to the further
transportation of convicts. History, when all
the lies of the daily press have faded away, will
recognise the justice of the objection, and will
enthrone it with other historic revolts as a stand
in defence of that passion for liberty, which is
the dearest of human possessions.
• • * *
It is vitally necessary to keep this aspect of
the question before the mind when considering
the validity of the strike. Had the issue been
merely one of expediency, or of wages, or hours,
or anything else on which compromise or sur-
render, or evasion was possible, there were other
ways out of the situation. The men might, if
the issue had been any other than one of sacred
principle, have feigned to withdraw from the
Trades Hall, whilst preserving a secret and
conspirant connection, just as the weakling in
religion might, during a time of persecution,
abjure in public the faith which he confessed
to in private. But any such paltry subterfuge
would have been unworthy of the occasion. A
defiant deed was demanded. There are certain
things which a man must fight for if he.be
a man, and chief of those things, to the working
classes, is trades-unionism. To trades-unionism the
worker owes his political and economic liberty.
It has raised him from serfdom and
restored to him his wife and children from the
coal-pit and the mill. It has given to him self-
respect, and brought the idea of mutual brother-
hood to lighten his toil. It is a religion, noble in
sentiment and effective in practice. And separa-
tion from the Trades Hall, for no sane motive, but
merely at the bullying behest of the brutal Bent,
would have been an abjuration of trades-unionism
by the Victorian railway men under the lash of a
persecutor.
* m * * *
It has been represented that the affiliation
was so recent that it was only undertaken as a
protection against Bentism. Even so it would
not affect the issue. But it is not so—
the railway men were affiliated 16 years ago.
It has been represented that affiliation puts
the railway unions at the behest of the Trades
Hall to be called out on strike, willy-nilly, at any
time. That is a lie. The Trades Hall could not
call the railway, or any other, unions out without
their full consent, and during the great maritime
strike, when organised Labor was in a ferment all
over the continent, and Gillies, Bruce Smith
and M'Millan were using the railways to convey
armed soldiers and Gatling guns to overawe and
coerce striking workers, there was no call-out of
the railway employees. When the noisome pur-
pose of the Victorian Government to force on
a railway strike was made indecently
apparent by the brutal bullyragging of
the unspeakable Bent, and Irvine came for-
ward to mask the design under a cover of
polite words, he refused to discuss with the rail-
way workers these points. A “conference” sug-
gestion which he made was purely farcical. The
men were to absolutely yield the one point at issue
before conferring; he would listen to no state-
ments in disproof of the lying assertions that the
Trades Hall Council was a “political” body,
and that association with it involved the
railway men in the liability to be called out
at any time. The Victorian railway strike
is not therefore a protest against wage-
reductions and wage-robberies. It hinges not
on a question of industrialism but on a ques-
tion of liberty. It represents the only possible
protest that free men could make against a need-
less humiliation, a wanton interference with their
freedom, an unreasoning outrage on what the
human mind holds most dear. With full considera-
tion, therefore, of the losses, public and private,
which will flow from it, and of the possibility of
failure, it is impossible to regret that it has taken
place, for if it had not, the railway men would have
shown that they had no manly dignity, and that
the sentiment of liberty had gone out of Victoria.
• • »
The Victorian railway strike was necessary,
because of the Government’s policy of Bentism.
But it is not necessary that it, or its cause, should
be allowed to exist a single day longer than would
suffice for the summoning of the Federal Parlia-
ment. The paralysis of the Victorian railway sys-
tem is not merely a State but a Federal matter ;
one part of the Commonwealth cannot be bled
white without injury to all other parts. Bentism,
too, is not merely a State but a Federal matter. If
a Bent or an Irvine can stir up strife in one State
to the point of stopping a whole railway system,
by attempting to inflict on a body of workers a
degradation which, in N.S.W. under the Arbitra-
tion Act, is made a criminal offence, and can thus
stop the Federal mails, interfere seriously with
inter-State trade, inflict grave loss on the whole
of Australia, and bring the shadow of disaster over
the whole of the continent, Federation is a failure.
The supreme power in Australia cannot ignore
the present situation. Last session an Industna
Arbitration AcS'was contemplated by the Federal
Parliament; only the squalid greed of the im-
porters’ party, which stretched the tight tor
shoddy over many months, blocked the measure.
Had it been passed, there is no doubt but that
it would follow the lines of the N.S.W. Act
and in that case it would have treated as a
criminal offence the demand of Irvine and Lent
that the Victorian railway men should abandon
their rights as unionists. Now that a crisis un-
precedented in Australian history has come, the
Australian Parliament should at once meet
and establish a court of arbitration. -foe
outrage which—there is good cause to believe
—the Bknt-Irvine Cabinet contemplated, ot
asking the Victorian Parliament to pass a retro-
spective Act making the action of the railway men
illegal need not be imitated. It wou.d be
beneath the dignity of an Australian Parliament
to stretch back its arm to seize and punish the
miserable Bent-Irvine Government for its wanton
outrage; but a tribunal for the immediate
settlement of the difficulty should be established,
and at the same time precaution taken
that such conditions as caused the strike shall
never arise again.
*** * .
The actual momentary crisis of the strike could
probably be settled in a day by Premier Barton.
If he were to offer to-day to establish a fair
judicial tribunal, by Executive authority, and
insist on both parties submitting to its arbitra-
tion, threatening otherwise to force them to do
so by an Act of Parliament, that would be almost
certainly efficacious.
• * • *
The Federal Government is now faced by
its first great difficulty. Hitherto it has shown
no lack of courage in carrying out its man-
date from the people to substitute _ -Aus-
tralian authority for inter-State jealousy in all
national matters; let no hesitation be shown
now. The occasion is momentous ; a State is
threatened with ruin, and Federal authority is
seriously challenged by the action of a State
Government in taking a step, illegal and criminal
north and west of its borders, and which the
Federal Parliament had announced its intention
of making illegal and criminal within the confines
of the Commonwealth. Whatever its motive
purposeless, mad malevolence, or a plot to
represent State ownership of the railways as
impossible—the Bent-Irvine Government has
deliberately chosen a time to force on a strike
when the Federal power was, in a sense, slumber-
ing—Parliament out of session and the Industrial
Arbitration Act not on the statute book. The
Commonwealth cannot tamely submit to that out-
rage on its authority and still maintain its dignity.
At present its mails are held up and its. trade dis-
located. Should any disorder arise its troops
must be called out for the work of suppression.
Without firm and prompt action the Australian
Government will be in the position, perhaps, of
having to act the policeman to stop disorder con-
sequent on wrongful actions which it has
neglected its duty in permitting. Within a month
the Federal High Court and a Federal Arbitration
Court should be in existence; within a week
the Victorian railway men should be back at their
posts by order of the Federal authority, pending a
just settlement of all matters at issue between
them and Bent. A great opportunity of estab-
lishing the dignity, the authority, the power of
the Commonwealth is at Premier Barton s
hand. Seizing it, he will stamp Australian
unity with the mint-mark of living power;
neglecting it, he must lower the position of the
Commonwealth, showing its legislature, not as
a supreme Parliament strong to preserve
Australia from the consequences of the wicked-
ness and folly of any set of men, however
influential —but as a mere separate Parliament,
which any Bent may flout. The Victorian
railway strike had to be entered upon, for the
principle of human liberty was at stake. It
must now be ended, for the principle of Fed-
eralism is at stake.
The Poisoned News Supply.
To civilised man a newspaper is as much a neces-
sity as bread. His mind craves for a knowledge
of passing events as his body craves for food. It
is, therefore, of as high importance to the com-
munity that the supply of news should be free
from adulteration and infection as the supply of
bread and of meat. But, while steps to protect
the public from poisoned meat and unwholesome
bread are feasible, and are put into effect with
great benefit, protection against false news,
against poisoned mind-food, seems to be not
yet thought of. The newspaper may circu-
late vicious lies, may partly falsify or
wholly suppress the truth and thereby cause
incalculable damage to the community, but at
present there is no remedy and no punishment.
The very curious conditions under which news-
paper enterprise has grown up are largely respon-
sible for the existing evil. Originally newspapers
were not the vital necessity that they are now,
and the earliest journals were more devoted to
discussion of problems of politics and ethics, to
literary and philosophical -disquisitions, than to
the actual supply of news. When a daily news
service became a necessity of civilisation, the old
journals, with their traditions of pamphlet-
eeering, began to supply the need, and thus
there grew up, what is really an anomalous
institution —a journal which supplies news, which
is a necessity, and a certain amount of polemical
matter, which is a survival of the days when the
press was solely an institution for debating and
arguing. Of course this polemical matter, though
not necessary, is a highly desirable product—of
great advantage sometimes in educating public
opinion by giving wide dissemination to thought.
But it is unfortunate that the custom should have
grown up of combining in one newspaper what is
polemical and what is merely informing. A
better system, in the abstract, would be the
publication of newspapers which are merely state-
ments of facts and the separate publication, daily,
weekly or monthly of papers which discuss facts
and voice opinions. Then the news supply, which is
a necessity, could be kept pure and undefiled, and
the publication of opinions, which is a luxury,
would be still unfettered.
• * • *
The almost inevitable result, in a human and
fallible community, of the publication by the same
journal of news and arguments, is that the news
is tinged to a certain extent to suit the argu-
ments. At first that result did not show ; news-
paper proprietors professed (and practised as well
as they could) a policy of keeping the news
department free from bias—giving the facts
honestly in the news column, however much they
twisted them in the comment column. But
very soon a system of sympathising the news
with the comment crept in, and it would be
very difficult to-day to mention any one daily
pa per which gives the news with absolute honest,
and fullness, and with no sort of bias I n 4 y
tralia the process has been carried to a noint \
which unconscious bias is left behind and shlT
less, naked lying is freely adopted. All tl
great daily ” papers—combined as thev are e
Trust to limit the cost of production and to it?
tiuence Governments for concessions—whilst th
may differ in minor details, agree absolutely in
modelling their news columns to suit the busing
interests whose advertising constitutes their <S
support Newspaper lying and suppression
carried to the degree of a fine art It !
not one capital only that is affected-’ all th?
metropolitan centres have their news sudul
poisoned; and where all agree to deceive there l
no hope of correcting one by comparison with
another. It would be perhaps possible for an
intelligent man who read all the daily papers of
London, and compared their various accounts to
come to some fairly accurate knowledge of naasi™
events That is not possible in any AuS
capital. In regard to N.S.W. matters, the two
morning papers are practically one as regards
policy and news. They have the same cable
service for all news outside of Australia and thev
have a very thorough understanding-conscious
or unconscious—as to the tinging of local news to
please the importing and shop-keeping interests
One has more scruple, the other less shame • that
is merely a degree of difference, not a difference
And they supply the news of this State not only
to the State but to all Australasia; from their
offices and from their columns onlv does news
flow, and the polluted stream thus spreads from a
State to overflow a continent.
* • » »
Customs Minister Kingston, at his Sydney
meeting last week, took occasion to expose some
newspaper lies; he showed that one newspaper
had described his “cool reception ” at Newcastle
though he had had a welcome from 8000 voters ■
that it had stated that only two of the ships in
the harbor had been decorated in his honor
though 47 had been—and so on. Those were
somewhat more glaring instances than usual
of a policy and a purpose which is persis-
tently pursued. The present Australian Govern-
ment can get no fair play in the news
columns of the daily press, because it refused to
continue the subsidies given by the State Govern-
ment to the newspapers, and because, guarding
the interests of the community, it does not strain
nor wink at the law in the interests of the drapery
and other importing concerns. The cause of
Australian Nationalism can get no fair play in the
news columns of the daily papers because their
proprietors are suppliants for Imperial favors, or
the friends of such suppliants. The rights of
organised Labor can get no fair play in the daily
papers because those rights infringe on the class
privileges of shoddy capitalism, (in regard to the
Victorian railway crisis, the public mind has been
poisoned with the most malignant lies as regards
the actions and purposes and claims of the men.)
This poisoning—repeated daily—of the community
with false news has become a great public danger;
it threatens to destroy civic freedom and to en-
slave the people to a newspaper bureaucracy. As
mischievous really as a daily supply of tuberculous
milk or of infected bread, the poisonous stream of
misinformation calls for some similar measures of
suppression. The difficulties in the way of effec-
tive precaution are very great, but not insuper-
able. One day the common-sense of the com-
munity may be awakened to the extent of putting
in the dock—with the Customs swindler-the
newspaper proprietor who deliberately circulates
falsehoods.

In Praise of Minister Owe’Suilivan.
N.S.W.’s Owe’Sullivan made amends for a good
many follies by his courageous, manly and
straightforward declaration of sympathy for the
Victorian railway unionists. Of course the inci-
dent was seized upon by the Shoddy press as a
subject for denunciation, and the large, damp
paw of hypocrisy was held aloft in affected horror.
The Sydney Telegraph, in particular, had glar-
ing headlines about “O’Sullivan’s Astounding
Speech,’’ and moaned thus :
As the dethronement of the Ministry is regarded as an
extremely probable contingency when Parliament meets,
the idea is that Mr. 0 Sullivan aspires to lead Laborites
into Opposition when he leaves office, and thus hold the
Premiership in reversion. In the light of this explana-
tion, his attitude of hostility to the Government of Vic-
toria, and his carelessness of the responsibilities of a
Minister of the Crown, might be comprehensible, if cen-
surable ; but certainly no act of this much-discussed
Minister has been so roundly condemned by politicians
and citizens generally as this latest escapade.
All which labored and bogus explanation is quite
unnecessary, and is merely more lying and hypo-
crisy. When the political row in Victoria was
between Protection and Foreign-trade, “ the
responsibilities of a Minister of the Crown”
never prevented Reid and the two Smiths
(Bruce and Pumpkin) from denouncing the
Victorian Government and its policy with all
the fury they possessed. Now that the row
is between Unionism and Bent, O’Sullivan
has just the same right to speak his mind, even if
the Telegraph, which cordially approved when
the N.S.W. importing gang put its meddlesome
paw into Victorian politics, disapproves to-day.
And, curiously enough, a day or two after the
above tale appeared, the same Telegraph was
savagely denouncing Premier See for not commit-
ting himself on the Victorian strike subject—for
not committing himself on the side of Bent. As
for the “explanation ” offered to account for the
Owe’Sulltvan’s attitude, it might as reasonably
be explained that the Telegraph’s editor took up
his attitude on the Foreign-trade question in the
hope that it might lead to him getting a steady
job as a corporation pick-and-shovel man. The
leadership of the N.S.W. Labor party is not a
billet, as the party is at present constituted, which
opens the way to power; in fact, it practically
puts its holder out of sight of office. And Owe’-
Sullivan, the most restless builder of railways
and bridges in Australasia, isn t the man to
petrify in a job like that.

Spruce Myth’s Moral Triends.
When Sydney Morning Herald, the prim and
proper old lady of Hunter-street, does make a
slip it is always a regular “howler.” No daily
paper in Australia is more carefully “ read ’’ in
proof, no paper is more desirous of avoiding any
luridness in its columns; and yet few papers have
published so many hair raising items. This crept
into the Herald on Friday last about Mr. Spruce
Myth’s moral friends the Japanese (whose immi-
gration to Australia Mr. Myth would encourage
for the good of Australia) :
SENSATION AT A WOMEN’S MEETING.
Brisbane, Thursday.—A sensation was caused at Char-
ters Towers at a meeting of the Women’s Equal Franchise
League to-night. At the last meeting a paper was read
on certain Japanese houses in Charters Towers. This
was discussed to-night. Mrs. Ratting stated that she
had donned a man’s hat and macintosh, visited these
places at night, and talked through windows to Japanese
women, who could not speak English, and could only say
“Give me five bob.” This remark was received with
gasps of horror by 70 ladies present, and with laughter by
about 100 men who congregated on the verandah around
the windows of the Town Hall looking in. Mrs. Ratting
was proceeding with further experiences when the lady
president interposed.
Mrs. Katting —man’s attire —Japanese geishas—
“gimme fi’ bob” —gasps of horror from 70 ladies
—laughter from 100 men ; and then the Herald
suddenly remembered, and over “ further experi-
ences ” a veil is thrown. But what a pity it is that
whenever the Japs.—whose “virtues,” according
to Spruce Myth, are the sole reason why the
White Australia party wants to keep them out
turn up even in the Tory and pro-Jap. press of
Australia, it should either be because Mr. Chrys-
anthemum has murdered somebody, or because
Miss Chrysanthemum, the boss harlot of the
globe, has been holding out her brown hand more
humorously than usual for the wages of sin.

Kingston to the People of Sydney.
Australian Minister Kingston got to the minds
of the.citizens of Sydney last week as he is, not as
a prejudiced Freetrade press represents him to
be, and had a most enthusiastic reception and a
flattering endorsement of his policy. The night
was marked by a tempest of rain, which made it
a matter of some courage to venture out;
nevertheless, the Town Hall was packed,
and with the exception of two or three scores of
dissentients, the thousands present cordially
acclaimed the strong, straight man of Australian
politics. It was a tribute to courage and honesty
m politics, for Kingston showed no oratorical
graces. His speech—probably because of his ill-
ness and his recent over-hard work—was disap-
pointing when compared with other utterances on
lesser occasions, and was not in itself inspiring
except in brief flashes. But the palpable courage
of the man aroused enthusiasm. He took no yes-
no stand on any point, trimmed in no particular,
hesitated in nothing between honesty and expedi-
ency. He boldly arraigned the Foreign-trade
daily newspapers as liars; proved them so;
asserted firmly his Protectionist and nationalist
views; and left no doubt in anybody’s mind
that, no matter what the abuse and misrep-
resentation he earns thereby, he is resolute
to maintain an honest administration of the Cus-
toms Department. On all those points on which,
according to the Sydney daily papers, Kingston
is at utter variance with the sentiment of the
N. S. W. metropolis, he declared himself
without qualification and without trim-
ming;. and on each he was loudly cheered.
His indictment of the Sydney morning
papers was acclaimed, his declaration of
Protectionist faith was triumphantly endorsed,
and his announced resolution to continue to
administer his department without fear of the
rich importers’ frown was greeted with enthusias-
tic approval. It is evident that Kingston has
done very much personally to popularise the Pro-
tectionist cause in Sydney—the hotbed of the im-
porting disease—and also that the obvious good
effects of the tariff, weak as it is, are beginning to
make themselves felt in New South YVales.
The Victorian Civil Servants’ Member
Many Victorian railway men and other civil ser-
vants, it is alleged, are resolved, as a protest
against the Bent-Irvine Government’s “separate
representation” swindle, not to record any vote
at all at the State elections. There are also ridi-
culously humorous stories in circulation about
proposals to elect a Hindu and a Chinaman, and
about other weird devices for bringing “separate
representation” into contempt, but these are
mere crude comedy—the proposal to abstain from
voting is, in some quarters, real enough. No
doubt this idea might amount to something as a
protest, but all the same it will be a piece of
grievous ill-luck if any such tactics are adopted.
The idea should be rather to make the post
of civil service representative the blue ribbon
of Parliamentary life. If necessary, the civil
servants should subscribe enough money to make
the salary of each of their members equal to
that of a Cabinet Minister, and, having got the
right men, they should do all they can to assure
them that their positions will be permanent. And
they want, not Labor men in the narrow sense of
the term, but men who are good Democrats and
who are also large enough to reach out for
Cabinet rank—for the Premiership indeed. Such
a man as Isaacs (if he could be induced to step
down from the House of Reps, and qualify) would
be an admirable representative ; and if two others
of anything approaching his size could be found
the public service would present a formidable
front in the Legislature. It should strive at any
cost to put a specially good man into the House of
Landlords, where he would shine out conspicuously
as against the doddering imbecility of his fellow
members, and—if money can buy the right man
—he should be such a one that a Liberal Govern-
ment, when Bent is politically dead, can hardly
avoid, solely by reason of his merits, appoint-
ing him its Council representative. And in the
Assembly the service should try to have at least
one representative who has a fairly possible lien
on the Premiership. Then, having selected its
men, it should give them such a vote that they
can tell the House that they speak for the largest
constituency in Victoria as well as the widest, and
can bid a small fat man like Bent, who represents
a comparative handful out Brighton way, to take
a back seat and keep quiet till his intellectual and
political superior has done speaking. The separate
representation idea was intended to practically
disfranchise a large class of the community, but it
could be so worked, with proper management and
organisation, as to make Bent and Irvine curse
the day when the idea was invented.

The Vic. Police Funds Grab.
jT looks as though the Victorian police, who pur-
sue the Cabbage-Garden pickpocket, have them-
selves been the victims of a long series of em-
bezzlements and purse-trick frauds. It is an old
story and runs back a long way. In the beginning
2 per cent, was deducted from the policeman’s
pay to establish a superannuation and reward
fund. Naturally the money came in faster than
it went out, for the force then consisted of young
men almost exclusively, and the limit of retire-
ment was for most of them still a long way off.
Some idiot presently thought it was a pity that
they should wait till they were old men to get
their old-age pay; and the foolish idea was popular
enough to get itself adopted. A system of “good
conduct ” rewards was therefore established in
order to empty the till with all possible speed—if
it was a plain drunk, the constable seems to have
drawn 6d. for it; if it was a drunk with frills who
tore his uniform he presumably drew 9d. The
plan worked too well; the speed with which the
till was being emptied created alarm, and so that
stupid steal of insurance money was stopped.
The only redeeming feature of this particular
•steal was that some of the contributors to the
fund themselves divided the plunder. The next
embezzlement apparently took place between
1860 and 1864. In 1860 the fund was in credit
£60,658 ; by 1864 it was down to £45,195, although
the expenditure had never reached the income,
and most times was only about a third as much.
This seems to have been plain Government thiev-
ing. The constable of the period didn’t have a
vote, but even then had developed a large foot,
and he kicked. In reply, Parliament admitted
that he had been badly treated, so it gave him a
consolidating statute—it didn’t order that the
money should be put back in the till. Actual
public robbery of the fund seems, however, to
have been stopped for a while, and that was some-
thing. The State, nevertheless, still continued to
“ borrow ” the funds, and went on forgetting to
pay any interest for the money (there was only
one year in which a Treasurer had a spasm of
conscience and paid something in the way of
interest); but when, during the past few years,
things began to get bad in Victoria, it began
playing up with the principal again. There
were .£70,000 of debentures which had been the
backbone of the fund for years. In 1899 the
Treasurer got away with £20,000 of them ; next
year another £20,000 went; in 1901 still another
£20,000; and last year a desperate Treasurer,
looking in the till and finding .£lO,OOO still left,
promptly sold them too ! In return, the Govern-
ment professes to put in its I O U, undertaking
to pay police pensions out of revenue to make up
for the stealing of the bobby’s own pension fund.
But, as a matter of fact, Icicle Irvine isn’t pre-
pared to do anything of the kind. The police
paid into this fund over £120,000 in order to
permit of their retirement at 55 (afterwards
the limit was jumped up to 60) ; what the
Icicle proposes is compensation on retirement at
65. So the State first of all diddled the fund out
of about £50,000 of interest, then embezzled some
of the principal, and afterwards, having “con-
verted ” the balance and left the insurers helpless,
proceeds to rob each man of five years’ super-
annuation pay. Even Bent hasn’t yet struck the
idea of throwing off the train at Gastlemaine the
man who has paid his fare to Bendigo ; but the
Icicle Govt, seems capable of doing as much as
that.

Kingston and the N.T. Chinaman.
The following item is quoted verbatim from a
Sydney Ereetrade paper :
At the annual banquet of the Brisbane Chamber of
Commerce on Tuesday, Mr. John Arthur, in proposing
the toast of “ Parliament,” referred to Mr. Kingston and
the colored labor question. He said that Messrs. Millar
Bros, had a contract for the railway in the Northern
Territory of South Australia, and under alternative
tenders got £BO,OOO extra if they did it by' white
labor. After six months’ trial they had to give it up and
bring down Chinese. Mr. Kingston was the Premier of
South Australia, and the tropical portion of that State
contained 4000 Chinese and 400 whites. With such facts
before them he asked if Mr. Kingston was qualified to tell
them the methods to be adopted in regard to practical
legislation.
The Bulletin doesn’t know John Arthur and,
on the whole, it isn’t sorry ; if the above is a true
report, he doesn’t look a desirable acquaintance.
His statement, as reported above, certainly
doesn’t say that Kingston was Premier of
South Australia when the famous Chinese
railway contract was made in the Territory, or
that he was in any way responsible for that con-
tract, but it is difficult to see why Kingston’s
name is dragged in so needlessly unless to
leave that erroneous impression on the minds
of hasty readers. The Northern Territory
railway, from Palmerston to Pine Creek,
was certainly built with Chinese labor. It
is not true that it was so built because white men
couldn’t do the work in the Far North climate ;
from motives of disastrous economy Chinese labor
was adopted from the outset. And from that time
dates the ruin of the Territory. It ceased to be a
white man’s country ; the imported Chow settled
down in the land and a blight came upon it; white
immigration ceased ; the new railway never paid
expenses ; and the stupendous annual deficit of
the Chow-ridden, Chow-cursed, Chow-damned
Territory has been a burden on S. A. eversince. It
was in 1889 that the Northern Territory railway
was finished, and four years later, in 1893, Kingston
first became Premier of S. A. And as Premier of the
Territory, as well as of S.A., he had a better
opportunity than most men to size up the full
dimensions of the Asiatic curse, and he shaped his
white Australia policy accordingly. It is now up
to Mr. John Arthur, of Brisbane, and the Cham-
ber of Commerce which he represents, to explain
these things and remove all misapprehension. If
it was intended to be conveyed that Kingston
was in any way responsible for the Chinese curse
which has fallen on the Territory, then the
intention was to convey a slander and a
delusion. If there was no such intention, then
there remains a good opportunity for the im-
porters of Brisbane to mention publicly that they
are fair and honorable white men, who had no
desire whatever to deceive.

The Broken Roof.
A GOOD part of the chairman’s speecn. at the
annual meeting of the Brisbane Chamber of Com-
merce consisted of a vigorous curse of Government
control of railways. Said Carter, M.L.C., the
chairman :
Lease the railways. In private hands, the lines could
be made to pay better than they were now paying, and
at any rate the loss would not fall on the State.
In reply to which foolish advice a lot of things
could be said, but two will do :
(1) In private hands, the lines might perhaps be made
to pay better, simply because, when Garter, M.L.C.,
headed his Chamber of Commerce to the syndicate’s
office with a demand for a reduction of rates for carrying
stock, or fodder, or something, he would be told that
that wasn’t a Benevolent Asylum, but a place where they
expected 20s. at least for £l. He might even be called a
begging pauper. If Carter, M.L.C., and all the Cham-
ber of Commerce people who point out at intervals that
the railways would pay better if they were leased will
help the Govts, to run the lines on the cold commercial
basis on which a syndicate would run them, there won’t
be any need for a syndicate or a lease. But what Carter,
M.L.C., and others do is to knock holes in the roof, and
then complain that the rain comes in.
(2) A good many of the lines weren’t built to pay off-
hand, and can’t he expected to do it. Carter,
M.L.C., himself talked of the “enormous resources”
of the country; and most of the lines have been
built to get at some of those alleged resources.
The S. Australian Govt., which began to build a
transcontinental line on its own account, stopped for
want of funds; but now, when it invites “private
hands ” to finish the job, it doesn’t invite them to do it
on the old terms, because it knows they' wouldn’t think
of it —it offers to subsidise them with the freehold of a
territory as large as three or four European kingdoms put
together. If the Govt., when it built the same sort of
line, had similarly endowed its own Railway Depart-
ment, and added the rents and other profits to railway
revenue, there wouldn’t be one State-railway failure for
ten private-railway failures. But the people whom
Carter, M.L.C., mostly represents, want the Govt, to
build the railway and endow them with the land ; where-
fore they make the money which the State ought to get.
So what Carter, M.L.C., and the others do in that case
is to take several shingles off the roof, and then complain
that the rain comes in.
Anyhow, if Carter, M.L.C., really wants to
see the splendid results that private railway
enterprise can produce, there are no lack of ex-
amples. The Emu Bay Co. (Tas.), Midland of
Westralia (bankrupt despite a 3,000,000-acre sub-
sidy and cash assistance), the old Tasmanian
Main Line, Sydney Railroad Co., the Midland
Co. of M.L. and Queensland’s own Mareeba-
Chillagoe affair are all there. Likewise there are
others.

Industrial Arbitration.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, just at the time when
Victoria was on the edge of a great crisis
through the lack of a fair-minded judicial tribunal
to which an appeal could be made to settle an in-
dustrial dispute, discovered that the N. S.W.
Industrial Arbitration Act was a failure. The
trend of an article, somewhat yes-noey in tone,
shows clearly enough in these excerpts :
When every decision that is not wholly favorable pro-
vokes either abuse of the Court or a demand for its re-
constitution in such a form that it will be more likely to
give judgments acceptable to one of the parties to litiga-
tion, it is not unreasonable to doubt at any rate the
present probability of the workers adapting themselves
to an essentially judicial system of composing industrial
difficulties.
Why not make the scheme one of conciliation, instead
of litigation, and save the State all the expense and the
irritation ?
If the two sections for whom the system was created
continue to express dissatisfaction with it, the public will
very soon have to consider whether the experiment is
worth going on with or whether its failure shall be con-
sidered proved.
This is quite a new Tory demand. An Industrial
Arbitration Court must, it seems, not only
give a just decision and keep the industrial
peace ; it must also completely please both parties
to all suits, or be abolished ! It is difficult to
imagine how such egregious folly can be penned
for the reading of persons who, presumably, have
some common-sense. It is not demanded of the
Central Criminal Court, in adjudicating on the
case of Blinky Bill (who has entered the pre-
mises of James Weight as a burglar and stolen
therefrom), that it should please both, or either of,
the parties to the suit. Blinky Bill’s dissatis-
faction at getting 10 years’ gaol, and James
Wright’s possible dissatisfaction that Blinky
Bill was not grilled over a slow fire, are matters
that the law refuses to take into considera-
tion at all. Provided that the community’s
interests are served, the law moves on
its majestic way undisturbed by the blas-
phemy of Bill or the grumblings of James.
Similarly, the Industrial Arbitration Court cannot
be expected, is not expected by sane people, to
please every suitor in every case. What the com-
munity demands of the Court is that its decisions
shall be reasonable and just and shall be firmly
enforced. No amount of grumbling should dis-
turb the equanimity of the Court, and if, as is
alleged, there is grumbling from both employers
and employed at its decisions, that is some sort of
an indication that the scales of justice are evenly
held. On the whole, allowing for the fact that
the N.S.W. Arbitration Court is a human insti-
tution and therefore not impeccable, allowing also
for the fact that it is entering upon an entirely
new and unexplored sphere of effort, it may be
congratulated on having done excellent work.
And the public (which is not exactly the ass that
the D.T., with its recent ideas of doddering
Toryism, seems to think it) is not likely to con-
sider its operations a failure because necessarily
they have displeased somebody, any more than
it is likely to abolish an honest customs system
because it interferes with the predatory habits of
such firms as Robert Reid and Co.
