Australia’s Funeral.
There is a mine of pathos in the little wires
which come from the inside of Australia when-
ever a weather disturbance happens along. The
clouds bank up “ at the back of Bourke,” a few
drops of rain fall, and “our local correspondent,”
who has been waiting for rain for months—who
has heard the drought talked about every hour of
every day for a year past —rushes to the telegraph
office and earns sixpence by letting “his paper”
know that “rain has commenced to fall, and
prospects are decidedly hopeful.” It is dry
by the time he gets back to the pub where he
is going to spend the sixpence, and in an hour the
old dust storm is blowing as usual; but the daily
paper has been given an excuse to produce again
its stereotyped reflections on “ the break-up of
the drought” and “the early return of pro-
sperity.” It would be a lot nearer the truth now
to say that the drought never does break up alto-
gether ; if it is slightly damaged in one part it
continues in full working order in all the rest. So
far, in fact, from any good coming out of these
pathetic little 10 or 12 points which the inside
usually gets from the weather disturbance that
floods the coast, the effect is nearly always disas-
trous; for the change simply means more wind than
usual, and every such added gust of wind buries
another few square yards of this continent in a
grave of rolling, drifting sand. Under aboriginal
conditions the inlands were never overstocked ;
when drought afflicted one part the game moved
to another part, and the native went with them,
and the droughty part recovered. But when white
men came, and brought their stock, and built their
fences, the burden couldn’t be shifted off the sick
country when the drought struck it; fit or not,
it had to carry the hoofs And so disaster came.
The low edible bushes were eaten down till
they died. The higher scrub was cut till the
country was barren. In our national lunacy we
have sometimes even paid men (with borrowed
money) to do this work of scrub destruction. If
a shower fell, and there was a spring of grass, it
was wolfed as soon as it showed itself. The sur-
face was no longer knit by grass-roots ; for miles
around every watering-place it was broken by the
sharp hoof-points; and the wind, unchecked
any longer by bush or scrub, swept the
loose sand into rolling mounds, on which,
even in comparatively good seasons, nothing
will grow, except, perhaps, a little quickly-
germinating, quickly-dying soft herbage. Thus
every wind-storm adds to the desolation ; and
every severe pinch of drought, involving more
destruction of scrub and bush, adds to the speed
with which the inlands are being submerged.
And yet Australia might have expected it; for it
is the way that every other similarly-circum-
staneed continent has gone. There are cities
buried under the Sahara, in Africa—cities that
once stood in fruitful lands ; and there are cities
and treasures beneath the sand-drifts of the Gobi,
in Asia. Similar encroachments Australia may
be able to avert; but as yet Australia hardly
realises that there is anythingto avert—itcertainly
doesn’t generally realise, when it reads that the
drought has broken up, because showers have
fallen around the coast and at a few inland
stations, that for the most part the inland’s
portion has been gales of wind, for which the
continent must pay with a few more acres of
desert. Until Australia does realise what is
taking place, there is no hope at all; and when it
has realised it, there probably isn’t much. Years
of absolute rest might do, rest such as there was
when only the kangaroo and our black brother
were there ; and even then the imported rabbit
would prejudice the scheme. But Australia
won’t take that heroic measure ; probably it
can’t, for it would mean writing off millions from
the country’s supposed wealth. So Australia will
almost certainly go on getting buried till the
continent will be a Sahara with a moderately
fertile rim and a few inland oases.

Civilised Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He’s lazy and fond of his ease,
His trumpet he frequently blows,
He frequently knocks at the knees,
He frequently turns in his toes.
He’s frequently red at the nose,
He’s frequently bulky to span,
And he winds a big horn as lie goes,
And they christen him “ Civilised Man
His faith is a matter of fees,
And heathens are all of his foes,
He frequently launches on sprees
And wakes in the morning morose
His shoddy’s a torrent that flows
From Beersheba down unto Dan,
He’s a bantam that constantly crows
And they christen him “Civilised Man
The gods of the heathen he flees
And worships his own in a doze,
He’s lord of the land and the seas
And the stars with their treasures, he trows;
So proud and so paunchy his pose—
His form in amazement you scan,
This two-legged limper in clothes
Who’s christened as “Civilised Man
He’s not a fit subject for prose,
And Poetry’s not in his plan ;
A creature unique in his nature he grows
And they christen him “Civilised Man”
P. L.

»
The quality of the latest production at
Sydney Royal is an improvement on the lament-
able “ Dick Whittington,” and even if it is not a
very aggressive improvement, still the advance
is a subject for congratulation. Musical comedy
is generally something about a girl—the “Gaiety
Girl,” or the “ Shop Girl,” or the “ Work
Girl,” or the miscellaneous girl, or something
like that; so the “Circus Girl” is strictly
orthodox in its line. Musical comedy is also, as a
rule, a ihixture of comedy, burlesque, opera, night-
mare, kaleidoscope, oil-painting, and circus ; and
here, again, the new production is strictly orthodox.
The plot is so fragmentary as to be reminiscent
of those Centralian rivers which disappear for
long stretches under the sand, and reappear as
suddenly without any ostensible reason. The
incoherent story, or rather paragraph, keeps on
vanishing in a wilderness of variety turns and re-
appearing cheerfully at intervals, with a pre-
tence that it hasn’t been away at all. What the
present Royal audiences enjoy is the sight of a
British Honorable being shot out of a cannon, in
which he has taken refuge from a jealous wife ;
and the comparison between the gigantic Toothick
Pasha, the wrestler of Drivelli’s circus, and the
diminutive Cockney mixer of drinks, who has
undertaken to wrestle him in the hope of winning
£lOO so as to be able to marry Lucille, the slack-wire-
walker. The British Honorable who has fallen in
love with the legs of La Favorita, the Circus Girl,
is not a specially interesting personage, and in the
fortunes of La Favorita. herself no one is much
concerned. Even the loves of Dick Capel, the
Cannon King, and Dora Weymss, the girl
who is in Paris to marry the British Honor-
able aforementioned, are comparatively dull.
The interest in the show centres round the
Cockney barman and Lucille. That tiny adven-
turer, having accepted the challenge to wrestle
Toothick, decides that it is a case of guile and
not brute strength; so he fraternises with his
monster, and pours 25 francs worth of champagne
into him (the giant is nearly seven feet high, and
is understood to be a native of the State just now
being ruined by Bent). The giant breaks things,
and is got into the circus with difficulty, and the
curtain rises a second and shows the mammoth
down on all fours and the pigmy pushing for all
he is worth to roll him over. When he has done
this the slight thread of coherence peters out.
Thenceforward it is all spectacular.
• * * »
Hugh Ward takes the part of the circus
proprietor and works it for all it is worth.
Maurice Dudley makes a big success of his part as
a Commissaire of Police who is waiting to hear
the bugle sound to intimate to him that he is a
father, and is reduced to imbecility by the bugle
sounding three times at short intervals. The
last seen of Dudley is when he is rushing home
to stop that bugle sounding again at any cost.
Miss Rose Hamilton as the querulous wife of
Drivelli, the circus proprietor, has a squalid part,
but plays it well. Elsie Moore, the Circus Girl,
has to struggle with the dullest item of the show,
and the fact that it isn’t anything like so dull as it
might be says a great deal for the lady. The
people who have the best opportunities are Miss
Rose Musgrove as Lucille, which part she plays
with considerable skill and human sympathy;
C. R. Bantock as Sir Titus Weymss, and Fred
Graham as Biggs. They are three artists whom
one meets with pleasure and parts from with
reluctance.

The large black goloshes and the private
secretary with his Bath bun and his glass of milk
appeared duly at Sydney Criterion to an appre-
ciative but not boisterously enthusiastic audience
last Saturday. The curate of to-day is not so
much the bun-devouring animal as formerly. He
is more often a large, aggressive person who has
joined the Young Men’s Christian Athletic
Society and patronises football. Consequently
the “Private Secretary” isn’t the biting satire
that it was years ago, but so long as Frank
Thornton manages the brolly and the bun it will
continue to be revived at uncertain intervals with a
certain amount of success. The plot hasn’t varied
with the even flow of years. Douglas Cattermole
pretends to be the private secretary, and the
private secretary unconsciously figures as Douglas
Cattermole. He is also understood to be a spirit-
medium owing to his habit of going to sleep with
a tablecloth round him. The rest is mostly loud
cries, confusion, the frequent ejecting of the
secretary, and the sailing of the bun and the
bottle of milk along the atmosphere. There is no
great moral attached to the “Private Secretary,”
except that the Meek and Lowly are liable to be
shot into a large box and that he who ruleth his
spirit shall invariably lose his golosh ; but what
moral there may be is well conveyed by Frank
Thornton and not utterly spoilt by the rest of the
company.
As the Rev. Robert Spalding, Frank
Thornton makes the most of a character wherein
the humor must be conveyed largely by facial
expression, and by a humble and contrite accent,
and much business with the bun and the
brolly. The private secretary has but one
epigram, and he makes it while being stowed
into a trunk. John Denton, as the tailor
who desires to rise in society, acts well.
Joseph Wilson is fair as an excitable uncle—the
uncle whom Harwood used to make a man of
might in the long ago, The stage uncle is invari-
ably a fiery person. Alexander Bradley makes a
presentable hero, but R. Erskine, as another
uncle —a worried-looking one—in a red coat, acts
as if shot into the part at the last moment. As a
lady who sees ghosts, Mrs. Walter Hill shows
some force, and Miss Yera Fordyce and Miss
Leonie Norbury gambol round as the usual light-
hearted English girls. Miss Norbury is playful
in a somewhat perfunctory fashion.

An especially new item appeared at Fitz-
geralds’ Circus on Saturday, when three noblv-
born horses clove the air and disappeared into a
tank about 18ft. deep and containing 300,000 gals.
of water. The jump is taken from a height of
20ft., and to make it look as if the event happens
casually at “our beautiful harbor” or thereabouts,
and is done by way of an agreeable pastime, a
good deal of scenery is painted round the tank.
Likewise there is a large advertisement telling
the public to drink somebody’s something,
without which no harbor is complete. The
horses, by the way, follow the excellent
advice of the advertisement, and have a little
nip of brandy before taking their bath, with a
warm bran mash and a massage afterwards. As
they jump they spread their hoofs in the air in
a masterly fashion, but probably do it to get away
from the advertisement which is specially hideous.
The horse-act is a serious and poetic function,
comic relief being supplied by three humans in
weird garments who flop into the tank first. The
rest of the show is very much as before. Mdlle.
Onra flutters along the wire like a silver butterfly,
and Mdlle. Dagmar, a cream white lady on a
cream white horse, has a picturesque effect. The
performing dog still ties and unties himself, while
the “educated donkey” halves himself publicly
and leaves the ring in detachments.

“Are You a Mason?” continues its
wonderful success at Sydney Palace ; last Satur-
day (the fifth of the play) the house was as much
crowded as on the first night. There is a moral
for theatre managers in this, and it is: Spend
money and you make money; try to save money
and you lose money. There is as yet no whisper
of the withdrawal of “ Are You a Mason ? ” and
no intimation as to whether the company pro-
ducing it will be seen in other comedies. The
general hope is that it will—in as many other
comedies as possible.
514 514 5)4 *
At Sydney Lyceum “ A Woman of
Pleasure ” concludes her rapid career on Friday to
give place to “ A Face at the Window,” said to
be a thrilling detective drama. It is to be hoped
that the “ humor” of the new piece will be better
than was inflicted on the public in the last. In
“The Woman of Pleasure” the comic relief of
one Act consists largely of the funny man being
bereft of his garments in public, and this dreary
old semi-nude joke is spun out to unnecessary
length. It is alleged that the man who has to take
one of the parts in this scene nearly threw up his
billet rather than go through with it, but this
statement may be a gross libel. These remarks
were reserved till this late stage so that the
Purity Brigade would not have time to crowd
the theatre to see if the play was as bad as it was
painted.

have changed their turn somewhat and Seeley (or
it may be West) adds new fun to his coon im-
personation. The Boissetts, pantomimists and
acrobats, continue to be the chief attraction.
5)4 51* 514 *
Notwithstanding the strike, the new
comic opera, “My Lady Molly,” was welcomed
on Saturday by the usual crowd, most of whom
had trammed or tramped to Her Majesty’s.
Sidney Jones, the “Geisha” man, is responsible
for the “Lady Molly” music, which is bright,
tuneful, catchy stuff, unweighted by dull,
unattractive passages. Its weakness, may be,
lies in a certain lack of the robustness appropriate
to an 18th century picture. The story, Arcadian
and stereotyped, is enacted by characters remin-
iscent of “Dorothy”—a hunting squire, duelling
gallants, masquerading maidens, an amorous
spinster, and a comic menial. Squire Bathurst
destines his daughter, Carrie Moore, to be thebride
of the baritone captain. Neither dad nor daughter
has ever seen the captain, and she refuses to
marry him, in any case, owing to her forbidden
fancy for the small tenor. Miss Florence Young,
in the name-part, has previously scorned the
addresses of the baritone, but she also scorns the
thought of letting the other girl get him. To
prevent them meeting, she steals her old love’s
portmanteau containing his gala array and letters
of introduction. Florence Young and Celia
Ghiloni, having put on the captain’s finery,
including nether garments so tight that Satur-
day’s audience was frightened to see them walk
down the stage stairs, pass themselves off as
Harold Thorley and friend ; with the result that
when the real baritone arrives he is imprisoned M
a highwayman. Then the misunderstanding" are
cleared up in a satisfactory musical manner-
f ( $ •
To Thorley, as the hero, fall some good
songs which he treats to advantage, and his
appearance and stage form show a vast improve-
ment on his first Australian effort. But he must
get on ever so much more “pace’’before he can
dream of exciting a thrill in even the most
susceptible woman’s soul. Florence Young sings
well and acts intelligently, as usual, but reveals
her lack of resource by the resemblance of her
“boy” business, to her remembered tricks
as Dorothy in breeches. Reginald Roberts,
with his American intonation, seems _ out
of drawing with his old English surroundings.
Carrie Moore, too, as the Squire’s daughter, rubs
in the fact that she is not the sort of girl to play
an old world Miss —all prunes, prism and propriety.
Meekly plastered hair disagrees with her features,
and when she sings “I’m going to be very good ’
in coon style hearers can’t help reflecting that,
though the words are the words of a country
mouse, the airs are those of a town cat. Bathurst
makes a decorative old English father. Maud
Chetwynd, in short skirts of pale green, flits
about lightly as a thistle-down fay. Celia
Ghiloni gets some swing into her song of
a trousseau. Marietta Nash does good work
but somehow does not succeed in disguising her
back-numberishness. Lauri, as an Irish gossoon,
has to keep himself in hand so as to fit in with the
Old English picture. In “Florodora” brazen
waggery and gaggery went down well, but in
“My Lady Molly” the fun must be quiet and
quaint. Decorative dances and bright choruses
help out the effect of the new production at Her
Majesty’s. One of the most comic bits in the
piece is provided by a group of staid, portly
chorus-men, who coquette with flower-arches in a
wedding scene. Said plump vocal identities are
understood to flirt with the bridesmaids, make
eyes and frisk generally. Their frisk methods
suggest the mature gaiety of the rhinoceros.
5)4 * * *
Bland Holt’s new drama, “The Price of
Peace,” got a fair start at Melbourne Royal on
Saturday night. The title has no reference to
the engine-drivers’ trouble. It means that Walter
Baker, disguised as the Prime Minister of Britain,
takes a mean advantage of a Russian gentleman
by shooting him in the small of the back,
whereby the unhappy foreigner is prevented
from sending such information to the Rus-
sian Government as would bring disaster
to certain British troops. Then Balter con-
gratulates his tough political conscience on
having secured peace with murder, after which he
expires on the floor of the House of Commons.
Next week The Bulletin will have‘something to
say about the clever performance of Baker and
others; also the weak points of Holt’s deserving
show will receive attention.

At the Princess Theatre the banner of
Shakspeare floats on the breeze of popular favor
as gaily as circumstances permit. Rudinoff and
Co. are doing their best at the Opera House, but
the Bijou management decided to close the
season rather than present a firm, unflinching
front to the railway strike.
** * *
Any deficiencies in the Melbourne show
items above must be set down to the fact that some
interesting copy is wandering helplessly over the
face of the earth in Bent’s trains. That extra-
ordinary individual got away about six trains
on Monday, therefore if six sound engines
could be picked out of the wreck to which
he has reduced the Vic. rolling-stock,
these trains should have reached their destin-
ations. Nevertheless his Sydney express broke
down, and at latest advices was about 24 hours
late on a six-hours’ journey. Bent is a great rail-
way manager.

“ The Fortune Teller,” with its spectacular
magnificence and occasional rise into grand
opera, has been doing big business at the Adelaide
Royal. Madame Slapoffski scores particularly
as the girl who is another girl as well, and
occasionally an improbable young man into the
bargain, out the whole row of principals is strong.
Coleman has left the Tivoli, but Winton
stays on as business-manager. He now, by the
way, advertises himself as “Jay Winton,” to the
great derision of M’Ginty. Spry and Monti
improve on acquaintance.
Launceston (Tas.) Examiner in a fit of
admiration :
Mr. Reid realises that before you can collect customs
duties you must have imports.
Which puts the whole idiotic revenue tariff
disease in a sentence. You must have lots of
imports so that you can make money by taxing
them, just as you must have lots of drunkards if
you are going to make money out of fines for
drunkenness, and lots of leprosy if a poll-tax on
lepers is going to yield any appreciable income.
And to have these imports you must keep down
local industry. And then to pay for these imports
you must borrow money. The Freetrader believes
in keeping down local manufactures, so that he
may buy other people’s goods and tax himself for
the privilege of doing so, in order that by paying
the tax to himself he may have some money.
Item from a Sydney daily :
MR. BENT’S OPINION.
The Minister for Railways, when seen to-day, said :
“ The Government must and shall win, and the sorriest
men in the country presently will be the drivers and fire-
men, who are giving up their gilt-edged billets. lam
greatly improved in health, and feel as gay as a bird to-
day. Now, that the strike has come, it relieves the strain
on the feelings wonderfully.”
The brutish joy of Caliban when he has smashed
something ! Could there be a more absolute con-
demnation of the Victorian Government’s attitude
than the gaiety of this unspeakable man when he
has brought so much suffering on the State and
ruin to so many individuals ?

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

purchase paying mines, and mining properties (not
merely surface shows), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and

Ore without sulphides in which the gold values
are finely disseminated throughout the gangue is one
amenable to cyanidation or chlorination. Should this
ore contain sulphides it could not be so simply treated.
It is now recognised to be good practice to first concen-
trate out the sulphurets on Wilfley Tables and to treat
the tailing thus cleaned by the proper lixiviation process.
Should the ore be a tellurid? that would require previous
roasting the tendency to “ bead ” or “ shot ” in the roast-
ing is surmounted by passing the leached pulp over
"Wilfley Tables, by which the values too coarse to be dis-
solved by the lixiviation process, are concentrated, to-
gether, with any unroasted ore, sulphides or foreign
matter. Illustrated catalogues of the Wilfley Table, with
valuable notes on ore dressing, can be obtained from the
Austral Otis Company, South Melbourne, the Sole Manu-
facturers in Australia.*

to which we referred last week, are sole agents for
Australasia.*

To empty old abandoned shafts, or to cope with
inrushes of water at a minimum cost, may be effected by
the use of the Vertical Plunger Sinking Pumps, manufac-
tured by The A. S. Cameron Steam Pump Works of New
"York ; and represented by Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, who hold stocks of these and various other
sizes and styles of steam or air pumps. These pumps are
distinct from any other make of so-called “Cameron’
Pump. They work automatically, and have no dead
centre, neither have they springs nor outside valve gear
to become deranged. They occupy less space in a shaft
than any other Sinking Pump of same capacity.*

Westralia continues to make it difficult to
understand why its Treasurer, Gardiner, went out

All the same this surplus was with one exception the
smallest for the year, while the expenditure—£3ol,l94
was absolutely the greatest in Westralia’s history.
The principal features of all which are these
N.S.W. probably continues the shocking example of the
group. Victoria is most likely a shocking example, too
but its sins won’t be found out for another couple of
months. Queensland, at the present rate of going will
have a deficit of about £400,000; that is to say in ten
months it has saved £242,757, and in two months
more it has to pay out £748,000. Still its recent
movements are more satisfactory. Tasmania, through
doing the thing which G. H. Reid said would ruin
and impoverish any country, is climbing into affluence.
S. Australia is, comparatively, also in temporary affluence.
And Westralia, which still collects border customs duties
of its own, continues to have a good time, and (unless
some amazing foolishness or bad luck happens in the next
couple of months) will have Australia’s one substantial
surplus for the year. With the possible exception of S.
Aus., and the probable exception of Tas. (of which it is
too early yet to speak) it will, in plain English, be the one
Australian State which won’t have to borrow money in
order to pay its interest bill, although that is alleged to
be the thing that no Australian State ever does
* * • •
The Commonwealth Government requires its
civil servants to insure their lives in some “ approved ”
office, and it has published a list of “ approved ” offices.
Thereby it in some sense guarantees these offices as
thoroughly desirable concerns in which to insure, and
gives them a certificate to that effect. The list of
‘approved ” offices, as published in the Age, includes
the Australian Metropolitan—a concern whose expenses
and dividends last year swallowed up more than the
entire income ; a concern that accumulated out of earn-
ings less than £lO,OOO in its first six years, and then
saw more than half of it vanish in its seventh year ;
and whose alleged assurance fund is mostly created on
paper by persistent writing-up of the nominal
value of its building. At its investigation it only
prevented its assurance fund appearing as far less than
its liabilities by the process of writing up its building.
The Commonwealth Government, according to the Age
has “ approved” of this office ; likewise of the Standard
Life Association which is in a very different category but
which is so small and new that a wise Government would
wait awhile before putting it on the list. Also, it has
seemingly “ approved ” of the small and seedy Temper-
ance and General. Does the department which looks
after these things make any inquiry, or does it “ approve ”
at random ?

The Equitable Permanent 8.8. L. and S.I. is in the habit
of presenting a fearful mass of figures, through which
only a glimmer of light can be seen ; and the glimmer
isn’t much brighter this time than usual. The profits are

bursements to shareholders a great deal larger. In addi-
tion to the dividend and bonus to shareholders in the
ordinary way, £247 has been distributed as a bonus to
borrowing shareholders ; in the foregoing, this amount
as an expense and is deducted from profits.
The cause of this burst of generosity, which even went to
the length of distributing some of the reserves, isn’t ex-
plained. The shareholders’ funds at the commencement
of the year amounted to £70,425 capital, and £28,253
reserves—a total of £98,678. Upon this the profits (ex-
clusive of the amount to be divided among borrowers)
are equal to £8 11s. 9d. per cent., which is a good result
nowadays.
• * * *
More theories and more explanations keep com-
ing to hand in the controversy between John Bridge and
Go., Sydney wool-brokers, and the Association of Buying
a, n Brokers. It is shown on the Association’s
oc/ 90 ar as particular incident (referred to, B.
25/4/03) is concerned, Bridge and Co. were in the
wrong, and that their “ manifesto ” to the public gave an
and misleading statement of the case. As to
® a ®. 'arg*r question—the benefit or otherwise of the rules
which the buying and selling brokers have mutually
drawn up for the regulation of the wool trade—it is not
possible to be positive, but the case for the brokers looks
feasible and is as follows :
(4) That some strict rules and regulations to govern
the sale of wool are necessary, otherwise the work of a
season could not be satisfactorily carried out.
(2) That rules which appear to the outside public to be
arbitrary have to be considered in the light of the
peculiar requirements of the trade and to be judged by
their effect in actual working ; that, in this connection,
the rules are drawn up after free and full discussion, that
they are fully approved of by the broking firm which is
supposed to specially represent the producers, and that,
to the knowledge of the chief brokers, they do not in any
way conflict with the interests of the producers.
Ib is set down that wool must nob be offered at
Sydney by auction more than twice. This, it is claimed,
is necessary to avoid congestion at the auction sales ; an
unreasonable reserve might be put on some wool, and the
Offering of it again and again would prevent other wool
getting a chance at auction ; wool may be catalogued and
offered for private sale as many times as the producer
chooses. The rule that a lot of wool must not be removed
from one broker’s store to another is defended on the
ground that it prevents a competition among brokers
which would be possibly very bad for the grower. One
broker having offered wool at a reserve of 10d., and not
gettingit, another broker, “to secure the business,” might
guarantee the grower lOd. and sell it to a buyer at that
figure, by allowing him to get at the same time a 9d. wool
for Sid. That, of course, would be bad for the man
who owned the 9d. wool. In effect, the brokers claim
that cut-throat competition amongst them would serve
no good purpose, and might serve an evil one. So far as
The Bulletin can judge, from an inquiry as to the rules,
the system of trade-unionism among the brokers is of
advantage in many respects, and inflicts no hardship on
the producer. At the same time it occurs to this paper
that if any union of carpenters or railway employees had
equally stringent rules, the wool-brokers would want the
military called out to fire on these carpenters or railway
men. The “restriction of free competition,” the
“restraint of trade,” the fining and boycotting of
the broker who “blacklegs” on the union, the
dictation which the brokers exercise towards a
leading industry, and their interference with other
people’s business are all things they would denounce
with great bitterness if the Hebrew Hod-Carriers’
Association were the offender. The Bulletin, however,
has a friendly feeling for the Hebrew Hod-Carriers’ Asso-
ciation, and though the wool-brokers carry their trades-
unionism pretty far, it isn’t prepared to say that they are
wrong.

The Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative Society,
Ltd. (N.S.W.), has steadily increased its business, though
the increase isn’t what it might have been if the Richmond
River cow were drought-proof :

Mar., 1903.. 2957 {| 0 P n S’ 8 = 2 |ll} 121 639
Gross profit was £2999 ; from this, £42 is taken for wear
and tear. Of that gross profit £1603 was made in the first
half of the year; £1396 in the second half—a rather con-
siderable falling away. In spite of that, bonuses are
being put up instead of being modified : previously, all
consignors received 20 per cent., while for the past
year dairy-produce consignors are to get 25 per
cent. The result is a ridiculously small addition
to the reserves—a matter of only £l2l, or about
4 per cent, of the total profits. This, in view of
the exceedingly large proportion of credit business done
by the society, and hence the large possibilities in the
way of bad debts, is insufficient. The business is
advancing very satisfactorily (for the first year the value
of the produce sold was £71,855, while for last year, the
third, it was £277,439); and there ought not to be any need,
therefore, to offer such an unreasonable bribe as a 25 p.c.
bonus. In fact, the business that can’t be got without
an inducement of that size had better be allowed to. go
somewhere else—for the present, anyway.
• • • •
George Turner, as acting-Minister for Customs,
found himself compelled recently to meet the fraudulent
importer with a new regulation : “ The acting-Minister
directs that, in view of the number of cases in which
goods are included in the packages and not in the invoice,
such goods will in future be forfeited, and no excuse
whatever will be accepted.” This moved the anguished
Sydney Telegraph to announce that Liberty had now
finally departed from Australia : “ Whatever the value of
the goods,” moaned that paper, “ they may be forfeited
right out and that through no fault of merchants on this
side." It is a pity the ineffably silly Telegraph doesn’t
better disguise its sympathy with commercial thieves.
If the man at the other side sends goods to the man at
this side, and doesn’t charge them in the invoice—the
genuine invoice, that is—the man here only loses some-
thing he hasn’t paid for. But the exporter "doesn’t heap
presents on the importer in that off-hand fashion, so the
goods are charged in the genuine invoice, but not in the
bogus invoice presented at the Customs. The Depart-
ment now warns the commercial thieves that this variety
of bogus invoice swindle must leave off—wherefore the
Telegraph’s decision that Liberty has now been blown
out like a tandsticker match.
• • • •
The last three annual reports of the Indepen-
dent Order of Oddfellows, N.S.W., a long-established
friendly society with a good name and a N.S.W. member-
ship of 8256, have been received, along with an invitation
to post-mortem them. The reports are, however, pre-
sented in such a slipshod way that serious analysis is
impossible. The table which purports to be a “ state-
ment showing state of funds as on 31st December, 1900
and 1901, respectively,” really shows, according to the
headings to the columns, the position at Oct. 31st, 1900,
and Dec. 31st. 1900. Again in the 1901 report the funds
of the Grand Lodge (practically the insurance fund of the
Order) are said to have been, for 1900, £27,662 ; but for
that same year they are, in the 1903 report, said to have
been only £23,074. In the 1902 report the amount of
Grand Lodge funds out at interest is said to have been,
at Jan. 31st, 1902, £23,833; in the 1903 report the
amount at that same date (Jan. 31st, 1902) is
said to have been £24,309. These and a great
many more eccentricities of the same sort pro-
bably admit of explanation ; meantime they make the
reports valueless except as curiosities. The best that can
be said is that the last report appears to be the best of
the three ; and if for the future the returns, etc., are pre-
pared on the same lines, effective checking and com-
parison may be possible. Apparently the society is
making satisfactory progress with the investment of
funds, a matter to which the average friendly society pays
little or no attention. On Dec. 31st, 1902, the total funds
(including those of branches) totalled £63,257, which
earned £1966 interest, or £3 2s. 2d. per cent, (with the
usual muddling this is referred to in the report as the
interest for 1892 instead of for 1902); whereas, when
Registrar Coghlan previously overhauled the State’s
friendly societies, the earnings of the 1.0.0. F. were, for
1899, found to have been £2 lls. 7d. per cent.
• * • *
Westralia is the only State in Australasia which
has anything important in the way of a Public Debt
sinking fund. The fund now amounts to £810,309.
About the most brutally unkind thing ever said officially
about Australian finance was the assurance recently
given, in connection with the new W.A. loan, that this
fund is invested in the hands of trustees in London, so
that no dishonest Treasurer can get at it. The assurance
doesn’t really amount to anything, for the dishonest
Treasurer can get at it indirectly any day, after the
Maoriland fashion ; finding there is, say, £1,000,000 sink-
ing fund in London which has been provided out of
revenue, he can borrow £1,000,000 and put it into
revenue, and thereby nullify the whole business;
and this will probably come to pass as soon as
the boom begins to fade. But it is a bitter jibe
at the instability of the Australian character when
a Government cheerfully acknowledges that it can’t
be trusted with its own money and has, in a manner of
speaking, issued a prohibition order against itself to
keep itself from drinking, and locked itself up to keep
itself from thieving. Australian Governments are be-
coming a bye-word in the way of thieving. Victoria has
prigged and put into revenue the Police Superannuation
Fund, and most of Turner’s sinking fund, the railway
accident fund and the railway stores fund. S.A. has
prigged the land assurance fund provided under Torrens’
Act. N.S.W., at the end of the present financial year,
proposes to steal all that is left of the Civil Service Super-
annuation Fund. Maoriland has stolen the sinking fund.
These are a few items quoted from memory, but there are
many others.
• * * *
When, in the Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords,
it was proposed, the other day, to allow a trustee co. to
act for art executor in obtaining probate jointly with
other executors, the Gent One crowd sprang upon the
harmless and necessary idea as an unholy interference
with its own privilege of gorging on dead men’s estates.
Vic.’s journeyman Solicitor-General Wynne was more
virulent than most; conveniently forgetting that no trus-
tee co. had ever packed up its portmanteau and faded
away beyond the horizon, after swindling the beneficiaries
under a will, he made some delicate allusions to trustee
co.’s “ robbing the public ” and “ grabbing the
fees.” Yet, even if there had not been any Greys and
Harpers, and other disappearing “Gents One,” Wynne’s
arguments could be riddled by the hard facts of the
case. Of 13 trustee co.’s doing business in the Common-
wealth last year, with a paid-up capital of £394,422, the
average dividend was well under 6 per cent. The Trus-
tees, Executors, and Agency Co., of Melb., with profits
greater than any of the others, paid its shareholders only
5 per cent.; and the Perpetual (Melb.) paid only 2£ per
cent. The Union (Melb.), whose accounts to Feb. 28,
1903, were put under the glass the other day (8., April 11),
made a profit of £2848, less than enough to pay
4 per cent. ; and it was pretty clear that most of that
was made out of the investment of its own funds.
Allowing 3£ per cent, as fair average earnings for
their capital, the difference between that and the
sum actually distributed by the co.’s represents the
profit on the trustee business ; that is, out of managing
about £30,000,000 of trust funds, they make a profit
of something between £7500 and £BOOO a year, which, as
a “ grab ” or a “steal,” would be beneath the contempt
of the mildest “ gent one ”in the country. Of course,
the “grab” or the “steal” might disappear in the ex-
penses ; but it doesn’t. The expenses of the National
Bank of Australasia, by no means the most extravagantly-
managed bank in the Commonwealth, amount to about
£l6s. 6d. per cent, of the total funds ; the A.M.P. expense
rate is Us. 7d. per cent, of its total funds ; while the
average for the trustee co.’s is about 3s. 4d.—the Trustees,
Executors, and Agency Co. (Melb.) gets down consider-
ably under that. And the trustee co. is an expert in its
business ; the private trustee often isn’t. One of the
dark mysteries of the times is how the idea got abroad
that a person knows how to administer an estate
simply because he has learned the trick of getting off
with a five shilling fine the drunk and disorderly who
deserves to be fined ten shillings.

Heddon Greta Coal Co. (N.S. W.) is still unable
to show a profit on working, though its land sales now
bring in a comfortable revenue. With a mining co. in
the development stage nothing very accurate in the way
of a statement of its position can be made ; whether the
amount it credits to development is too great or little is
all quite a matter of speculation. At present this co.
claims £5692 as an asset of this sort, and whether it is
worth twice as much as that or nothing at all, remains to
be proved. Working losses (including the amount spent
on development) have moved in the last three periods as
follows : March, 1902 (four months), £lOB6 ; Sept. 1902,
£2253; March, 1903, £B6O. As, however, land sales
last half-year brought in £1685, the nominal deficit was
reduced by £825. What the deficit really is cannot, for
the reasons stated just now, be set out with as near
an approach to accuracy as to make it worth attempting.
» * * •
East Greta Coal Co. shows a gross half-year’s
profit (to March 31) of £10,252, from which £1213 is
written off for depreciation of plant, leaving a net profit of
£9039 on a paid-up capital of £lOO,OOO. A dividend at the
rate of 10 p.c. absorbs (apparently less income-tax) £4641 ;
£3200 is written off mining and other property, and £lOOO
off loans. The nominal surplus, after payment of the
present div. and the writing off, will amount to £4240.
After payment of the £lOOO now to be applied to reduc-
tion of loan, the co. will owe, exclusive of current
accounts, £19,164, of which £9164 is on account of the
Stanford Merthyr mine purchase.
• * • •
Broken Hill Prop. (N.S.W.) has ab last decided
on erecting a plant to work the new Delprat process for
the further separation of the metals in the tailings, in
order to get out a zinc concentrate. The delay has been
mainly due to an uncertainty about the supply of “ salt-
cake,” the chemical (mainly sulphuric acid) which is used
to leach out the zinc ; the co. finds, however, that if it
cannot secure a satisfactory supply it is quite able to
make this chemical from its own at-present waste pro-
ducts. The zinc plant will be capable of handling 1009
tons a week ; and, though no large profits are looked for
at the start, it is expected that economies will be
developed before long. The cost of the new plant will be
about £4OOO. At the beginning the zinc concentrate will
be shipped to Europe ; although the co. has made such
progress in the manufacture of coke at Bellambi (N.S.W.)
that it is quite possible it will presently attempt the com-
plete local production of metallic zinc.
• • * •
Associated (W.A.), sinceits overhaul by Ledoux,
has been going in for all sorts of changes. Underground
work will be done by contract wherever possible, and by
two shifts instead of three, which will naturally mean a
reduction of surface hands. The third bore hole from the
1050 ft. level of Judd’s shaft has, like its two predecessors,
proved a duffer, and failed to find the continuation of the
rich Brown Hill-Oroya chute. Knowing ones had pre-
dicted that the fault, in the country between the two.
properties would cut it off. However, as it has already
been traced without a break for 3000 ft., it is hardly likely
that it has suddenly vanished. The Merton furnaces are
not likely to get going for another three or four months,
owing to delay in delivery; it seems that Martin, of
Gawler, S.A., has secured the sole right of manufacture.
* * • •
South Kalgurli (W. A.), after undergoing eight
years’ tribulation, is, if effect is given to a recommend-
ation of the committee of investigation, to be shut down
till a further £60,000 is forthcoming. On the Rand, the
working capital requisite for a mine is deemed to be in.
the neighborhood of £400,000, and, as a consequence, n®
money has to be taken out of revenue for exploitation or
equipment; so that, once the mill gets going, divs. are
assured. In W.A., on the other hand, a miserable £lo,ooo'
or £20,000 is provided, the consequence being a long
policy of cheese-paring and flnnicking, which generally
spells disaster. In the case of the South Kalgurli, fully
£420,000 has been squandered in eight yeais, and the
mine is no nearer paying divs. than at the start. The.
committee’s recommendation is a testimonial to Isles,
proving that he was given a task beyond human power.
In the present state of the London market, £60,000 may
take a lot of raising ; and, in any case, the mine is, and
always has been, so patchy that holders of shares will be
well advised to quit and let John Bull nurse the baby.
• • • *
Ivanhoe (W.A.) continues to make great pro-
gress under Nicholson’s management—another instance,
of the success of a shrewd and energetic business man.
The diamond drill, operating from the 1600 ft. level of the
Great Boulder, has already cut some of the lodes in the
Ivanhoe, but particulars of values are not yet published.
The profits are now so satisfactory that it has been
decided to pay quarterly divs. of ss.—equivalent to
£200,000 a year. This makes Ivanhoe shares a good
investment.
• • • •
Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) has cut the rioh.
chute at 860 ft. in Pomeroy shaft, and the average of the
assays is 4oz. When Capt. Parkes was in charge, he
calculated, after comparing notes with Capt. Rodda, of
the Associated Northern, that he would cut the chute at
700 ft., but the lode has been thrown a long way to the
east and this upset calculations. The Oroya is already
working this chute at three levels, and, as it is wide as
well as rich; the co. should maintain the present yields
for a long time. Additions to the plant are talked of.
Lake View Consols (W.A.) is still prospecting,
but the eagerness shown just after chairman Govett’a
visit has vanished, and the monthly footage has dwindled
to half. At the 1000 ft. level and, in fact, at all the lower
levels, the best values, in both lodes, are found to the
north when nearing the Perseverance boundary. The
reserves of so-called pay-ore are now estimated at 80,000
tons, or fully 50 per cent, more than a year ago, but the
grade is lower, being only 13£dwt. However, as the costs
have been much reduced, there is alleged to be a margin,
though it can’t be much. The Elmore oil-process plant is
completed, and has made its trial run, but the opening
shivoo has not yet taken place. The plant is said to have
cost double the estimate.

Paddington Consols(WA.)isor,cemorein the list
of producers, and, during the last five months, has treated
some 4000 tons for 25000 z. So far the full 40 head
have not got going, but it is expected that, when devel-
opments are further advanced, the mill will run continu-
ously. The big lode has been well opened up at the

maintains its value.

Great Fingall Consols (W.A.) annual meeting
was, according to cables, more or less a bear garden. The
issue of new shares was vetoed, but the iniquitous pur-
chase of the West Fingall was agreed to. It was pointed
out that the co. held 183,000 odd of the 200,000 shares
issued, so it practically amounted to robbing Peter to
pay Paul. All the same, the people who picked up the
remaining shares at rubbish prices and got £17,000 hard
cash for them must be shaking hands with themselves.
• • • •
Local flotation of Plant’s Day Dawn Gold
Mines (C. Towers, Q.) is still incomplete, though Towers
speculators are said to have taken over 40,000 contributing
shares. Doubtless it would go off with much more of a
bang if it were a shallower venture. A deep-sulking
proposition like this takes a lot of waiting for. Also,
when you come to think of it, so do the majority of
shallow ventures ; and even then they either don’t turn
up at all or only stay a very little while.
Ravenswood is at present exciting North Queens-
land mining circles. The steadily increasing output, and
improving value of the stone from the Grant and Sunset
mines, owned by a Bull co., has stimulated holders of
surrounding leases. The London is developing well, and
the Grand Junction recently cut the Sunset lode in its
No. 2 shaft. All these claims are on stone going up to
three and four ounces.

SOCIETY
“ QUID PRO ”
“I believe you'd do anything for money."
“ Well, money is always doing something for me."
The night that Burgess was acquitted (lie
was charged with complicity in a mail robbery)
Broken Hill held jubilee. At 6 p.m. the foreman
uttered his “not guilty.” A cheer was started
which ran quickly outside into the street and was
taken up by a hundred or more throats. A couple
of minutes, and Burgess was in the van of a
thirsty throng heading for the pub. over the way,
his arms aching from the hand-grips on either
side. Until midnight, wherever Burgess went
there went also a crowd, who drank freely at his,
their own, and anyone else’s expense. One hotel-
keeper ukased “ open house ” : he was generously
patronised. The celebration was kept up most of
the following day—then the acquitted one was
pulled off his pedestal and became once more
“Jack” Burgess.
The gentleman who sells half-crowns for a
shilling, and then proceeds to dispose of his
cigar-ash and other unconsidered trifles at a con-
siderable advance on late rates, has been having 1 a
rare picnic in Vic. country towns lately. Even
at Ballarat and Bendigo, the salesman achieved
capital results. The method of procedure is
similar to that adopted by Dowie, Torrey, and
other bounders —to work the listeners up to a state
of mental excitement until they are prepared to
part with all their worldly belongings for salva-
tion or a cigar-butt, as the case may be. Torrey,
&c., got away scathless, but someone set the law
in pursuit of the other American.
-* * * *
The ridiculous practice of arming Kanakas
in gaols with axes to chop wood nearly resulted in
serious consequences at Ingham (Q.) recently. A
KLanaka, awaiting trial, had been put to the usual
axe-work, but the sergeant, after reading the
report of the Mackay axe tragedy, decided to
take the weapon from him. Tommy Tanna,
declined to give it up. The sergeant recovered
the weapon after a great struggle, and, with the
aid of a couple of policemen, Tommy Tanna was
bumped into his cell. Later on, a tomahawk was
missed from the kitchen, and the Kanaka, on
being questioned, told where he had planted it.
“ Me want killem three fellow,” he explained.
* . • •
Westralia, where the Japanese fleet is still
fooling round, is trying to get used to the
Japanese naval cheer. First of all the bos3
officer present shrieks something that sounds like
“Bonzoi,” and then all the rest shriek three
times. The whole show is interpreted to mean
that the crowd that shrieks wishes the crowd that
suffers will live for 10,000 years.
* * * «
It was interesting to note the temperature
and pulse, so to speak, of public feeling in
Melbourne during the railway trouble. The first
day or two all was discussion, most of the
argument being on the men’s side of the case.
Then, when a strike looked imminent, a settled
gloom overhung everybody—the whole city
seemed to be awaiting the delivery of a heavy
blow and trying to furbish up some hasty protec-
tion therefrom. Turkey might be eaten up by
Russia, Edward VII. grossly insulted by Parisian
gamins, the House of Dull Landlords be wrecked
in an awful heap of blue ruin—nobody seemed to
care. The possible strike was the immediate,
dire, horrible danger that blotted out everything
else. Then the Argus told how Bent had denied
his rumored resignation and was waddling about,
grinning fatuously, and babbling of the ‘ soft
thing ’ he had on, and the public’s fear turned to
sullen rage against the ignorant, blundering
cause of the trouble. Next came the news that
Parliament was being frantically called together,
to carry the responsibility or to legalise the
Government’s illegal acts ; and the city, knowing
that Parliament was no good at driving a locomo-
tive, gnashed its teeth at the impotence of the
whole affair. Then came a stupid, miserable,
helpless waiting for whatever might happen, and
a broken-up inclination to drink whisky.
There is a mild
hammy flavor about
the humorless liter-
ature which tem-
perance apostles
take with their tea.
One of the veteran
items of this class
of publication is
the story of “An in-
digent old woman ”
—in Scotland —who
was supplied with
half a ton of coals
by “two kind
ladies,” whilst the
minister gave her
blankets.
“The grateful
recipient, a
few days after-
wards, met the
worthy pastor
—a bachelor.
‘Eh, minister,’
said the poor -old woman,
‘it’s a grand thing for the
likes o’ me to bide in this parish, for I aye say the
twa guid leddies keep me warm in the day-time
and the minister keeps me warm at nieht.’ ” It is
a very mild hammy flavor —so mild that a godless
whisky drinker wouldn’t notice it, but for those
who take nothing stronger than ginger ale the
little joke seems pleasantly suggestive, no doubt.
Teetotallers can feel quite wicked after reading a
pale-blue anecdote like that.
* * • *
Stipendiary Magistrate Payten, at Sydney
Water Police Court, has ruled that a money-
lender may break into a private house to seize
goods on which he has a bill of sale. Mr. Payten
quoted a Canadian precedent to justify his deci-
sion, but it is probably wrong. If it holds good,
then it follows that burglary by a money-lenderV
agents is lawful (the distinction between that and
breaking and entering is only one of time : what
is breaking in by day is burglary by night). And
if burglary by a usurer is lawful when the interest
is in arrears can the money-lender’s burglars arm
themselves to resist any opposition to their lawful
authority ? And should they kill anyone whose
is the responsibility ?
NO WONDER THEY PRAY FOR RAIN.
When the admiral and officers of the
Japanese warships now visiting Australia paid a
call at Kalgoorlie they; were entertained by the
warden and leading citizens, and the little brown
laundrymen and Japanese ladies of pleasure con-
trived to erect a triumphal arch of welcome. It was
a notable union of cleanliness and ungodliness. The
fact that a Japanese admiral should be enthusias-
tically welcomed by the Japanese prostitutes of
Australia is also a large, bright, glad commentary
on Bruce Smith’s impudent statement that it is
because of their virtues that Australians seek to
exclude the Jappy person. Once more, what is
B. Smith’s definition of virtue?
* * • *
The aftermath of Maoriland’s Jingo mad-
ness is the hordes of returned swaddies who hang
around the beer-tap, telling lies and waiting for
something to happen. Recently, an insane rumor
got about Wellington that the local American
consul was calling for men for a contingent to the
Philippines, and, in about half a day, the un-
fortunate man was inundated with swarms of
swaddies tendering their noble services at so much
per day. The harassed Yank was compelled to
put a denial in the press at last, so mighty did
the flood of offers become; and in telling the noble
ones that they weren’t wanted, he got off this
beautiful snub: “If we did (want a contingent),
we have men enough of our own without going to
a foreign country to get them.”. Expect to see
soon in M.L.’s front window this sign : “ Swaddies
for hire within in any quantities. Cause or just-
ness of any war no object, so long as the pay is
good. Whilst engaged, the hired will cut their
grandmothers’ throats without extra charge, if
needed.” What a breed of bowelless, mercenary
ruffians Bun Tuck’s foolishness has to- answer for !
• • * *
Victoria’s Bent Government is full of
bright ideas. One of them is to save the penny
postage on the letters of the police department by
making the police carry them. A letter on police
business is taken by the first constable to the
limits of his beat, and handed by him to the
“ cop ” on the next beat, and so it passes from one
policeman to another till the tenth or twentieth
bobby delivers it. Of course Policeman 11. may
not happen to connect with Policeman 111., who
should meet him, but who may be away fighting a
violent drunk at the far end of his beat, and
through these breaks in the chain of circumstance
the letter that the post-office would deliver in
three hours may not reach its destination in three
days. And the result of all this delay and worry
is that Victoria pays Id. less to the post-office
and gets back Id. less at the division of the
Federal surplus. The Bent Government actually
can’t see a small thing like that. Already letters
are going astray, because the policeman who is
carrying a valuable communication often has to
stop and combat a gang of hoodlums, and when
he emerges from the fray the letter and some
fragments of his uniform have probably blown
away down the street, and fallen into Fitzgibbon’s
sewer.

The lines and trams of one Adelaide and
suburban tram co. are in such a condition that
when the people on the top floor dance about a
little they can always shake the vehicleoff thetrack.
When this happens (and it does frequently) the
driver swears a bit and then drives on till he
casually strikes the line, perhaps a couple of
hundred yards further on. It doesn’t make so
very much difference to the passengers whether
the car is off or on the line though. It bumps
just about the same.
Pathetic home-made sign on a Melbourne
suburban cottage: “Work urgently wanted
Man, active, garden, anything. Wife, wash
iron, sew. Three young children.”
A Hobart (Tas.) parson has made a con-
siderable row because the local Blind Institution
recently accepted £5 of “Tattersall” Adams’
‘ ‘ ill-gotten wealth ” —as the phrase always goes.
What a fuss the parsons make about the last
owner of a casual bit of gold ! The average
sovereign that dates back ten or twenty
years has been in so much bad company and
has gone on so many villainous errands that
it isn’t safe to begin asking about its Past.
It was last in the possession of “Tattersall”;
and if it is an old sovereign probably a bishop,
two murderers, a philanthropist, six swindlers, a
bigamist, several ladies of loose character, many
men of tight character, one saint, also publicans,
usurers, pressmen, Chinamen, liars, hangmen,
preachers and atheists—all had it before him. It
was probably the ill-gotten wealth of twenty men,
and the hard-earned wealth of forty, and the
blessed charity of this person, and the blood-
money of that one, and the price of several
women’s shame, and the benefaction which saved
some hungry woman from shame, long before
“ Tatt.” heard of it. And it will be all these
things again whether the parson takes it or not.
Probably two days after the parson has spent it
in the good cause it will have degenerated into a
fan-tan establishment.

HAD TOMMY HELD HIS TONGUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
V.
Whilst Tommy talked, a cloud of dust
Enveloped all the land ;
The brawny engine-drivers cuss’t
The State that baked their scanty crust,
And few asked what was fair or just
In this or that demand.
The hope of peace it perished fast,
Its passing-bell was rung ;
But sense might have prevailed at last
Had Tommy held his tongue.
Had Tommy Bent, who mischief meant,
The Bashan bull of Parliament,
Been sat upon, and made content
To hold his angry Tongue.
’Tis said that toilers for the State
Should never strike the boss;
That civil service wrongs, if great,
Are surely righted, soon or late ;
That comfort comes to them who wait
And bear their heavy cross.
Which may be true ; I cannot tell,
My song is simply sung
To state that all might have been well
Had Tommy held his Tongue.
Had burly, surly Tommy Bent,
The Bashan bull of Parliament,
Been set upon and made content
To hold his bitter Tongue.
Why did the busy, burly B.
These engine-drivers vex ?
Why did he glare at them, and me,
As though a Sultan proud were he,
And we but slaves on bended knee,
With bow-strings round our necks?
The old man seems no wiser now
Than when his paunch was young.
Let’s chuck him out and stop the row
Which comes from Tommy’s Tongue.
Comrades ! buck up with one consent,
Ere yet our righteous rage is spent,
And hurl him forth from Parliament,
This tyrant with the Tongue !
E.F
GLITTER CLATTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Clitter clatter, clitter clatter,
See she patters down the street,
Making quite a little matter
Of the lace about her feet.
How the johnnies chatter at her
As they hear the high-heeled but.
Clitter clatter, clitter clatter,
Be it bright, or wet with sleet;
I grow mad as any hatter,
On my heart have mercy, sweet.
Ev’ry time you clitter clatter,
It is shattered ’neath your feet."
Clitter clatter, clitter clatter
(What a petticoated treat).
“ Sir,” she says, “ I pray you flatter
Not yourself more than is meet.
Other hearts there are, and fatter,
That I flatten ’neath my feet.”
VIC.
HUGH R. M'O[?].
LYNETTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Onward, through hours of red roses and wine,
Over a road smooth and shining as jet;
On with your passion-warm hand clasped in mine,
Swinging to red mists of sunset, Lynette.
Heads in the Paradise made of our sin
(So the world called it when first our lips met),
World by a mirage of faith taken in,
Come, let us meet the red sunset, Lynette.
Look at them climbing yon boulder-strewn peaks-
Ants on the mountains a-search for a God—
Making of white days brown, wearisome weeks,
Come let us trip where their tired feet have trod.
Down let us fly on the wings of the wind,
Swing to the music our heart-beats have set.
Clasped close together. The sin that we sinne
Was the ladder to Paradise, little Lynette.

Premier Seddon gave vent to an Eiffel
Tower screech lately re the inordinately large
number of stowaways landing every week in M.L.
from Australia. But it’s not the stowaways that
the big man is really troubled about; it’s the
hundreds who are flocking over (paying their
passages) as the result of his own boasting that
Pig Island is the Paradise of the Southern Hemi-
sphere. With prophetic eye Seddon sees that in
the near henceforth, when harvesting is over and
the winter sets in, there will be a hoarse cry from
the unemployed. Maoriland has carried a heavy
load of immigration of late, and now there are
fears that -the rush to get to the land of heavy
Protection and “disastroussocialistic legislation”
will be too sudden altogether.

The pile of offices called the “ Olderfleet,”
in Collins-st., Melbourne, built in boom time by
Mr. P. K. M'Caughan, at a cost of something like
£BO,OOO, was sold last week at about one-quarter
that sum, the City Mutual Life Assurance Society
being the purchaser. In the early 40’s, on this spot,
stood a wattle-and-dab building, kept by one
James Smith, and known as the Lamb Inn. It
was a roaring pub., frequented by stockmen and
shearers on their periodical “busts.” Next, or
very near, lived Mrs. Batman, widow of John
Batman, who died before he could realise on his
enterprise in Port Phillip ; and adjoining lived for
a time E. J. Eyre, squatter and explorer, and
afterwards Deputy-Governor of one of the islands
of Maorilanjl and Governor in Jamaica^
“ T.D.” : The new Melbourne eight-hours
memorial, a long, slender, granite column with a
glittering gilt device in the shape of three B’s on
top, is yet another unsightly testimony to the
generally inartistic spirit of people hereabouts.
The pedestal is almost the only good thing about
it. Said pedestal is said to have mopped up
nearly the whole of the subscribed £2OOO. The
thing on top is worth probably about (3^d.
Perth lawyers struck a fat oyster lately,
and hadn’t got to the shell when the mail left.
The case concerns the division of a Groper estate,
which was a very small thing, not worth fighting
about 15 years ago, when the will was made, but
worth many tens of thousands to-day. Three
brothers Clune—Matthew, John and Jeremiah
owned the place. Matthew fell out of a window
and landed on his head, doing it so much damage
—this is one yarn—that he afterwards pulled out
his whiskers and made a will in which he left
most of his interest to his brothers John and
Jeremiah, and very little to his children. The
brothers’ yarn is that he fell on his hip and not his
head, and that, if he pulled out his whiskers, he
did it out of pure love of ornament; also that, as
far as making a will was concerned, he was as
good as new. When it was suggested that
Matthew was treated as the fool of the family
and made to do the washing up, while his
children were regarded as encumbrances, Jere-
miah Clune pointed with triumph to the fact that
his own eldest daughter did all the “ killing ” for
the Clune community, so Matthew’s encumbrances
couldn’t complain if they had to work also. The
case was at this stage when the mail left, but
telegraphic advices show that the tide of battle
finally went against John and Jeremiah, so now,
presumably, the lawyers are busy making up the
bills of costs. But the case of Matthew, John
and Jeremiah makes a picture which Groper-
dom ought to frame as a reminder of old times —
the families of Matthew, John and Jeremiah
huddling together, eldest daughters killing the
mutton, fathers occasionally having a brotherly
fight and knocking pieces off one another,
and the alleged idiotic father who was
made to wash up. Also the quarrel over the
division of an estate which isn’t valuable through
anything that Matthew, John or Jeremiah did,
ought to be remembered as a pretty good
argument for a stiff land tax. Apparently no
violently progressive conduct on the part of
Matthew, John and Jeremiah was likely to
improve the estate materially.
* * * •
Rev. W. E. Brownlow, writing from New
Guinea to the pious Spectator, tells of a remark-
able conversion:
We meet with many remarkable cases of. conversion,
but one of the strangest I have ever met with came under
my notice on Sunday last. A man and his wife came to
me before service asking me to talk to them first and they
would join class. The man said he wished to be saved
from becoming angry with a man who wished to steal his
wife. “If I let my anger grow I shall spear him.” The
wife said she wished to join class to help her husband and
not take any notice of what the other man might say.
Apparently the wife wished to join the class so as
to help her husband not to be angry with the
other fellow. This is very comforting for the
other fellow, but what does the husband get out
of it, except the joy of putting his cocoa-nut in
the plate ?

“ Ses.” : Touching those surf sensations
at Manly, and the cry for lifeboats, danger
signals, etc. There is one man among the early
morning frequenters of the beach who goes out
beyond the breakers with a knife between his
teeth, or in his hand,
“looking for a shark.”
Some fine morning he’ll
find one.
At Adelaide, a few days ago, an ex-cohen-
tinger obtained a divorce from his wife on the
ground that she had sought consolation in the arms
of another fellow whilst he (the husband) was
looking for glory in S’Africa. How misleading
are the sentimental pictures that artists paint on
the subject of the lone matron, whose man is away
at the war ! “The Soldier’s Wife,” “A Letter
from the Front,” “News at Last”—what droll
satires on the frequent reality ! Wanted a cynical
work entitled “ The Lieutenant’s Locum Tenens,”
showing the lady on the knee of a nice unwarlike
young man, reading aloud a letter from the dear
one whose image lives in her memory—with
the help of another man to recall it. A huge
envelope lying on the floor and postmarked
Jewhannesburg, would serve to locate the where-
abouts of the absentee.

“C.P.” : Saw a guard slip on the foot-
board of the van one evening lately, as I was
riding on a Melbourne suburban line. It was
a narrow escape from serious accident. Out of
curiosity I asked him what provision would be
made for him by the department if injured.
“ Provision !” he laughed. “Why, my wages’d
be stopped from the minute I struck the ground.”
Nice Christian organisation! It may not be
generally known, however, that in cases of ship-
wreck wages stop the moment a seaman leaves the
deck. He drowns in his own time—not in the
shipowner’s.

“Bon” contributes his scrap to the debate
about people who bathe in the altogether :
I don’t know much of the figure feminine when bathing,
nor, judging by its appearance when suffering the torture
imposed by its conventional garments, have I any violent
desire to increase my scanty knowledge. But —and this
is another story—l once had the glory of gazing with the
gulls at the solemn spectacle of a full-blown archbishop,
“ bobbing up and down like this,” in the St. Hilda baths.
While taking the sun in a state of nature at the end of
a springboard, I noticed the entrance of a solemn pro-
cession in black. A dozen or so boxes had been booked,
and these were taken possession of by all save the
“star,” whose magnitude was too great for any box
to contain him. I lazily watched the crowd disrobe
from the vantage ground of the springboard. At this
stage of the proceedings a lay-brother, clad in a rosary
and a fine purple mole, emerged from his box and ordered
me to move myself to the other side of the baths. Re-
fusing to obey orders, I was told that fcrce would be
employed. Told the party with the mole that he would
have to walk the plank first, an invitation which was de-
clined on the paltry plea that he couldn’t swim. Finally,
I took a courteous header and floated low on the weather
bow, awaiting developments. They came. So did his
Grace, carefully hidden from the eye profane, from chin
to toes, in a nice new nightie. Followed by the First Lord
of the Life Belts, he advanced to the staging, did
a giddy little cake-walk thereon, and then 2401b5.
of Archbishop struck the startled deep with a smack.
In his Lucifer-like fall the sacred swimmer struck an
affrighted jellyfish amidships, coming up again with its
shattered remains crowning his head like a wreath of
nautilus on the brows of Neptune. But what I, the vic-
tim, whose head was violently bumped on the bottom
through the tidal wave which arose when one-tenth of a
ton of archbishop struck the water —what I want to know
is: Can I sue the Church, or was it an act of Providence
as per shipping charter !

Charters Towers (N.Q.) is another proof
of the truism that voters get the representatives
they deserve. Though the district is fever stricken,
a vote taken on the proposal to abolish the noxious
cesspits resulted in reform getting beaten by 339 to
154 votes. About the same time the municipal
detector of smells had to give the local Council
notice of a nuisance in its own back-yard under
the windows of its own Board-room.
* * * *
There were a few days lately when it
wasn’t certain who was Acting-Governor of
Queensland. Lieut.-Gov. Griffith came south
to witness the investiture circus, and Governor
Chermside followed him, leaving with Hugh
Nelson a commission to act “in my temporary
absence.” Griffith beat Chermside on the home
journey; and then who was Acting-Governor—
Griffith or Nelson ? Nelson had a commission to
act while Chermside was away, and Chermside
was still away; also Griffith has a dormant
commission to act while any Governor is away,
and the Governor was away. There was plenty
of scope for a lovely row with two fighters in the
case; but Hugh Nelson is much too tired a
person to fight over anything, and so there
wasn’t any row, and, for all it mattered, there
needn’t have been any Acting-Governor at all.
* * » *
Queensland ex-Governor Lamington, the
rummiest noble of them all, set the fashion up
North with grey soft-felt hats. Now Chermside
is endeavoring to make the old Hinglish hard,
grey “ nail-keg ” the proper head-gear. He wears
it at all functions with a devotion worthy of a
better “tile.” It was a solemn sight at the recent
sod-turning of RockhamptomGladstone Railway
to see his Nibs, coat off, sleeves turned up, and a
14-inch grey plug hat perched insecurely over a
rubicund face, wheeling the initial barrow-load up
a wobbly plank.

“Zip”: Re the much-vexed question of
feminine modesty. A mother of six confe s. j d
recently to me her inability to explain why, when
taking a bath in her bedroom, she invariably
covers the looking-glass, and any photos cf male
individuals that may be hanging about the wall.
Said she had been putting the towel over the
mirror since girlhood, but had only acquired the
photo.-smothering habit since marriage. Also,
know a Potts Point boarding-house keeper who
has her bath in her bedroom because she can’t
bring herself to disrobe in the bathroom utilised
by the male lodgers.
* • • •
The suggestion to the Australian public
that they should find Hector Macdonald guilty of
nameless offences because, when in the Common-
wealth, he avoided the “society ” crowd is grossly
unfair. Knowing what we do of the infernal
snobbery of the British army and of the sort of
“society” it favors, can there be any wonder
that the promoted ranker, the man who had made
his way by sheer grit and courage and brains—not
by the cut of his stays, or his skill at bridge, or his
friendship for some magnate’s wife—shunned it V
Macdonald’s existence —outside of his work—was
made accursed by the snobbishness of “ society,”,
and he avoided it, probably, for no other motive
than that which makes a man avoid the company
of bad drains or of unwholesome dogs.
* * * *
The northern trip of N.S. W. State Gover-
nor in the Victoria is a beautiful example of
economy as practised by the Owe’-See Govt. It
was at the beginning of April that the papers
announced that the costly Victoria had left the
moorings where it had been lying idle for some
months, and was again going into commission.
After four weeks’ waiting for orders the vessel
finally started with Gov. Rawson, Premier See,
and a scratch lot of politicians for a ten days’
trip, the actual time at sea being about five days.
Once ready for use, the Victoria simply eats
money, and has to be stoked with bank-notes, and
it must have run the general taxpayer into a pon -
derous bill for this ten days’ burst. And the
party extracted so much misery out of that tem-
pestuous trip, and its members were thrown out
of bunk on their heads so frequently, that they
would almost as soon have been dropped overboard
to end their sufferings.
ADRIFT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With drifts of bloom on the hills,
And drifts of clouds and snow ;
And Autumn leaves, and the rills,
And the Ocean’s ceaseless flow,
Old Earth was swung into space
In the whirl of wind and star ;
The sunlight drifts o’er her face,
And the moonlight follows afar.
It was thus your young love came,
And passed through my heart on its way ;
But as flame is drawn after flame,
My soul after yours must stray.
And ever amid the great wheel
Of the stars and the winds and the years,
Together our spirits shall steal
Through the drifts of smiles and tears.
N.B.W.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.
