A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 10, 1903.
My Dear Arinl,—
Considerable rain, some grey impressive days
and then dazzling-bright winter sunshine have
been our happy lot in Sydney lately. The grey
weather was lovely—-wraith-like spirals of mist in
the harbor bays, like ghosts on their way to
heaven, recalled the Biblical “I will call my
people out of the depths of the sea.” The ash -
colored autumn sea frescoed with slaty, inverse
shadows seemed to sulk until the rain-drops
caused it to break into dimples—millions of
dimples. One scarcely liked to see the busy-bee
ferry steamers fly over that smiling surface and
even for an instant scar it smooth.
Autumn tints make the golden age of some of
our foliage, although our Sydney leaves haven’t
the reputation of “turning” to any extent. But for
living gold and burning red the trees and shrubs
around the Blue Mountain townships beat the
world. Blackheath in autumn glows like an opal
with color. From these spots come the picked
flowers and foliage (picked in two senses) that you
see in the florists’ windows, like noble displays
of gold and burnished copper and gems.
Chrysanthemums hold the floral sway just now.
Incidentally: I often wonder why no proud
mother of a golden-haired child has ever named
him Chrysosanthus. Perhaps a sense of what the
child would have to endure amongst the bad plain
Bills and Jacks at .school on account of such a
name restrains her Once, when I was about 18
months or two years old, I wrote a story called
‘‘Chrysosanthus.” The hero was a fairy who
lived in the sunset, and he loved another fairy
who abode in the sunrise, and they used to slide
their love letters from east to west and vice versa
on a single hair of Golden Hair’s crop. It was a
beautiful story, and I very nearly cut my wisdom
teeth with grief, when an old crocodile of an
editor returned it.
PREMIER IRVINE, OF VICTORIA.
Bridge (the game) is settling down in Sydney
for the winter, and promises to flourish like the
sweet briar, or the green bay tree, or Jonah’s
gourd. But bridge is not likely to get such
a hold in an out-door climate as it has, for
instance, in New York. An Argonaut woman
writer says that in New York city there are but
two topics which intensely interest society
women, and they are bridge and the reduction
of weight. No matter what kind of a bore or
a dunce you may be otherwise, you are sure of a
hearing if you are a good bridge player or if you
have a recipe that will cause fat women to lose
30 pounds in three months. Every woman X
meet in Sydney seems to be able to play a good
game of bridge, poker, or whist. Most women
decline to play for anything but the game,
although I know a family of daughters who are
quite proud of the fact that their bridge-playing
has relieved '‘poor pa” of the drain of their
dress allowance. During the current fortnight
they have been “bridging” every night in order
to get gowns for the Fresh Air League ball, and
thus assist sweet charity.
A young woman, who doesn’t know one card
from another, tells me that she was lately one of a
dinner-party of five, and, after the broiled butter-
fly meal, the host and three women guests sat
down to bridge. The minor fifth sat by, declaring
she would learn the game in half-an-hour. At
the close of the first game she decided to learn a
foreign language instead. The minor fifth felt
herself a nuisance, wandered all over the room
pawing all the ornaments, making comments on
the pictures, and finally settling down at her
host’s writing-table to scribble verses on the
blotting pad. She now concludes that a woman
should play bridge, or stay at home and study the
theory and practice of stocking-darning.
The blue ball gown which Miss Ethel Mollison
wears in second act of “Are You a Mason?”
always causes the wearer to be put under the
lenses of the women’s opera glasses. A long flight
of silver swallows adorns the front from left to
right—full-grown swallows and baby birds and
iust-betweens, and all beautifully poised. The
blue of the background is a trifle hard, but the
strong contrast makes the gown an effective one.
A grey tucked chiffon overlaid with lace orna-
ments is “real elegant.” Miss Mollison wears it
whilst she sits fancy-working. There appears to
be some honest sewing, glorified embroidery and
art needle work done during the progress of the
play, for in two acts the wormwood mother and
her three sweet offspring sit, clad in glorious
gowns, plying the needle and silk. Canadian
Ethel’s big, red, upstanding hair-bow, worn on the
left front, has caught on as a fashionlet. I saw a
whole theatre party of seven women wearing
similar erections in different colored velvets.
The stage mother of these birds of paradise,
Miss Munroe, has the woman’s part of “ Are You
a Mason ? ” As a combination of strict mother,
crab-apple mother-in-law and suspicious, rasping
wife she’s as fine and frightsome as a bag of
wasps. One of Miss Munroe’s frocks is an un-
common shade of red, a glowing soft dark red,
like a rose on fire, if roses are ever on fire.
A country doctor came to town recently,
sickening for typhoid, and was put into a private
hospital. As his illness promised to be a pro-
tracted one, his wife, who had been married a
year, wired to the coachman a notice that his
services would not be required after a certain
date. Promptly he wrote back, saying that he
had been with the doctor longer than she had, and
that he would not take notice from her, a mere
newcomer.
A prettily-dressed, pleasant young woman
called on the Sydney sub-manager of a certain
shipping company, and asked the fare of her hus-
band’s oorpse to Tasmania. Said corpse desired
to be buried there and nowhere else. The official
gave the information, and inquired if the body
would travel by the first steamer leaving for
Tassy. “ Oh, no ! ” was the cheerful reply, “he
isn’t ready yet. ” Then she smiled herself out of
the staggered official’s presence. After the lapse
of a couple of weeks the lady again materialised
in the manager’s den and announced, “ He’s dead
now ”; and proceeded to discuss the transporta-
tion of “it.” In reply to the manager’s query if
she would travel along in the same vessel, the
ministering angel answered, “No ; it would be so
uncomfortable for both of us.”
A feature in the “Situations Wanted” by
domestic servants these days is that the adver-
tiser is a “country girl.” The employer woman
has pounced upon that “ country girl ” so often,
and so often found that she knows no more of the
country than she can learn in a day’s holiday in
the bush about Mosman, or the North Shore line,
that the phrase is ceasing to allure. One missus
(she is on a charitable committee) engaged a
“country girl” who accounted for her lack of
references by the statement that she was only a
week from Jamberoo, or some such commercial
centre, and didn’t know her way about Sydney,
much less anybody in it. The damsel was
en £ a ged and turned out hopelessly inefficient,
but good-tempered and honest. One day
her mistress took her along when she went to
inquire into the claim for charitable assistance of
a certain family in Redfern. “The kind o’
families you help,” remarked the damsel en route,
“ makes me laff.” She reeled off a list of the
lately assisted persons, proved them quite un-
worthy, and said she ought to know, as she’d lived
in Redfern all her life and knew these families
well. Challenged about the Jamberoo fable, she
replied that “if lydies would prefer country girls
then lydies would get them.”

The latest thing in Paris and London for
drawing-room use is the sandal for women.
This fashion needs a special class of hose,
made with a compartment for each toe, and
spangled oer with seed pearls, rubies, and quaint
and cunningly wrought flowers, beetles, and tiny
goblins, of amethyst, opal and chrysoprase. The
sandals are dainty artistic creations of all manner
of designs. There is a special system of massage
for the feet, by which an improved circulation is
induced so that the warm pink glow of the flesh
shines faintly and beautifully through the trans-
parent hose.
Hats are delicious,” says a S.M. H. London

Two songs by Claire Newton are to hand. Their
attractive titles are “ I Told My Love to the
Roses, and “Hush ! ” The latter is an unusually
sweet bit of melody, but both deserve introduc-
tions to every harmonious household.
When Australians read that the King has dined
with Mr. Moses Dynamo or Sir Thomas Sham-
rock, they are prone to take the intimation as
proof that the hosts are in the highest society in

the land, or that the kingly browse has at once
raised them to that pinnacle. But then Edward
himself is not in the society. There is a square of

could break to admit the hosts mentioned.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Women suburbanites got their strike scare
when they learnt evening trains were off. Satur-
day morning saw hundreds, hanging on to dress
baskets, making for the homes of relatives and
friends within walking distance of the city. Other
feminine souls, with the remembrance of neces-
sary tooth-brushes and fringe-nets hot on their
souls, made a frantic rush to the shops where dis-
tracted counter hands served out safety-pins for
hose suspenders and sewing silks for thimbles.
The strike time-table in mind, and the haunting
thought of the desperate station struggle plaguing
the soul, shop assistants let decorum go hang and
kept their affrighted eyes on the clock. Chemists
were rushed by the wealthy for nerve tonics.
“A man, who has spent several years on far-out
Westralian gold-fields, gives his views on women
(not much more than girls, most of them)—
English, Australian and American, respectively—
whom he has met camping with their husbands,
latter, as a rule, being surveyors, engineers,
mining.managers or syndicators. The English
bride, says my informant (a German, by the way),
when she arrives on the field, knows less than a
baby how to keep a camp cosy. Left on her own,
she drags a huge tree from her wood-pile, boosts
its roots into her open fire-place, and jumps over
the trunk whenever she crosses the tent. A far-
seeing housewife would make her husband chop
firewood before he left home. Jane Bull, never-
theless, is as plucky as possible, and second to none
in personal refinement, innate daintiness and
unconscious ‘good style.’ The American girl is
a marvellous creature—an all-round paragon—
pretty, cultured, musical, capable, and common-
sensical all in one. She beats the English Rose
hollow in many ways, but the Briton’s posy scores
in feminine ‘finish.’ La Belle Americaine, says
Mein Herr, likes to shoo the men round in a
crisp, good-humored way, and all men don’t take
kindly to being ‘ bossed. ’ From a Teutonic point
of view, Mrs. Australia inclines more to the
Yankee type, so far as practical utility is con-
cerned. She is handsome, showy, capable, but
less cultured and intellectual than Auntie Jona-
than, and not so dainty and dignified as the pick
of British belles.
“The ‘Princess May Pavilion’ at Melb.
Children’s Hospital was formally christened and
opened last week. In a tew years time people
will be wondering as to the identity of this for-
gotten Princess May whose name became attached
to a public charity in 1903. Who was she ? The
daughter of the Duke and Duchess of Teck, and
the fiancee of a fleeting Duke of Clarence;
subsequently she married Clarence’s brother
and changed her name to Duchess of York, and
then changed it again to Princess of Wales.
Princess May belongs to the buried past. Father,
mother, husband-elect, and the Queen who sanc-
tioned the unhappy engagement—all are now
dead.
“The army of Yank she-scribblers increases
alarmingly. Every mag. has its variety of psycho-
logical and historic stories under feminine pen-
names. Yet get the extremes from profound
thought to c-risp salad talks in these columns.
Where, a few years ago, there was one woman
writer to every ten men writers, there are now
apparently twenty women of printable merit to
every two of the other sex.
“English visitors passing this way say that
Miss Maud Milton, the Helena of ‘ Midsummer
Night’s Dream,’ is amazingly like Miss Ellen
Terry.
“There’s a fierce war going on between Scotch
mealy materials and Donegal tweeds. The pur-
chase made by an impressionable female depends
upon the countryman who serves her. Had an
experience myself the other day in a dress deal.
The brogue of the salesman invested the Donegal
flake with all the charms imaginable. Asked to
be shown the Scotch oatmeal or thistle-down
tweeds, he turned with unutterable scorn to root
them out of a dark corner. Then he showed them
begrudgingly, never troubling to switch out yards
and flash the beauties of the fabric in the full
light streaming in at the door. Of course,
Donegal won the day, under those conditions.

mechanical pianoon hisown premises to familiarise
his two children with ‘the works of the great
tone-poets.’ The professor and his wife are both
pianists themselves, but time does not permit
them to pour as much melody into the youngsters
as they would like, hence the recourse to an auto-
matic substitute. Mrs. Peterson, who is the
daughter of a newspaper editor in Buda-Pesth,
claims to be ‘ Hungarian and Magyar to the core.’
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A recent photo shows John Burns, M.P.,
the Labor leader, as quite white in hair and
beard.
Dr. O’Connor, consecrated Coadjutor
Bishop of Artnidale (N.S.W.), makes the 28th
R.C. Bishop in Australasia. The C. of E. has
had 24 bishops.
<l X.O. : Was Kingston ever a telegraph
operator? He talks in telegrams. “Australia
White. Kanaka must go. Said it; will stick to
it, ” How like a telegram.
N.S.W. Attorney-General B. R. Wise had
a most enthusiastic reception from the audience
at the Kingston meeting—a fact which the daily
newspapers carefully suppressed.
Henry Septimus Badgery, of the People’s
Reform League, was once Minister for Works
for 24 days, in Dibbs’s shortest-lived administra-
tion. He represented successively Maitland and
Monaro.
Premier Seddon’s tenth anniversary as
Premier recalls his maiden speech in the House
on Oct. 2, 1879. “I rise to say a few words,”
said the new member, and he then proceeded to
fill 19 columns of Hansard.
George Scarfe, the bachelor hardware
merchant and miser of S.A., who has no poor
relatives, and who died recently at the age of 76
years worth £750,000, did not leave a single penny
to the employees who helped him to bujjd up his
immense business.
One Hogan, a Caltowie (S.A.) laborer,
was choked recently by a piece of saveloy. The
doctor found a large lump of meat with a piece of
string attached in the dead man’s throat. The
careless sausage-maker who doesn’t take the string
off the dog should be prosecuted.
Malcolm M’Eacharn, of Melbourne, nas
been appointed local consul for Japan ; it entitles
him to wear much gorgeous ni 1 linery, and pro-
bably—to his order of intellect—is a solid consola-
tion for having been declared impossible for the
Australian High Commissionership.
The father of Caswell, P.M., now retiring
from N.S.W. public service, was commander in
the Royal navy, and
conducted operations
against Napoleon I.
He also fought in the
war of American in-
dependence. He ar-
rived in Sydney with
his family in 1830.
The retiring Caswell
entered the public ser-
vice as C.P.S. in 1847,
and has thus put in 56
years toiling behind
his bleeding country’s
inkpot.
The Neotsfield,
belonging to “ Dan-
gars’,” has arrived in
London after a voyage
of 99 days. In these
days of ocean grey-.
hounds and ocean
tramps, this item per
cable is interesting.
The Dangars are the only people In
Australia who load their own ships,
with their own wool and produce. The
original Danger was born in 1798 at
Cornwall (Eng.), the son of a small
farmer. In 1821 he came to New South
Wales and got employment as as-
sistant Government surveyor, the
Hunter being his district. In 1826
he took up 700 acres at Neotsfield,
near Singleton, the commencement
of his big estate, jin 1830 he was
working under Sir E. Parry, the
Arctic navigator, when that worthy was manager
for the A. A. Company. Later on Henry Dangar
took up the Myall Creek property, which is
associated with a horrible massacre of blacks in
the Thirties, for that crime seven men were hanged
in the gaol in Lower George street.
Frugal, cautious and canny are the Scotch.
Squatter A. B. Murray, who died worth £107,000,
in Adelaide the other day at the age of 88 years,
left to Mrs. Murray an annuity of £250 so long as
she remained a widow.
Two reputable Sydney citizens appealed
to the Full Court last week to determine a ques-
tion of interest in the settlement of sosee land
accounts. The amount of the claim was l^d. —a
fact which caused the Court, after a five hours’
argument, to make some unusually frank observa-
tions to the litigants.

graphs by the Little Duke can now estimate their
value to a farthing. At the sale of journal let
Blowitz’s effects in Paris autograph photographs
of the present King—signed when he was Prince

Vic. Premier Irvine is one of the most
difficult of public speakers for a reporter to
“ take.” Hedging the most simple and direct
statements with all sorts of verbiage and qualifi-
cations, it is necessary to catch every word to
grasp his meaning; and he speaks in a small,
throaty voice which becomes almost inaudible at
about every tenth word.
Died in Adelaide Mrs. F D. Beaeb, L ieut.
Gov. Way’s sister. Adelaide “society” didn't
know much of her, though she was pretty well
known in other circles as an old lady of a most
charitable disposition. “Society” knew her
husband better—he used to cater for most of its
ehivoos. “ Now which shall it be, caterer or
guest, one but not both? ” little Way, it is said,
used to put it to his brother-in-law when a
“ function ” was nearing ; and Beach always had
horse-sense enough to choose to be caterer.
Two old Australian families come together
in the marriage of the grandniece of Hamilton
Hume and a son of the Rev. Geikie. Everybody
knew the Rev. Geikie. Probably he was better
known in Canada than in N.S.W. lie was
especially marked in N.S.W. for his habit of in-
sisting that Members of Parliament ought to have
some education. Hamilton Hume, a native of
Parramatta, printed himself over a large part of,
Australia. Statues have beer built to him, and
rivers and townships called after him. He was
associated with Oxley, Sturt, and other notables
in early Australian developments.
A very ancient R.C. cleric is Archpriest
Sheehy, one of the very few remaining who
receive State aid. The archpriest is a Sydney
native, his father having been clerk at St. Mary’s
in the long ago. Half-a-century ago he was
appointed Assistant-Bishop to Archbishop
Polding, under the title of Bishop of
Bethesda. When the Papal Bull for the
consecration arrived Father Sheehy refused to
accept the honor, as some of his co-religionists
sneered at the humble position of his father.
In early life he was a teacher at old St.
Mary’s seminary, where he had as col-
leagues the late William Dolman of
the Freeman, and William Francis
Xavier Bailey, the noted Bishop of the
Free Church of England, whose law
suit with Sydney Morning Herald
was a sensation 40 years ago.
The oldest R.C. cleric in
or about Melbourne is Dean
O’Hea, now known as Monsig-
nor. The “ Dane ” was born
on Ist April, 1814. He was
ordained in Rome, and returned
to Ireland in 1840. He arrived
in Melbourne, in 1852, in the
ship Kohinoor, having as fellow
Eassengers Dr. Fitzpatrick, Dr.
hields (afterwards Bishop of
Adelaide), and Dean Hayes, all of
whom he has survived. The
Dean’s long life was very nearly
cut short in 1854. With Mr. Bar-
row, the first superintendent of
Pentridge Stockade, he went out
in an open boat to the Golden Age
in Hobson’s Bay, to bid farewell to
Gov. La Trobe, who was going to
England. The boat was upset and
Mr. Barrow drowned, but the Dean
swam to St. Kilda, a mile and a
half away.
An old Maoriland settler
waxed reminiscent lately :
I knew Dick Seddon before ever he dreamed of becom.
ing Premier or a Member of Parliament. In those days he
possessed a horse, which, while being shod, would always
lie down, and I have seen Dick, who used to bring the
horse to be shod, stoop, and resting his hands on his
knees, place himself under the horse's stomach, then
h ild up the brute until the last nail was driven in his
shoes.

PLUNDER.
The Sultan of Johore (just arrived in Australia) has
golden false teeth set with diamonds.
A burglarious possibility with the aid of
dentists’ forceps.
An old identity died, last week, at New-
town, Sydney: Mrs. James Bligh Johnstone,
aged 93, widow of the gentleman of that name
who resided at Crow’s Nest, Windsor. She was
the daughter of Captain Charles Waldron, an old-
time military officer, who settled at Illawarra,
and who died, in 1834, from injuries received in a
scuffle with two female assigned servants.
The late J. T. Toohey once presided at a
meetingin Sydney Town Hall to get up a testimonial
to Cardinal Moran on his departure for Rome.
In exhorting the audience to subscribe, the chair-
man said any member of the committee would
receive subscriptions, adding “And you all know
where the brewery is. ” And the audience tried to
look as if it didn’t, and failed badly.
Judge Hartley Williams, of Victoria, who
retires this month, mounted the judicial throne at
the early age of 38. His first case was the trial of
a golden-haired Flossie who had shown her
opinion of a faithless Johnny by putting a bullet
into him. The youthful Judge caused much excit i
ment by weeping copiously as he pronounced sen-
tence on the peroxided one. He grew out of the
emotional phase later.
Thos. Anderson, pastoralist, who recently
died at Westernport (Vic.), aged 85, had resided
on the shores of that bay since ’56, and in the
State since ’49. His brother Samuel, who died
in ’63, pioneered the Westernport district in ’35,
discovering the Tarwin River and Anderson’s Inlet,
the latter named after him. Samuel Anderson was
a contributor to the Cornhill Magazine, and in
the early ’so’s Melbourne Argus proprietor,
Edwd. Wilson, pressed him repeatedly to join
his literary staff, bub Sam. preferred the freedom
of country life.
Vic. Chief-Justice Madden’s knowledge of
history is probably fully equalled by his Wax’s
knowledge of law. A case came before him in
the Melb. Practice Court (30/4/’O3) concerning a
heritage consisting of—
-123-448 parts of a King’s share in the New River Com-
pany. When he charter was granted one moiety of 36
parts was held by “incorporated adventurers,” the other
moiety of 36 parts being held by His Majesty King
James I. Subsequently persons now called King’s share-
holders became incorporated, and the moieties were sub-
divided and held by numerous persons. Being real
estate, they are subject to entail, and to trusts for
minors.
His Honor: “ This part of the affidavit seems to be
chiefly interesting as a history of the later Tudors.”
Fancy James I. as a “later Tudor” !
Obit: Alex. Fyfe, at Preston (Vic.), aged
77. He landed in Melbourne in ’4B, and shortly
after the gold discoveries visited Singapore and
brought to Geelong a number of two-storeyed cedar
houses which he re-erected on the shore of Corio
Bay, letting them at good rentals. Later on he
owned a station near Peak Downs (Q.), and he
was member for Geelong in one of the early Vio-
torian Parliaments.
Chief-Justice Madden (Vic.) will be 59 on
Saturday and Henniker Heaton 55 on Monday.
Madden was a more or less wild Irish youth of 13
when he landed in Melbourne in ’57. He was
admitted a barrister at the age of 21, and was
Minister of Justice under Premier McCulloch at
31. In 1876 he fought a tough election battle at
Sandridge against Dr. Hackett, the present editor
of the West Australian, who was then a strong
Radical, but shrivelled into a Conservative under
the warm rays of the Forrest influence over at
Perth. Madden has been Chief-Justice for 10
years. His wife is a Stephen, of which, family in
the male line Judges have been made continuously
in Australian history.
John T. Toohey, the big Sydney brewer,
who died lately in the United States, was the
son of a publican at Emerald Hill, now South
Melbourne (Vic.). After several ventures, he
and his brother, J. M. Toohey, started a bottling
business in Harbor-street. When the Albion
Estate was cut up, on the death of Mrs. Terry
Hughes, in the early 70’s, Mr. Toohey bought the
ground on which stood Hughes’ brewery,
an old building in ruins. From Harbor-
street the brothers removed to the site of
the present brewery, and, by degrees, the
present enormous business grew up. To one
incident must be attributed the final success of
the To’ee’s. In the Dandenong gale time, 1876, a
phenomenal storm broke over Sydney. Gleeson
and Burrows had a brewery at Waverlby; the
stormwater swept through their cellars and
destroyed their entire stock. In their dilemma,
they made arrangements with the Tooheys to
supply their customers until they lighted them-
selves. The Tooheys did so, and supplied so good
an article that the majority of the customers
stuck to them. There were no tied houses then.
The “lifers” of M.L. Legislative Council
are fast thinning out, Whitmore, Grace, Morris
and Swanson following each other within a few
weeks. Billy Swanson, the last of the bunch to
pass over, was a picturesque character, and during
his declining years was responsible for some queer
doings when Parliament was in session, owing to
infirmity. He used to trip down from Auckland
regularly despite the protests of his friends, and
the attendants at the House had much trouble
looking after him. Swanson will always be
remembered (if for nothing else) as being one of
four Auckland members in the Lower House
(Reader Wood, Hurst and Captain Colbeck were
the others) who transferred their votes to the Hall-
Atkinson Govt, in ’Bl. It was said that the under-
standing was that a large sum should be spent on
roads and bridges in Auckland province; but
Swanson always strenuously denied this, and
averred that the compact was to secure the chief
planks in George Grey’s policy, One Man One
Vote, etc. Swanson even threatened to post the
signed agreement up in the House to prove that his
version was correct. Deceased married a Maori
chieftainess of great landed wealth, who died a
few years ago.
THE AUDIENCE THAT GROANED.
“Mr. Barton was received with groans, thus clearly
demonstrating,” &c., &c.— (Freetrade Press.)
Sydney brewer John T. Toohey, M. L.C.,
died, like his brother, whilst travelling abroad
in search of health. The Tooheys built up a big
brewing business, and achieved a remarkable com-
mercial success. Both of them sacrificed health
in the work. John T. Toohey was practically an
invalid for 20 years before his death. A sturdy
Protectionist and fairly democratic in his views,
he had some good influence in N.S.W. politics.
He was for a period chairman of directors of the
Australian Stab, but was not so successful in
the newspaper as in the beer business.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— In the ease of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
A SLIGHT MISUNDERSTANDING.
MRS. MULVANEY : “So ye be fond of drawing,
Mr. Boosey? ”
MR. BOOSEY: “ Well, I dunno. S'pose I am.”
MRS. MULVANEY : "And what may it be ye like,
drawing best? ”
MR. BOOSEY : “ Well, mum, I'd prefer a cork to
a wheelbarrow any day."
Extracts from two letters, for the benefit
of the people and the papers who have the idea
that there isn’t anything left of the drought now,
and that it’s therefore time we started thinking
about another loan :
From Broken Hill.—“ Two months of torture ; rain
threatening every day almost, and (save for less than two
minutes one recent morning, when a heavy shower
scudded by) not enough in all that time to wash the dust
from the roofs.”
And from Renmark, half way along the Murray, in
S.A.—“ The river is still only 9in. above its lowest point ;
the rise has been so imperceptibly slow that the water
has remained clear, and, from the up-flow of the sea
water, is still brackish.”
Poets who write of the break-up of the drought
and “the thunder of the waters,’’ are notified
accordingly.
• * • *
At a depth of 4420 ft., or a good deal more
than three-quarters of a mile, the Mt. Gason bore,
in the far north of S. Aus., has struck a flow of
artesian water of at present something beyond
half a million gallons a day. The flow isn’t, com-
paratively speaking, anything tremendous (Coon-
goola bore, in Queensland, turns out six millions
a day), but the deptli is ; it easily beats Dolgelly,
N. S. Wales’ deepest completed bore, at which
three-quarters of a million gallons bubble up
from 4086 ft. Also there isn’t much need to boil
the billy at Mt. Gason —the water runs the
register up to 204° when it reaches the surface,
and presumably it cools off a bit on the journey
up. The water serves a dry stage on the Birds-
ville track.

“ Polstan ” : Re wild boy (8., 25/4/’O3).
A friend told me that a family living near Tumut
(N.S.W.) has a real wild man chained in a hub
near the house. He is a relative, who grew wild
as he grew old, till at last he had to be chained
for safety. He will not wear clothes, tearing
them off immediately they are put on him. Con-
sequently, Nature has provided him with a coat
of thick hair. His food has often to be thrown to
him, because of his savage moods. He detests
strangers, and howls and barks and snaps in a
doglike manner whenever any come near him—
which wouldn’t be often as far as I am concerned.
He got off his chain once and cleared into the
bush, but whether he ate anybody there, or did
any other damage, I know not. I don’t guarantee
the truth of this item, but the man who gave it to
me is the sole support of his aged mother, which
may be something in the way of corroboration.
• * • *
“Ed.” : When biking into Geelong (Vic.),
along one of the main Western district roads, a
week before Xmas, I passed a small flock of
decrepit rams. Five miles further on I came
across another flock of the same ancients plodding
to the City of Psalms. Asked the drover where
he was taking the wrecks. He said he was going
in to' catch the Xmas market, for the holiday
sandwich and the German sausage. A butcher
has since told me that, when highly seasoned,
boiled and smoked, you couldn’t recognise your
own grandfather.
* * * *
Life in the Far West—a telegram from
Milparinka (N.S.W.):
Typhoid fever has made its appearance here. The
sufferer has been removed to the nearest doctor, 130
miles distant. Until recently there was a doctor at
Tibooburra, 25 miles from here, but owing to the closing
of the hospital there he was forced to leave.
* * * *
At a backblocks settlement in the North
Island (M.L.) some time back a huge Maori man,
who had been imbibing too freely of the local
tanglefoot, and was apparently suffering from
snakes, rushed into the street clad in absolute
nothingness. A score of his own race clusteVed
round, but were unable to shift him, probably
because there was nothing to clutch at. The
services of a J.P. were sought, and rushing pell-
mell to the scene, that functionary balanced him-
self precariously on a tree-stump, and with awful
solemnity read the Riot Act over the naked
warrior.

A belated item from far inland Westralia :
I spent my youth in N.Q. and
always understood the Ragged
Thirteen to be a gangjwhich
was outlawed, and numbered
anywhere between 10 and
30, and that its lair (which
no one seemed to know
anything about) was al-
ways available for anyone
“ wanted ” by the police.
Then I came across
from North Queens-
land to the Territory
in ’97 prospecting, and
on the way made in-
quiries concerning the
gang. The only man
who was not a very
great liar and who knew
anything definite about
them was Joe Keenan,
mail contractor. He has
been in the Territory practi-
cally ever since the Over-
land telegraph line was built,
and by his account the Ragged
Thirteen fizzles down to this :
At the time of the Kimberley
(W.A.) rush, somewhere about
’B5, men were flocking across
from Queensland through the
Territory to Kimberley in two’s
and three’s and half-dozens. At
Anthony’s Lagoon (N.T.) one
afternoon they had gathered to
the number of a dozen, none
of them well-equipped—in fact,
there wasn’t a decent turnout
among them. They were ex-
changing jeers about their con-
dition whenja solitary and most
dilapidated swagman bore down on
them, and on the strength of his
arrival one humorous wanderer
christened the party the Ragged
Thirteen. They were proud of the name, and when
they scattered round the goldfields certain loud-
mouthed individuals, probably by bragging of the ill-
deeds they hadn’t done, created a Bagged Thirteen Myth
which lasts to this day. Also a few of these braggarts,
by horse-thieving and other common enough sins, lent an
air of reality to the murky and mostly imaginary narra-
tive. The Ragged Thirteen, while there were thirteen of
them, which wasn’t long, plodded across the wilderness
in a not much more disreputable way than pther travellers
of the period.
• * •
Barbara Sutherland writes on two points
interestingly:—
When Bulletin artists picture a way-back settler he is
wearing trousers too short and a beard too long, and his
women-folk have enormous feet and frowsy heads. They
constantly lean against a shaky post while the pigs and
fowls run riot in the best parlor. Seems to me it’s time
these “ Steele Ruddisms ” were dropped, and we coun-
try bumpkins credited with using an occasional “h”
and “g” and wearing decent clothes. If we took a
Wexford-street “lydy” and branded her as a typical
Sydney girl, wouldn’t the long-trained, narrow-chested,
simpering misses be disdainful ? Vet artists and writers
take as a model a loutish Ignoramus from Lordknows-
where and mould us into the same shape.
And the bush. It is “ melancholy ” and is possessed
with a “ weird expectancy.” Pshaw ! Merely a matter
of temperament after all. The bush is glad, or tragical
as now, but weird never. Watch a box-tree on a calm,
hot day, standing still and dark. A breath of air comes,
and it is a mass of shimmering gladness, every leaf dances
with it. The listening of the animals! Is that not
natural ? To them man is an intruder—instinctively they
watch him, lie still and wait. Then they go on their way
content. You cannot trace an expectancy of disaster.
The stillness of the night awes the city dweller, llis
brain craves the clatter of traffic. A dark clump of
belars strikes a chill through him, but even there the
moonlight dances quiveringly in wee shafts. The day-
breaks, sleepily. A bird’s chirp scarcely' distinct. A
bleat from an imprisoned calf. A porcupine creeps lazily'.
But with the first glint of the sun Nature lives joyously,
sings loudly, but never discordantly. The bush is so
naturally glad that I am hungry for it. The rain drips
on the asphalt bv mv window, and I think of the smell
of dead leaves with the new raindrops clinging to them,
and wonder if the drought tragedy is not better than
this —the imprisonment of the city.

IN THE PATH OF ART.

H E: “ Well, Miss, it’s very kind of you, though

“ Bunny ” : A few impressions of Broken
Hill. The absolute bareness of the surroundings
is appalling. Scores of square miles of gal-
vanised iron in buildings of all kinds ; allotments
bounded by wire fences, or by imaginary lines ;
outhouses mostly placed in the most conspicuous
part of the yard. Possibly the absence of the
usual kind of fence accentuates the loneliness of
the Hill. The dumps of the big mines almost
overhang the busiest part of the town, and a
sirocco could bury a good slice of B.H. beneath
thousands of tons of tailings. Women here do
not discuss servants. They talk dust storms and
the price of meat. Rain threatens frequently
but falls rarely—even by mistake. The price of
meat falls about as often as the rain does.
* • * *
“Shaker”: “G.J.M.” (B. 7/3/’03) has
probably been visited by a distinct variety of
“March fly.” There are four or five kinds,
ranging from twice the size of a house fly to an
inch long. The appearance and disappearance of
the varieties seems to be controlled by the
weather, one kind being entirely replaced by
another in a few hours. Sandflies also, north of
the Palmer, are so small as to be practically in-
visible, but, if you guessed their size by their
bite, you would probably say they weighed about
8cwt.

“ Woodsey ” : Re fly par. in a recent
Bulletin : Here in Tamworth you are very lucky
to get anything from your plate to your mouth at
all. The flies will have it in the passage, and
very often you have to eat a few hundred flies to
get your meal. People get used to anything,
and three or four dozen flies are not noticed
here in the soup. In fact, through eating flies,
some residents can walk on the ceiling head down-
wards. At a dance the other evening a young
lady forgot herself, and went waltzing with her
partner up the wall.
I was watch-
ing a stum-
py-tail dog
in the street
the other
day, when
all of a sud-
den he gave
a howl that
any Indian
would have
envied, and
“got” for all
he was worth.
J picked up a
stick and rushed to the
spot thinking the dog
was bitten by a snake.
I no sooner reached the
place than the stick flew
out of my hand, and I
was thrown to the
ground. I picked my-
self up again and de-
cided to have another
try. I got down on my
hands and knees and
went towards it with the
. wm d to investigate. After a few
minutes search I found the cause of all the
disturbance. It was the fragrance arising from
a dead flying fox. No one can imagine the odor
these creatures possess, unless from experience at
close quarters.

Dear Bulletin, — Verily Bananaland is
the home of hidden corpses, skeletons and un-
discovered murderers ! The saw “ murder will
out" doesn’t apply there. Quite recently another
skeleton, this time on Hobartville station, Rock-
hampton, and with large quantities of shot
(probably “ slugs ”) in his skull, was found stuck
in a hollow log, where it appears to have resided
for some years. This continual unearthing of
identity-less corpses sets one wondering every time
one misses a mate or reads a “ missing-friend ”
advt. which states that the long-lost person was
“last heard from in Queensland.’' Have often
noticed in these advts. (many unanswered ones
Kanakaland could probably account for) that
desired friends had been traced to every part of
the globe until they came to Mongrelia. Then
they apparently vanished off the face of the earth.
The creeks, scrubs, mountains, gullies and river-
bends of Mongrelia will be like a disturbed ant-
bed on Resurrection Day ! —6 xB.

“ Deeby ” : Old Bawly S—— is an iras-
cible squatter of the old English squire order. I
was present once when a young blackfellow from
a neighboring station rode up to Bawly as he
stood on the verandah to hand him a letter.
Bawly stood glaring at him for a moment, appa-
rently paralysed with rage, then roared out, “Get
off your horse and hand me the letter. How dare
you ride up to me, you scoundrel! ” For reply
the black threw the letter to the ground, and,
riding off, shouted back, “Gerrout, you old, bald-
headed , I could make a better dashed man
than you out of ox-hoofs and a bladder of lard.”
Bawly stood speechless for a time, then walked
down to the shed and sacked seven shearers for
daring to look at him. You couldn’t get a black-
fellow to go near Bawly now if you were to offer
him a case of grog.
A SON OF THE LAND .
“Tweed”: Be child-sweating on dairy-
farms. Cannot see the least harm in children
milking six or eight cows each morning and even-
ing. Common thing round here, and children
seem happy and cheerful and undoubtedly attain-
ing good physical development. Compare children
reared in dairying districts with city urchins.
School is the only trouble the children have. If
they had less mental strain and more physical
drill and exercises at school they would be far
healthier and better-bodied men and women.
And infinitely better mothers. If hygiene, plain
cooking, elements of physiology, replaced such
subjects as analysis, history of England, and
general cramming for examinations to advance
the individual kid-whacker’s screw, there might
be some hope for the future Australian physique.
Geniuses are born, generally self-educated, seldom
the product of early cramming and abject terror
of school punishment. Seldom are they the chil-
dren of geniuses—
Where the city of the healthiest fathers stands.
Where the city of the best-bodied mothers stands,
There the great city stands.

We called ’im “ After ’Arvest Bill.”
The sort o’ cove ’e were—
Suppose ’e ’ad a pro. note doo,
Or p’raps the roof were cornin' thro’—
It might be in a hurry, still
It couldn’t make ‘im stir.
’E got engaged to Thompson’s gal ;
She stuck to ’im three year.
W’en anybody arst ’ow soon
’E meant to start ’is ’oneymoon
’E’d chuckle “ After ’arvest, pal! ”
An’ grin from ear to ear.
’E tired out lots of ’em that way ;
They’re mostly married now.
’Twas just the same with ev’rythink :
If someone shouted’m a drink,
’E’d say : “ I’ll shout for you— some day
Next ’arvest, any’ow 1 ”
A fool once lent ’im two-pun’-ten,
To ’elp ’im pay ’is rent ;
An’ ev’ry year that fool said, “ Bill,
Them two pun’ ten’s a-owin’ still ! ”
But Bill says “ After ’arvest 1 Then
I’ll pay yer ev’ry cent! ”
Las’ seedin’-time ’e cot a cold
As turned to pleurisy ;
I took ’im in to Dr. Bung,
Who punched ’im all about ’is lung,
An’ said it were as “ good as gold ”
(I paid the Dr.’s fee).
Then Bill says : “After’arvest, Jim,
I’ll give you that ’arf-quid ! ”
I only smiled, an’ says, says I :
“ P’raps after ’arvest, Bill, you’ll die ! ’
I knowed ’e’d never pay, not ’im,
Though die ’e might—an’ did !

“ J.F.D.” writes :
Please save me from the fate of the rabbiting man who
collapsed when “ The Wrinkler ” talked about “ tenners.”
“ The Wrinkler” might add my scalp to his belt and
boast about the two of us when the next tyro dared to

abuse of the two bush authorities is too much. First,
“6x8”:“ The back country is the home of the ‘ two-
up’ school and the 'two-uppers.’” Is it? Ask any
habitue of the game. There is more money
“grounded” in a year at the two biggest Sydney

tion. And almost every man in town knows the ins and
outs of the game. It is not a case of a crowd of shearers

“ Right-Angle ” writes re mutton-birds :
I have seen Chatham Islanders pulling the greasy
squeaker out of his hole. At certain periods the natives
go catching these birds, which are cured and exported
When the bird is dragged from the hole, the islander
splits the upper flange of the beak and forces the lower
one through it. Why, is not clear; but it is con-
sidered desirable to keep the bird, if possible, alive
even unto the final disembowelling process. At the
camp a big fire is lighted and the men squat
round it and start plucking the birds alive, the
victims being, at intervals, tapped gently on the
head with a light piece of wood, presumably to entice
blood in that direction. When the miserable squawking
of the birds has died ouc the quivering bodies are thrown
in a heap. The different expeditions meet at Waitangi,
where the curing takes place. There is something of a
religious function about the whole of this catching and
curing business. About 15 miles off the Wharekauri
coast are three conical rocks, known as “ Tuawahine ”
(Three Sisters), and about birding time the islanders (in
whaleboats, provided by Maoriland Govt.) proceed to
these rocks, where some mysterious ceremony is pre-
sumably performed. No white man is ever allowed
to be of the party; and I have vainly sought permis-
sion to go. One reason for this expedition, however, is
the catching of young albatrosses, which are cured in
their own fat and barrelled for export. The Chatham
Islander is a surreptitious idolator, wrapped, during
birding time, in impenetrable surliness.

broken up. A sheep-farmer, who hasn’t touched

“ The Prince ” : Be some remarks awhile
ago, in The Bulletin, about giant logs. There is
still a legend up North about a cedar log rafted
down the Maroochy River (Q.) years ago. It was
20ft. long, and, when rolled into the water, it
floated end up because the breadth of it was
longer than the length, and it was taller crossways
than lengthways.

ALONE.
An Into rview with George Giddens.
It was immediately after the matinee that I saw
George Giddens, the London veteran, with the
grease paint still imbedded in the pores of his
nose. Over this nose he smiled with his eyes,
then he said, very calmly and collectedly :
“ Your climate is beautiful, and your harbor is
a dream. A beautiful dream of a Sunday on
a yacht. I love these sunshiny days, these
blue skies, these comfortable nights. And Hose
Bay ! Oh, that heavenly spot ! Made for moon-
light walks with the temperature at mild 65. You
can take your boots off and sit with your feet in
the warm soft sand. ISo much healthier and
pleasanter than being in a stuffy room side by
side on the antimacassar.
*'l love Nature. I love wild country and its
bull-dog ants. I love watching the birds, the
lizards running about. I get back where there
are no ants, and I take a long steady look at the
birds through my opera-glasses. There’s one cove
rt Rose Bay with a black back and white chest, a
cove that hops about and cocks his tail. He’s got
a lovely note. We can’t spend the winter in
Lngland with Nature. Dirty, dingy London !
Snowstorms, black mud, frosts, influ., fogs and
darkness ! And the only bright feature—flying
steps on the stairs ! —and Arthur Roberts dashing
in breathless, exclaiming : ‘ Hide me, Giddens,
the police are after me! I’ve stolen Wilson
Barrett s gold baseball boots and short Roman
chemise. Or the other brother-actor who comes
in very quietly later in the evening, sits down
with a self-satisfied smile, and remarks unctu-
ously : ‘ Oh, Giddens, it’s so soothing. Been
down in a cellar all day. I’m quite drunk, and
it hasn’t cost me a farthing ! ’
mu‘ Yes ’ 1 saw Willoughby play ‘On and Off.’
I hat was my piece at the Vaudeville. He’s an
energiser of the new school. I remember one old
actor who used to stop in the middle of arny scene
or any speech to reprimand an audience. His
Othello would come to the audience this way.
Farewell the tranquil mind, farewell content.
(To gallery) : Put cut those pipes 1
Farewell the plumed troop and the big wars that make
ambition virtue. O Farewell 1
(To gallery) : Stop eating those d d pea-nuts !
Farewell the neighing steed.
(Gallery voice interrupting) : Bah !
Actor (to gallery) : If that man will meet me outside
after the performance I’ll put a head on him in five
seconds.
Yes, he belonged to the very old school: the
school of the old Adelphi in Ben Webster’s time
where the regular catechism for the applicants
was-U) Are you over 40 ? (2) Do you wear false
teeth ! (3) Have you a dress suit? (4) Do you
wear a wig ? (5) Did you return the last shilling
you borrowed ? The applicant had first to suc-
cessfully answer these questions before he could
secure employment. Once through he was an
actor-r-r-r, who strode and posed and rolled his
rs, and frowned and glowered; and hatched dark
plots with a deep down voice, deep down in the
stomach. And off the stage, in some little back
parlor with a brother rogue or vagabond he
poured warm gin into the deep down voice,’and
<p3nly deplored the fact that ‘a new sohool is
c,rising, brother—a school that allows gentlemen
to come into the profession ! ’
Ihe old actress of the old school was just as
stereotyped as her brother. There was one old
lady—l forget her name; anyhow, she used to
play third old woman in my young days. And
she never could forget having once
played the Lady Tragedy Queen at
some provincial theatre. She couldn’t
even forget it when she abandoned
the boards and invested her savings
in a ‘stewed eels and tripe shop.’
No ! even behind the counter she was
still the Lady Tragedy Queen 1 Some
urchin would suddenly shoot through
the doorway, as if fired in with a
gun, bump up against the counter,
snuffle and say, ‘Two penn’orth o’
stooed eels, an* plenty o’ gravy me
mother sez.’ The Lady Tragedy
Queen would raise her eyebrows and
remark in deep tragio tones—‘Say ye
so, boy ? ’ And turning round she would
get the two penn’orth o’ stooed eels,
and place it before the nipper with ‘ Then
have at ye ! Two pence ! I thank ye 1’
“I regard an audience as one man
or one woman. Sometimes I’ve gone on
and felt there was somebody antagonistic
to me. I felt constrained, nervous
uneasy. Something like Beerbohm Tree
felt one night when Gilbert was in
iiont Gilbert who had said of some-
body’s Hamlet that it was funny without being
vulgar. Iree is a most excitable chap mad
some say. He’s a German Jew. He doesn’t
love praise, nor does he worship it. He eats
it. Hearing that Gilbert was in front he sent
round for him between the acts. When Gilbert
entered the dressing-room, he saw Tree sitting
in an arm chair perspiring most profusely. Tree
began fishing for a compliment. ‘ How do you
like the play, Mr. Gilbert ’ ? Gilbert, knowing
that Iree wanted praise, talked about everything
but the play. This worked the excitable Tree
up to a perfect fever heat. He perspired in
streams. He mopped his forehead, his wrists
his arms, he undid his shirt-he mopped his
chest, his neck ! Then for the tenth time, Tree
aching for Gilbert to say something, dying for
Gilbert to say anything, asked ‘ How do you like
the play ? Gilbert rose in silence and went out
Ihen he thrust in his head and said in a quiet
bored voice, ‘By the way, Tree, your skin acts
well.
“ 1 hay e gtme through all the vicissitudes of the
stage. In my very early days I experienced all the
ups and downs of the struggling young actor.
But when one is 19, getting a salary 6f 30s a week
is a jolly lark—that is if you get the 30s. regu-
larly Our manager worked his company on what
was known in those days as the ‘ Ortree system ’
That is, the company might sleep at an hotel or
it might sleep up a tree. Any week that the
company only earned salt, we only got 2s «d on
account. If we did well, we got our 305., but no
arrears. We d have starved, my two pals and I
only that one of us had a wealthy father who sent
him remittances. Our manager squandered nearly
all his money in betting, and choused everybody
Of course, he was constantly running the gauntlet*
GEORGE GIDDENS,
Leading Comedian in “Are You a MasonT
Photo, by Hana, Strand, London.
because in those days they could seize a man him-
self for debt. One night this manager was play-
ing lead in light comedy. He was on the stage,
in the middle of the performance. I was at the
wings in the character of Parker, the butler, wait-
ing for my cue. Suddenly on the left there was a
frightful rumpus, a thundering at the stage door.
I ran upon the stage with an impromptu ‘ May I
speak to you, sir—may I whisper?’ The manager,
electrified by the uproar without, answered:
‘Yes, Parker.’ I whispered: ‘The bailiffs are
trying to get in.’ The manager walked swiftly to
the right and said : ‘ Tell your mistress, Parker,
that I have gone out and will not return.’ And
with that he climbed into the flies, ascended to
the roof, got upon a neighboring house, galloped
alonsr the terrace roofs, and escaped.
“When the smash came to our manager I
parted from my two pals. I bought a knapsack
and set out to walk across the Yorkshire moors to
Scarborough. I had some bread and biscuits in
my knapsack ; and I had 3d. in my pocket. That
was the sum total of my worldly wealth. The
first night I camped out under a haystack. I had
a good rest with my knapsack for a pillow and
the hay that I pulled over me for a coverlet. It
was summer time and fine, and I didn’t mind the
mice that kept running over me. One cannot
have everything one’s own way in this world. In
the morning I got early upon my way, but I
hadn’t gone far when, coming to a turn in the
road, I saw a big tank hewn out of the rock and
full of water. It was about four o’clock. Nobody
was visible. Nothing was stirring. I whipped
off my clothes and dived in. The trough was
fully half the size of this room, and it was some
five feet deep, so I had a swim to my heart’s con-
tent. I was just getting out of the tank when I
was surprised by a startled scream, and I saw a
milk-maid coming round the corner with her pail.
She sort of looked away, and then sort of looked
at me and laughed. I said to her, ‘Don’t be
frightened ; you can go by. I won’t get out.’ So
she went by shyly with her head turned aside and
sort of looking at me out of the corner of
her eye while I kept down under the water.
She walked slowly until she reached a hedge
and then, with a last look back and a little laugh,
she disappeared. When I had dressed myself, I
saw that my way lay in the direction the girl had
taken. Have you noticed through life how often
the man’s way does lie in the direction the girl has
taken. Some two hundred yards along, I saw her
coming across a field towards me. She came up,
looking as sweet as a peach and as gentle as a
whisper. She looked me over with her kind,
brown eyes, then she asked ine to have a drink of
milk. I gratefully accepted. I sat down, took
my biscuits out of my knapsack, and made my
breakfast with the help of the rich new milk that
the girl poured into the lid of her pail. She made
me tell her all about myself, and I told her every-
thing except that I had no money. I offered her
my threepence for the milk. Bub she wouldn’t
have it. When I insisted, she pushed my hand
away from her, drew back and shook her head.
Doubtless her instinct divined the truth. Ah ! I
was only 19 then ! I wonder what has become of
her !
“The social status of actors and actresses in
London is the very best. In fact, I think there
is too much social business. The day is long past
since people looked askance at actors and actresses.
Ours is now recognised as one of the great pro-
fessions, and no super, is welcome now, no
beginner in the ranks, unless he can look a gentle-
man and is one. You see, if a man becomes a
barrister, he has to wait years and years without
earning a shilling. But if he is a good-looking
man with any talent at all he soon gets a chance
to distinguish himself upon the boards.
“No, I can’t sing, but I do.
“I think that the business of the theatre is to
amuse. Ido not believe at all in plays like ‘ The
Resurrection.’ It is sordid, gruesome, miserable.
I think it was Arthur Roberts who said that, as a
play, ‘ The Resurrection ’ is the dreariest wretch
lie ever set eyes on.”
S. A. MILLS.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Misfakes will occur in the best-regulated
in voices. — lmporter.
Tasmania makes the same old mistake. It
starts a Labor party when the thing wanted is a
Democratic Party.
“ Spud ” Young, N.S.W. ex-Minister and
Speaker, announces himself as a candidate for the
Manning in the next Parliament, on the Reform
platform. Young may be considered a very far
back number.
A query : Does the Labor party in N.S.W.
dominate Premier See to the same extent as
Major Randall Carey and ex-editor Brient domi-
nated Premier Lyne, and, if not, should not the
Daily Telegraph modestly hold its tongue ?
Re the “People’s Reform League.” Just
imagine John Stinson, H. S. Badgery, A. Mac-
arthur Onslow, A. W. Gilles, W. C. Goddard,
Alec Marked, C. Parker and Felix Randle mem-
bers of a committee of a league to reform the
people.
Every country Giafc has tried it has had to
pay a heavy price for importing colored labor to
work in its sugar plantations. The West Indies,
where Hindus Were introduced, are now over-run
with leprosy ; at Trinidad there is one of the
largest leper asylums in the world.
The Bent gang in Victoria is announced
to be advertising in England, S’Africa, America,
India, and Maoriland for blackleg engine-drivers.
Bent’s ignorance is about equal to his malevo-
lence. The Federal Immigration Act would
absolutely block such contract laborers.
Perhaps it is only a coincidence, but it is
none the less significant that, in the Victorian
railway crisis, almost the only classes that stood
to benefit by a strike were the market-gardeners
and wood-cutters round Melbourne, 90 per cent,
of whom are constituents of Ministers Bent and
Cameron.
Most per•fstently did the “Respectable
Press” of Australia announce its hope that “the
better judgment ” or “good sense” of the Vic.
railway men would avert such a catastrophe as a
strike. Even the Toriest rag of them all knew
better than to expect “good sense” or “better
judgment” from Bent.
N.S.W. Labor member Donald Macdonell
took a step in the right path at Cobar the
other day when he insisted that the Labor party
was not bound up with the Owe’See Ministry, and
stated that “the party would strongly advocate
a progressive land tax, to burst up big estates and
settle the people on the land.”
Almost the last act of the Lewis Govern-
ment (Tas.) was to resolve to fool no more with
the syndicate which has been just about to build
the Great Western land-swindle railway for the
last seven years, yet never got started. And
almost the first act of the Propsting Government
is to start fooling with it again.
Where, during the Vic. railway crisis, was
the voluntary arbitration so vaunted by the
Tories ? It failed, miserably. Had there been a
compulsory Arbitration Act in force the dispute
could have been settled without the necessity of
Bent making a shocking exhibition of himself,
like a bull-frog bellowing at an elephant.
It is only a few weeks since Tasmania, at
the bidding of the anti-income-tax people, cleared
out the Lewis Ministry, root and branch, yet
already a reaction seems to have set in. Two
Upper House elections were held a few days ago,
and each time the candidate of the Propsting
Government went down, Minister M'Call being
one of the lost.
Old Knight Braddon is said to intend
trying to capture old Knight Fysh’s seat in the
Freetrade interest at the coming Federal election.
Why Biaddon should take the trouble heaven
only knows. A man who was a Freetrade
Premier for 5L years, and all that time imposed
the heaviest tariff in Australia, shouldn’t make
such silly jokes in his old age.
For their cheap yellow miner the Jew-
hannesburg owners are going to rake Asia very
thoroughly. The route marked out for the two
Commissioners, Skinner and Noyes—who are
sampling the various brands —takes in Japan,
China (two months to be devoted to China), and
the Malay Peninsula. At present great hopes are
centred on Singapore as a base where innumerable
Ah Fats may be gathered for export to S’Africa.
The servile fear of what the British money-
lender may think tinges every act or projeot now
in debt-enslaved Australia. Of course, it cropped
up in the Victorian railway matter. Said Sydney
E. Noose
No capital would come into Australia under existing
conditions.
The horrible fear lest we may not be able to get
further into debt is always with us.
All Fat Men should see that the Irvine-
Bent Government is suppressed. It is making
the repudiation of solemn bargains so common
that the public is becoming used to breaches of
faith. And if the public becomes too much used
lo such things there may, among other matters,
be a great inquiry into land titles some day.
Where that might lead to as regards many of the
huge early grants heaven only knows.
Sydney Evening News lies over the rail-
way strike almost as magnificently as the D.T.
“ While claiming freedom of action for themselves
the men refuse to concede similar freedom to the
employers.” When? Where? Whoobjected to
Bent joining, or even organising, a union of Rail-
way Ministers ? Or who even objected to Bent,
in his spare time, joining the Employers’ Union ?
There is far too much shrieking at large over this
business.
It is really time someone took the editor
of Sydney Telegraph aside and urged him to see
that that dreadful paper, if it must lie, lies with
some air of probability. Last Monday it screamed
bitterly in its leading article about Premier
Irvine’s noble conduct in “ resisting the demands
of the men.” The demands of the men ! Wasn’t
it Irvine and Bent who were going round making
demands and innovations and revolutionary
changes!
Mr. Affleck, M.P.—a curious grey moss-
grown holy Wully who haunts N.S.W. politics—
having proclaimed that he doesn’t agree with the
one definite plank of the Joseph Carruthers plat-
form, Joseph promptly rushed into print to
explain that nevertheless he was proud and happy
to have Mr. Affleck as a member of his party.
Joseph as a political leader is a dismal failure. In
his eagerness for office at any price he may not
differ widely from the average run of politicians,
but in his incapacity to conceal that eagerness he
is simply pathetic.
George Reid fished carefully all over Tas-
mania with Protectionist bait. In the agricul-
tural districts he assured the people that “if he
protected anything it would be the great pro-
ducing industries of the country ” —which was a
broad hint that the potato growers would get
Protection if they voted for Freetrade. And when
he got to the west, where the great Blythe River
iron deposits are, the turncoat dry-doggist ex-
plained enthusiastically that he was in favor of
aiding and “ coddling ” the iron industry with a
Government bonus.
Sydney Telegraph says that Minister
Kingston was astonished at the shipping in
Newcastle Harbor, and adds “this was an
industry to which he had never given thought
in framing his customs tariff.’ Perhaps the
Telegraph will explain what Kingston ought to
have done about “ this industry.” He has left it
just as it was. In old days the coal ship came to
Newcastle in ballast, and took away coal without
let or hindrance. It does the same now. Does
the mangy, grumpy Telegraph desire that
Kingston should pay a bonus on exported coal,
or what?
DEPUTATION OF DRUNKS TO PREMIER SEE.
“ The importers suggest that a special court should be established to deal with charges against the mer-
cantile community.”— Daily Paper.
“ What we object to, Mr. See, is being dragged to the same court as thieves and swindlers who
rob the country through the customs. We may be drunk, but at least we’re reasonably
honest, and we dislike being associated with criminals .”
“Joey” Carruthers has again written a
lengthy mass of cantankerousness to demonstrate
that N.S.W. has the sole right to select a possible
site for the Federal capital. N.S.W., he claims,
can offer a certain site and say, “ Take this, or
none.” Even if he is right, however, he doesn’t
appear to throw much illumination on the subject.
In practice, the Commonwealth Parliament will
select its own site and ask N.S.W. for it, and if
the N.S.W. Parliament won’t give it the Common-
wealth Parliament will doubtless stay where it is
until the N.S.W. Parliament changes its mind.
Choice political item from D.T.’s country
news:
Blayney, Friday.—The local press report a growing
dissatisfaction with the State member, Mr. W. P. Crick,
Minister for Lands, and his re-election is regarded as very
uncertain when the next contest takes place. Much of
the dissatisfaction has been caused by Mr. Crick’s negli-
gence in attending to correspondence, and his lack of
interest in local affairs. There is a widespread desire that
Mr. Charles L. Garland should contest West Macquarie in
opposition to the Government.
It is only small men who are wanted in N.S.W.
politics. The big man gets into office. Then he
has the affairs of the whole State to look after, and
if he looks after them well, he hasn’t time to attend
to the culvert down by Mulligan’s and other
miserable little jobs. And in time the cry arises :
“ Take away this big man and give us some small
beadle person who won’t let great national affairs
distract his mind from our culvert 1 ”
“ T.D.” on Kyabram up to date :
The Victorian “reformer” is beginning to feel
that he can have too much of a good thing.
Three months ago I travelled some distance with
one of him by rail, and had to listen all the way
to a disquisition on the solid statesmanship of
Thomas Bent, “ the man for the railways.”
Yesterday I met the “ reformer” again, waiting at
a Flinders-street ticket window to book for Port
Melbourne. Both of us had barely time to catch
the train which carries farewellers to departing
mail steamers. There happened to be three
ticket windows, and Thomas Bent, in his over-
mastering wisdom, has left one man, instead of
two, to distribute the pasteboards and collect the
money at all three, serving seven or eight of
the busiest suburban lines. The one man was
down at the far window, and gradually worked his
passage our way. I hastily secured the first
Port ticket. A "thin, perky little woman shoved
her hand in for the next, and as I rushed off
the “ reformer ” was breathing heavily about an
important business transaction down at the boat.
The train was already moving as I hopped in, but
I had the satisfaction of observing Bent’s ad-
mirer and the thin, perky woman, and several
sleek personages who also had the aspect of being
some of the solid voting force that gave Bent
and the Icebergers their lift, all standing in a
row on the platform and expressing their impotent rage.
When I met the “ reformer ” hurrying down Port Mel-
bourne pier just after the liner had cast off, and vainly
looking for the other party to the thwarted business trans-
action, the time seemed opportune to ask him whether
he still retained his admiration for fat Thomas. “ Oh,
damn Bent 1 ” he replied.
Nearly every Tory paper in Australia is
aghast with wrath and indignation at N.S.W.
Minister Owe’Sullivan for saying that the
Victorian railway men would yet obtain
justice from their State Government. Ap-
parently these papers are bold enough to
imply that they do not want the men to obtain
justice ; to even dream of such a thing is criminal.
Next time Owe’Sullivan speaks he should correct
himself, and explain that the workers will never
obtain justice from the Irvine-Crooked Govt.
Perhaps that would suit better.
The last acts of the Victorian railway
trouble previous to the strike were characteristic
of the utterly lawless methods of the rowdy Bent-
Irvine push. The men intimated in the press
that if Parliament made it illegal for them to
belong to the Trades Hall they would not belong
to it. Parliament was to meet the ensuing week,
and then the point could have been settled. The
Bent-Irvine brigands said, “No ! you must leave
the Trades Hall because we, in defiance of the
law, say you are not to belong to it. You must
recognise us as the law.” Then it gave notice of
discharge to the union leaders for not recognising
Bent and Irvine as the law. Then came the
strike.
“ E.G.8.” : I know of a Yic. State school
teacher, with 24 years’ service, who took three or
four days over his proper vacation on a honey-
moon. Salary being small he “faked” the rolls
for those days, was found out, suspended, taken
before P.S. Board and dismissed. This was his
first offence during 24 years. Next Govt., with
Gurr as Minister for Education, inquired into case
anew and gave him temporary employment.
Present Govt, has sent him up to the most “un-
livable ” portion of the Mallee—no house for wife,
and has cut his screw down to £7O per annum
and told him to take it or go.
The Vic. railway men conducted their side
of the Trades Hall affiliation issue with far
more ability than readers of the unscrupulous
dailies would imagine. The Age and Argus, and
with them Bent and Irvine, constantly referred
to “ the open threats of mutiny and striking” by
the men. Yet it would be impossible to indicate
a single representative of the railway em-
ployees who used the word “strike” until the
Bent ultimatum came along, or made any remark
which might be construed into a threat of the
weakest description. All the threatening came
from Bent and Irvine. The letters which the men
sent to Commissioner Fitzpatrick were models of
trenchant but respectful writing. The railway
organisations were advised by men who have the
reputation of being cuter lawyers, and by at least
one man who is a far cleverer politician, than
Irvine can ever hope to be.
HOW THE LATEST PROPOSAL WOULD
LOOK ON THE MAP.
The Federal Capital shall he at least 100 miles
from Sydney, and in N.S.W. territory.
George Reid finished his career in Tas-
mania with a letter to the press in which h»
assured the people that if he became Federal
Premier’(1) He wouldn’t introduce a progres
sive land-tax to burst up big estates and settle the
people on the land ; (2) he wouldn’t tax absentees-
(3) he wouldn t introduce direct taxation in anv
form ; (4) he wouldn’t introduce Freetrade • (5) in
addition to maintaining the Barton Government’s
revenue duties he would also preserve those which
are only slightly protective. And the local i m
porters’ papers headed the last reports of the Yes-
No man’s orations “Freetrade triumphs” !
Recalled now, in connection with the
tenth anniversary of Seddon’s Premiership i n
M. that the 26 Ministries before him had
an average life of only 17 months. The State
had three Ministries in its first 26 days of
Responsible Government; after that Stafford
kept in office 5 years and 1 month; and that
made an easy record until Richard came along.
Maoriland has put up some curious records
Three Ministries were formed in 1856 (all inside
26 days as above mentioned); three in 1876, and
three in 1884 (all inside 18 days). Curiously
enough, N.S.W. also started Responsible Gov-
ernment by founding three Ministries in 1856.
Kingston’s late tour through Queensland
has done more to strengthen the Ministry’s
position in that State than could have been
accomplished by any other visible agency.
He received over 50 more or less hostile deputa'-
tions, and he sent them all away with a vague
sense of their own foolishness. It was a novel
experience to Bananaland’s moneyed potentates
to confront a heavy, severe-visaged Minister who
knew his own mind, was conversant with the
minutest details of departmental administration,
and who expressed himself in terse, direct and
unequivocal language. Beside the fawning Philp
he appeared a political Titan, and his tour was a
series of popular triumphs unprecedented in the
history of the State.
The Constitution gives the Australian
Parliament power to interfere in industrial
disputes extending over two or more States. The
present railway crisis in Victoria is sufficiently
grave to justify the immediate summoning of the
Australian Parliament, and when it had met,
the strike could be technically extended into
N. S. "Wales and S.A. to the extent of the
men of both States threatening to refuse
to drive or work trains to co-operate
with the blackleg services of Melbourne. The
Federal Parliament could then interfere and
settle the matter, and if Irvine and Bent refused
to obey the Commonwealth law then Irvine and
Bent could be arrested, and sent to gaol
like any other law-breakers. This course
would take the matter not only out of the
hands of the Crooked-Irvine Ministry, but also of
those of the Kyabram Parliament—a course to be
desired by all lovers of liberty and progress.
Close examination shows that the “back-
bone ” of Vic. Premier Irvine is only made of bits
of newspaper, after all. The calm, firm man
would doubtless have let the whole railway
Trades Hall matter drop after it had served his
purpose in getting the alleged reform proposals
through, had it not been for the persistency of the
papers in wanting to know when the Ministry
intended to carry out its threat. Then Irvine
renewed his hustling, and was greatly astonished
to find that the railway men wouldn’t knuckle
under. With that calm which is manufactured
for him by the papers, the Man of Ice held up his
reputation for impassibility for a time, and
refused to negotiate. Then both papers came
down one morning with advice to the Govt, not
to meet the calamity half way, and it was edify-
ing to note the speed with which the calm strong
man climbed down from his lofty perch to treat
with the railway workers. Daily thereafter, for
a week, Premier Irvine spent his time “giving
the men fresh time to consider.”

Hydatids on the Liver
ANOTHER VICTORY FOR
VITADATIO
Oampaspe (Vic.), 24th August, 1901.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
181 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure in giving my testimony
to the good quality and curing power of Webber’s VITA-
DATIO. Some two years ago I was taken ill; one
doctor said it was Whooping Cough. I was in bed for
over a month, and got weaker and weaker until I went
to another doctor, who said it was Hydatids on the
Liver. The doctors ordered me to go under an operation,
but I did not care to for fear of chloroform, and on the
next day when my mother was reading the paper, she
came across a testimonial from a person who had been
cured of a similar trouble to my own with VITADATIO,
so I said I would try some myself. I sent for a few bottles
at once, and commenced taking it, and I never felt better
than I am to-day, and do honestly believe that I would
have b en dead long ago, had I not resorted to VITA-
DATIO. I have taken ten bottles in-all. lam increasing
in weight every month, and feel it is a pleasure to be
alive. I was waiting to see if my cure was lasting before
sending you a testimonial, and will be pleased to give in-
formation to anyone at any time. You may publish this
as you think fit.
Yours truly,
(Signed) ISABEL M. WASON,
Campaspe.

ARE YOU A STRENGTH
BUILDER )
It is bad enough to suffer the direct pains
of specific illness, but it is far worse to have
to endure the weakness and debility which
come after. Specific fevers, inflammatory
diseases, in departing always confer legacies
of weakness. This weakness, often called
debility, is the failure of the power of the
individual to perform the ordinary exertions,
whether of duty or of pleasure in which one
habitually engages. Debility really spells ill-
ness, because it renders you again liable to
some specific malady. Always build up the
strength.
ARE YOU A STRENGTH
BUILDER? Do not go on in a feeble,
half-hearted physical condition, it is a mis-
take that may cost you hours of needless
pain. It is so easy to avoid pain by keeping
well. Be a strength builder ! The shortest,
surest way to regain strength, to prevent
liability to renewed attacks, whether in
grown people or children, is by the use of
Scott’s Emulsion. Don’t forget that what
will build you up after an illness will build
you up before and so prevent illness. If,
therefore, you are weak, start at once a
course of
SCOTT'S EMULSION. n is the
greatest possible mistake to make light of
slight illnesses, as they are the precursors of
more serious illnesses. Therefore take Scott’s
Emulsion promptly, because it will cure you
of your weakness just the same as it has
cured thousands of people in other parts of
the world. You have only one precaution to
take; and that is to see that on the wrapper
of the bottle there is a picture of a man carry-
ing a big cod-fish; if that cod-fish is not
there, you are not getting what we promise
you—a cure! You can obtain a sample of
Scott’s Emulsion, and the choicest children’s
booklet of the year, “A Bunch of Roses,”
beautifully printed in colours, by sending sd.
to Scott & Bowne, Ltd., 483 Kent Street,
Sydney, and mentioning this paper. All
chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.
