CURES THE WORSTCOUGHS.
Business, Robbery, etc.
• Vi7.hr> Sydney Publican who is, accord-
in?u° ( ? ULLETIN 23 /3/’O3) building a beer pump
with a double suction-pipe-one end to go in the beer-
barrel the other in a water tub-will fall in if he isn’t
careful. Premising that he starts operations with a full
barrel of swipes and a common bucket of water, as he
probably will, his pump will draw pure beer as lomr as
that is above the level of the water. When both reach
the same level they will be drawn up in equal quantities ;
it won t work un ess the two pipes are so nicely gauged
that the pump plunger will keep both pipes filled when
in operation, and even then things will go amiss for the
water wffl flow along the pipe faster than the beer.
What this gentleman wants is a small water-pipe let into
the discharge-pipe of his beer-engine. In case some
immoral publican may act on this suggestion let me
assure him that I have already brought it under the
notice of the excise officers of three different States and
that the game isn’t worth the candle.
• • • .
Melbourne Age lately printed one of a number
of complaints from fruit-growers as to their poor returns
One soil-walloper instanced a case, in which he sent to
Melbourne markets grapes which realised from 2s. 3d. to
3s. 6d. per case. Out of this, freight took 6d., cartage 3d
and commission 10 per cent., leaving him an all-round
average of Is. 9Jd. per case. The return, for 40 to 50 lb of
grapes, is by no means gaudy, but it is chiefly the
grower’s own fault. The “cartage” charge (the agent
sending his own lorry to the goods yards and bringing a
ton of cases at a time to his sale-room) is just as big
a swindle as it was in the days when it obtained in the
butter trade, and the commission charge is anything but
moderate, but the Vic. fruit-growers have at hand—in their
Central Association with its 30 branches—all the
machinery for doing away with such charges by estab-
lishing their own sale rooms. The chief cause' of this
poor return for his toil, however, is the direct result of
the Kyabramapootra’s yell for retrenchment. Work is
scarce in the grape-grower’s only market—Melbourne,
wages have been cut down to the bare living minimum,
and the inevitable result is that prices for everything
beyond the bare necessaries drop like a fat man Out of a
balloon. The aggregate fruit-raiser was one of the loudest
barrackers for Kj-abram, and has repeatedly avowed him-
self hostile to Minimum Wage laws, and now—well,
another Pootra fledgling has come home to roost.
9.T.5. : As to the “swindle” of mixing
straw with hay in making chaff (B. 4/4/’O3) “ Ohass” gives
his case away by admitting that both cockiea and busi-
ness-men have “ ceased to regard it as a swindle.*’ If the
vendor makes no concealment of what he is giving, and
the purchaser doesn’t mind, what harm is done ? Indeed,
who has fixed, and when, what you must make chaff of
in order to avoid being accused of swindling? Webster
defines it as “ the husks of grain, and straw or hay cut up
for the food of cattle.” As a boy, in England, I have cut
up many a lot of chaff for a pony, and straw was, as a
matter of course, always mixed in about equal proportions
with the hay. Is there any law or established custom in
Australia to the contrary ?
• • • •
Westralia is at last going to risk having Chinese
for Savings Bank clients. Up to now, the utter inability
of the average European to tell Ah Chow from Ah Fat,
and the extreme proneness of Ah Chow to swear that he
really is Chow Fat, when there has been money hanging
to the change, has scared the officials off the risky busi-
ness. Even now a large number of alleged safeguards
have been thought out, and are to be used. For instance,
a British subject, resident in the neighborhood of the
Savings Bank agency, must attend w'hen Ah Chow opens
his account, and must attest that it is Ah Chow, and
though thereafter anybody may bank in Ah Chow’s
name, the British sponsor must attend and swear again
to Ah Chow before any money may be withdrawn. What
will happen to the British subject who gets mixed and
swears that Chow Fat is Ah Chow, and thus enables Chow
Fat to return swiftly and silently to his own country with
the wealth of Ah Chow doesn’t seem to be provided for
in the new regulations.
* *"* a •
Even with European clients banks sometimes
adopt the expedient of taking a personal description ; it
is, in fact, found to be almost essential in connection
with “ marksmen.” Some bankers go further and won’t
have anything to do with a “ marksman,” unless he looks
like being a remarkably profitable client, on account of
the trouble and risk involved in paying out. Ordinarily
the description is written in the primmest and most re-
spectful language—just the language you would expect
a bank clerk to write where his bosses could read it—but
now and then the boss receives a shock, as when there
came over from the West the “ personal description ” of a
valued client, in which it was mentioned that the client
“ usually smelt very high.” It took the head office weeks
to recover from the shock.
* V * •
A colored man, giving evidence in a Melbourne
court case, stated that he had been seven years in the
employ of a Pill Co., and was now getting 16s per
week. The Bench telephoned to the Pill factory, and
the statement was confirmed. Sixteen shillings a week !
The man who makes pills for such a beggarly wage can
never need to take pills. He lacks the means to acquire
biliousness or sluggish liver.

A curious advertisement from a northern N.S.
Wales paper :
fJIO THE RESIDENTS OF THE CLARENCE.
ARTHUR TREDWELL, of Maclean,
Sends Greetings !
WHEREAS Times are dull !
AND WHEREAS the said A.T. is out work,
AND WHEREAS the said A.T. has a great notion
of going a-farming, but has not as much capital as
would jingle on a tombstone, he has, however, or
thinks he has, a few friends in this district (if he only
knew where to find them), and he proposes to give
them an opportunity of finding him, and giving them-
selves at the same time a NOVEL PHILANTHROPIC
TREAT. He proposes that each of his said friends
should deposit into a Fund (to be opened at the office
k of this paper) the sum of Five Shillings—which sum
would not break any of them if the venture failed,
and it might make him. Each contributor to furnish
his name and address, not necessarily for publication,
but so that the several sums may, at the end of three
years say, be returned to them.
* * * *
Vic. Minister’s Befit and Murray lately ex-
plored part of the fat western district. Along the
Gellibrand River they found miles and miles of flats
richly carpeted with red clover, and carrying a cow to
the acre. The sole product of the locality is cheese,
which has to be taken along the river by tow-boat, in
preference to attempting to get over the hills with it.
The Country is so dense that the Ministers met a team of
10 bullocks, which had all it could do to get along with a
dray and five cwt. of cheese. But the industry is pros-
perous enough. The first year of one of the cheese fac-
tories resulted in a profit of £2OO, next year £4OO, and
this kept doubling, until now the co-operators make
£3OOO out of it for the year. But let one hard year come,
and presumably these farmers would flock down to Mel-
bourne to ask the State to pull them through their
difficulties.

A perfectly respectable—indeed rather affluent
—contributor supplies this item, which casts a grave
doubt on its genuineness : During my early begging
days in Melbourne there was nothing that interested me
as much as my initiation into the Beggars’ As-
sociation. The Association had nothing to do with
those religious individuals who cadge in an authorised
and legal way, nor was it in any way connected with the
Salv’army. The Association was comprised chiefly of the
commonplace street beggar. Its founder was an ex-
spieler, ex-burglar, in fact ex-everything. He was even
ex-honest, but in that matter he was more completely
“ex” than in others. The ex-everything was a pawn-
broker. By subscribing 6d. a week to him he would
supply you with disguises as often as you needed them.
He also decided any argument between any of the
fraternity as to the ownership of stands, or ques-
tions of' similar character that might arise. Before
I joined the society I was an Indian Mutiny
warrior, minus an arm, and through the medium
of the get up, I received much sympathy and small
change. One day the ex-everything (unknown to me
then) came to me and said : “You doan b’long to the
’sociation, do y\” Innocently I asked if he meant the
Commercial Travellers’ Association. I replied in the
negative to his amended question—“ Do you belong to
the Bummers’ Association?” Whereat he exclaimed—
“ Then what the d do you mean by coming the
Indian Mutiny dodge when we’ve already got one in the
union ? ” I realised that two at the game must neces-
sarily lessen profits ; so, acting on the advice of the
ex-everything, joined the union. I was then appointed
to be a blind man in Elizabeth-street, but failing in that
capacity, I applied to the union for a Change, and it
allowed me to become paralysed in Prahran instead.
» * * •
“ J.E.D.” : Knew a time-payment furniture
man who has, to my knowledge, completely furnished
five different “houses of pleasure” within a very short
period. In every case the police ran in the lady for
keeping a disorderly house, before the furniture man had
been fully settled with, and, owing to fines and loss of
business, she failed to pay the final instalments ; so the
spring van gleefully carted the sticks back to their old
home, and the amount paid was forfeited. One place
that he has furnished hasn’t been raided yet, but a
policeman is never 50 yards from the door. The furni-
ture man knows a great number of the police.
9 * * *
The “ great safe game ” is one of the curiosities
of crime. Montgomery and Williams, the two men who
were hanged in Sydney years ago for cracking a skull or
two in breaking away from a police spun net, were said
to practise it. These two, with others, bought various
safes, and had them sent to a certain address. They
studied them a while, made a few experiments, and
duplicated a few keys. Then they sent the whole batch
into the auction rooms and watched the sales. Within a
month or two every purchaser found that his house or
office had been entered, and the safe opened “ apparently
without an effort.” The total losses (or proceeds)
amounted to £4OOO odd.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Mr. Harry J. Mosea, on* of the leaders of the
Westralian betting ring, went, rather suddenly, to the
“great settling” on the 7th. Mr. Moses was one of the
oldest metallicians in Australia, being well to the fore
with pencil, book, and bag in the brave days of old,
when Joe Thompson, “Brother Jock’’and Barney, Phil
Glenister, Bill Branch, Austin Saqui, etc., made the
welkin ring with their mellifluous shouts of “ A pony on
th’ Fe-euld!" “This an’ the’ next!" “Six t’one th’
Cup—an—Durdle ! ” And the fact of 3 to 1 being taken
twice about any horse didn’t scare a “Tommie” into
shortening the odds, or even shutting shop for the day,
as it occasionally seems to do these times.
• • * •
“D. Harum” : In Adelaide, sinoe the Gaming
Bill came into force, the betting shops don’t give you any
receipt or other incriminating document for your money.
Also you don’t give your name —simply initials or an
alias. The bookie puts this down and takes your money
and you trust to his honor. Tf he hasn’t any you get left.
If some one else overhears you give your fancy name and
collects for you, you get left. If the police raid the place
before the betting man has time to conceal the book he
gets left. If he gets left very badly you probably get left.

zies (Vic.) for long time acted as judge at V. A.T.C. meets,

is 10 years judge for V.R.0., and latterly for Sandown
Park, Moonee Valley and other Victorian clubs. Sixty-
four applied for the job at £4OO a year.

Goodwood Handicap (9.A.J.C., Adelaide) saw
21 line up, with Tip Top first choice and Trochon and
Fairy States next best. Trochon (slowing down to
nothing near the end) just won and no more, with
Chatter second, and Tip Top third. Chatter was very
unlucky ; he finished as if shot from a catapult after being
badly placed up to then. Another stride, as the two nags
flashed past the post, saw Chatter in front. Jockey
M'Donald vowed and evidently felt sure he got there in
time. S.A.J.C. Stakes were soft for Sweet Nell. Sojourner,
up to then favorite for Adelaide Cup, carried £1348
of £lBOO invested on the four starters for the St. Leger,
and Mo. asked 3 to 1 on. At the end of the first quarter
Sojourner was coated with foam, and from the home turn
his stable chum, Cross Keys, had him beaten. Crowd
raised Cain, but the crowd was wrong, as it often is.
Result is only another exemplification of the fatuousness
of trying to pick winners. Sojourner had beaten Cross
Keys badly on the track, and the trainer sized that what-
ever chance the latter possessed, Sojourner’s was
infinitely better. Favorite (Lieutenant Bill) gathered
Elder Stakes comfortably.

“Brumby”: Two Sydney betting-shops are
engaged in a fight to a finish, and incidentally the poor
punter reaps the benefit. Heat of the battle centres
round “double” prices. Usual town price is £4 or
£4 10s. to Is.—Bo or 90 to 1. The two irate rivals have
outbid each other alternately, until the price available for
a double on Canterbury races, held on the 9th inst., was
£lOO to Is. —2000 to 1. Second and third books were laid
at £BO and £2O to Is. Also, 150 to 1 was procurable about
pony doubles, usually laid at 20 or 15 to 1. Seedy turf-
cranks might be seen snapping up, with glistening eyes,
anything in the way of a double left on the card. It’s a
delicious sensation to gaze on a slip of paper worth a
prospective £lOO and imagine the money already yours.
It’s a cheap dissipation for a humble bob—even if it
doesn’t last long.
* * • *
Shrapnel (T he W orkman-Element), who got away
with the big Thompson Handicap at Hutt (M.L.) races
recently, had been racing with hacks for a few months,
prior to which he was used to round up sheep. The
time, 1.41 1-5, was pretty hot for a mile, the winner
galloping the field to a stand-still. Strange thing about
this particular event is that in 11 years ex-“ hacks” have
nulled off the stake seven times.

Must be said in justice to Mo that when he
owns nags he mostly runs them straight and ’tis safer

“Ne’er-do-Weel”: Goat racing flourishes in
Northern and Western Queensland, and the nippers
who follow the game occasionally take the Ikeys down in
style. At Longreach, there is a strong club, which was
subsidised by the adults until the nippers showed that
they could run it themselves and a bit over. Now they
come round for subscriptions, offer fair prizes, and do
the thing in style. Longreach also has its Carbine—an
ancient William which is run in that name. The stable
owning Carbine had also the champion goat jockey of
the district, and used to make a small pot out of the
combination. When Carbine became so well known that
no one would bet against him, the stable made a dis-
honest alliance with the rival establishment, which
owned the second-best goat—Ormond. Inconsequence,
thrums, deeners, half-dollars, and occasional dollars and
even half-quids were dribbled quietly on the latter
until Ormond stood to win the combined stables a tidy
sum. When the hairy animals came out for the Great
William Handicap, the crack jockey was seen to be
riding Ormond, and outsiders began to have dark sus-
picions. Suspicions were almost the only asset left them
when Ormond romped in, with Carbine second palpably
stiffened. There was a row, of course, but those in the
know were sufficiently strong to hold their own. M hen
remonstrated with over their evil ways, the combined
Btables were very indignant—“ Whatcher giv n us?
Didn’t Mr run two ’orses in th’ ’Andicap larst
Annual, an’ didn’t ’e stiffen th’ fav’rit ? Then why th’ ’ell
shouldn’t we? Whatcher ’owlin’ at? It’s orl in th’
gyme.” ,
“K.N.”: The booming which has been given
to Hindu Gunga Brahm (now the champion wrestler of
Australia), bodes much evil to the white athlete; and
the awful haste with which a “colored” champion is
welcomed by the “ leading ” papers and people is one of
the things which is extremely hard to understand from
an Australian point of view. Perhaps it is solved by the
wish that tribal differences may stir up the wild blood
and cause something akin to a gory bull-fight. And, if
that was the ideal, the witnesses of the recent match were
somewhat rewarded by the yell which the winner gave
vent to when informed that he had gained the title ;
and the subsequent “ wild ” attitudes which he assumed.
Perhaps the most hideous comment was the pub-
lication by Melb. Leader that the champion’s diet
consisted chiefly of “ meat and butter ... he devoured
half a sheep in three meals and a pound and a half of
butter at one sitting.”

The championship of billiards in England
seems to be even more open than was imagined.
Immediately after Dawson’s narrow victory over
Stevenson, the winner took on Diggle, of Birmingham,
in an exhibition game. Diggle received 1000 points in
9000—and won by 999 !
* • * *
There’s trouble brewing ’mongst the bikes. A
recent race, in which the stake was solid, is likely to
have its true inwardness ventilated in the courts. It is
alleged that the winner, prior to the event, approached
the man he considered his most dangerous opponent, and
in that delicate manner distinctive of pros, in all sports,
intimated that, should he win, his confidant would be
the richer by £2O. That agreed on, the “goer” flitted
away to make similar arrangements based on a sliding
scale with the other contestants. But the winner, after
paying all save the 20-pounder, found that when he kept
that part of the contract he would have a credit balance
of only £B. Strongly objecting to such a one-sided division
of the spoil, he promptly repudiated all liability. The
claimant is now deeply pondering the respective advan-
tages of a summons and a sling-shot.

Looks as if big Dunlop road race (Goulburn to
Sydney) won’t occur this year through dispute ’tween
Dunlop Co. and N.S.W. League. Trouble is over Beau-
champ’s recent attempts on records at the instance of
Dunlop Co. League demanded 10 guineas expenses for
League officials who acted ; co. offered out-of-pocket-
money for timekeepers, and explained that as their
compact was with Beauchamp only, the matter of exes
for necessary officials would naturally be between him
and the League. It was further stated that they (the co.)
thought cycling officials always acted in an honorary
capacity. Council of the League withheld sanction for
the event pending payment of amount demanded. Co.
remains firm in its stand, and race will probably be aban-
doned. No other cycling body throughout Australia has
ever made a similar demand under like circumstances.

N. C. Hopper— the Yankee bikist who raked
in Sydney Thousand and minor events —left for the land
of Stars and Stripes t’other day. Like the imported
Major Taylor and 99 out of 100 over-sea hikists who visit
Australia in search of boodle, he expressed “great
satisfaction at the result of the trip.” Which, after all,
is only human, considering that in a couple of months he
earned in prizes about £BOO, besides those “ trade extras ”
which always fall to the lot of successful riders. The
success of Taylor and Hopper will certainly result in an
influx of Yankee and Continental riders to Australia next
season, and League and Association handicappers will
have to keep a wary eye on the “ form ” of strangers from
abroad who may enter for the “ plums ” of the prizes.
• • • •
For the control of Rugby football in N.S.W.
there are two bosses —the N.S.W. Rugby Union and the
Metropolitan Union. The latter body isn’t such an old
infant that, if effectually strangled or wiped out, it would
be seriously missed. The N.S.W. Rugby Union prides
itself as the “ authority," its w'ork being simply to
attend to inter-State and “big” football (there are four
fixtures against M.L. and two against Queensland
this year). The infant is to promulgate suffi-
cient interest amongst metropolitan clubs, whilst the
older union takes all the kudos when the rep. teams
wipe out the visitors. The real reason for the second
body is mostly because the “head” Union doesn’t care
to soil its hands and waste its time with protests and
grievances in relation to competition matches, and so
relegates such work to someone else. One “ live ” body
could effectually do the work—and become the “ au-
thority ’’ in reality and not in name.
* » • •
N.S.W. Rugby Union has so far patched up
its quarrel with the Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees
as to arrange for the three most important fixtures with
the visiting Maoriland footballers to be played on the
Association ground. The Agricultural ground and the
University oval are to have their share too in the
inter-State matches. To the haughty individual who is
a member of the Cricket ground and to the haughtier
still who is on the dead-head list, it is a matter of regret
that all the games are not to be played on the big
field, where one can watch the play from a comfortable
stand, with a first-class bar to be shouted drinks at, or a
tea room to swagger round with his Saturday girl. But
the vast crowd of football barrackers who have to sit on
uncomfortable backless forms don’t care so much. Indeed,
they prefer the ’Varsity ground, to which most people can
travel in a penny tram, and not have to either pay 3d or
tramp out to Moore Park. The crux of the dispute is
money taking and making, and the wishes of their
patrons concern equally the Trustees and the Union only
so far as the “gate” is affected.
• * » *
Third battle between heavyweight pugs Jim
Scanlan (’Murka) and Billy M'Call (N.S.W.) took place at
Sydney National Sporting Club, last week, for £lOO
a side, a £250 purse, and Australian supremacy. An-
nounced weights were, M'Call 11.10, and Scanlan 12.4.
Six to 4 M'Call was freely closed with. The struggle
fairly bristled with exciting situations. M'Call made the
pace ; he was after and on to his man with the tenacity
of a bull-pup, never letting up an instant. Third was the
star round of the fight. A hair-raising rally saw M'Call
clip Scanlan on the chin as he came in, and ; Murka went
to the boards with a bump, and 2000 onlookers stood up
and roared excitedly. Scanlan squirmed and wiithed,
got on toes and hands, and then flopped on to his
stomach again ; he tried once more, and rose to his feet,
only to flounder all over the ring while M'Call essayed to
finish there and then, but, through excitement, his efforts
were ill-judged though he made the ’Murkan stagger and
sent him to his hams. Scanlan, grit to the chin, came
again, and just had sense enough to clinch and lean on the
Australian with legs lagging badly, but a heavily charged
punch sent him oscillating and staggering till he fell and
rolled on his back, spread-eagled beyond all likelihood of
coming to inside the allotted lOsecs. The round ended
while the Yankee was still stretched, and seconds
carried him to his corner mid an uproar of shouts and
hurrahs. Subsequently the fighting was mostly one way
—M‘Call’s—and this despite that he badly hurt the top
knuckle of the left thumb and wrenched his right
shoulder. Scanlan never recovered from the third-round
smashing, and rarely did much more than clinch and
hold, seeming dazed, till an exceptionally severe time in
the 16th and 17th rounds had him broken up completely,
and the towel was thrown in.
Unlikelv Scanlan people will come at M ‘Call
again, as the Australian’s latest success was most con-
vincing of all three. Scanlan had grown a lot in muscle
and weight and was fighting better, vide his short-order
defeat of giant axeman Heckenberg. He was in perfect
fix and had long and beneficial tuition from clever, retired
middle-weight champion, Mick Dunn. M'Call is a heap
better fighter than he is generally credited with being.
His endurance is remarkable and is, doubtless, due to
careful living and abstention from alcohol and tobacco.
For long before coming to Sydney to fight M'Oall used to
drop his pick and shovel and have a “rough up” with
tough pugs, who happened to strike Armidale in their
wanderings, and there was always a big crowd to see the
“ go.” It invariably proved a profitable venture for
the visiting bruiser, as very little remuneration satisfied
M'Oall—sufficient unto the scrap being the joy thereof.
As The Bulletin is being printed a match for £2OO a side
and a big purse, between M'Oall and heavy-weight cham-
pion of Tas. (James), is being discussed and will probably
be arranged.
< • * »
Punters who speculate on pugs got a hard
knock at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night. When
White and Ellis met, something strenuous extending over
a big stretch of the 20 rounds limit was looked for. Bet-
ting ruled 2 to 1 on Ellis, who rushed things ; but White’s
fewer blows told a more severe tale—his left being very
effective. It was the same in the second and third
rounds, until White loosened up more than ever, and,
after stingful lefts and rights on head and ribs, he landed
a smashing left on the chin and the favorite dropped,
done. It was a big boil over, that seemed hardly to have
begun before it ended.
• • • •
Bert Weekes and colored .Toe Thomal at Sydney
Gaiety A.O. Saturday night scrapped for the Bantam
Championship. Both are “scientific” and the battle
was consequently much relished. Thomal was taller,
longer-reached, and l£lb. weightier than Weekes (Bst.).
Things were at concert pitch from opening, footwork
and defence on both sides being neat. Weekes’ punching
had most force in it and was better directed ; the other
laying himself out to evade more than to attack. White
jabbed left in the wind frequently while Thomal swung
left to ribs or side of head as opportunity offered.
Thomal’s dash petered out some after the eighth round,
and then t’other forced him into corners and banged
away with both hands. Thirteenth round had barely
opened when Weekes swung left on the black’s jaw and
brought him down with a crash ; then, losing his head
and rushing, he swung viciously at the fallen man while
he was stil 1 down, but, fortunately for himself, missed
him and black was counted out.
Geelong (Vic.) is just now getting some amuse-
ment out of, and bestowing much contumely on,
its town council, or what remains of it. The
trouble started a while back, when the town-clerk
resigned, whereupon one of the councillors hastily
followed suit the day before the clerk’s successor
was to be appointed and nominated himself for
the billet. The sudden proceeding so annoyed
four of the remaining eight councillors that they
promptly left the council meeting, and there
being thus no quorum, nothing could be done.
Several other council meetings were held, but as
the four objectors still persisted in remaining
away, nothing continued to happen. The offices
of surveyor and rate-collector and inspector were
also vacant at the time, and naturally they
remained vacant. Later a public meeting was
held, at which several weighty but insulting
opinions were given as to the whole council—the
part of it that favored the appointment of the
retired councillor and the part that didn’t —and
resolutions were enthusiastically carried desir-
ing the whole council to resign forthwith.
The council accepted these cheerfully, and filed
them away in the dog-kennel. Then another
Council meeting eventuated, at which —it being
manifest that if no rate-collector were appointed
no rates could be gathered in and the whole
scheme of city government must come to a stand-
still —that necessary officer and a surveyor were
engaged on a 12 months’ trial. Directly, however,
the question of choosing a new town-clerk came
up two councillors suddenly resigned and two
more unaccountably left the room, thus leaving
no quorum as before. The position now is that
the town is three councillors short of its tally,
while two of the remainder are obstreperous and
decline to attend to business, so no business is
done. Moreover, there is no visible way of getting
a quorum, as one of the resigned councillors was
returning-officer, and there is no process for
getting another returning-officer. It is a really
quaint position altogether.

Dr. Phillip Muskett, of Sydney, who had
already contributed more to Australian literature than
any other two doctors, has now adopted a larger means
to let Australians know what they are built of, how the
works go wrong, and in what way repairs may be made.
The new Medical Guide weighs eight or lOlbs,, and con-
tains as much good and solid matter as-a book that size
ought to. Its avowed object is “to supply to the people
of Australia a vast fund of Australian medical informa-
tion which they would otherwise never become possessed
of : ” while its chief value lies in the fact that it is
written by an experienced Australian regular practitioner
from an Australian standpoint; that, describing how
diseases arise, it shows in the most obvious way how
most of them may be avoided ; and that, while it pre-
scribes the medical treatment, it also tells in plain
English how, in most cases, by means other than by
drugs nature can be helped to do her own repairs. It
doesn’t encourage a person to feel unwell, however; in
fact, when anybody, after reading a “ Don’t-you-feel-well-
young-man?” advt., has specks before the eyes and
dreads the future, Dr. Muskett says he has nosophobia
and talks about the matter under the heading of
“ Insanity.” The Guide is printed by W. Brooks and Co.,
Ltd., of Sydney, and D. H. Souter illustrates it; and
between the two they prove that anybody can have
his liver presented in as many attractive colors by
leaving it to Australian craftsmen as by taking it to
Europe.
The gruesome Bent on the
workers:— J
The way they have been going on ever since I have
been here has been very hard on me. These men have
special privileges, it must be remembered. Yet thev
break the regulations. No employee has any right to
engage in any business. Notwithstanding this, I found
that one engine-driver was running a public house at
Prahran. Some of the men have farms. I had a letter
the other day stating that the wife of one railway officer
met passengers at Ararat, and touted for her boardina
house. y
The Bulletin, by a mere accident, also knows
about the Ararat case above quoted. Close on 18
months ago a railway painter fell ill, and
has been slowly dying ever since. He hasn’t
been a railway officer in the sense of getting pay
from the railways since his illness, and Bent has
no more right to howl at him than he has at any
other dying man that he isn’t acquainted with.
(The habit of howling at a dying man to whom
you haven’t been introduced should be dis-
couraged in any case; it is only fit for a
dingo.) The wife of this ex-railway man keeps
a boarding-house, and, as the world isn’t quite full
of Bents, the word is generally passed to any new
railway men going up that way to stay at her
house. And she “ touts” to the extent of meet-
ing such men at the station, or of going there to
inquire of her husband’s old mates if any are ex-
pected. It would be interesting to know if the
rest of Bent’s charges are anything like this one.

VENTILATION.
Our own breath is our greatest enemy.”
NO MORE STUFFY ROOMS OR MEPHITIC AT/lOSPHERE.—A perso* finds a room stuffy, opens the
window, sits in a draught and pays the penalty, and forthwith blames the fresh air. It must be admitted
that hitherto it has been a choice of evils, whether to breathe the polluted air of a room or run the risk of
cold from the draught of an open window or door. BUT ALL THIS IS NOW CHANGED.
THE PULLMAN AUTOfIATIC VENTILATOR (the invention of the famous inventor and builder of the
luxurious Pullman Railway Carriages) admits into any room equipped with it a constant supply of outside fresh air
free from dust, smoke, or wet, and ABSOLUTELY WITHOUT DRAUGHT, irrespective of weather conditions. It
gently diffuses the air and also acts as an exhaust. IT SOLVES ABSOLUTELY THE PROBLEM OF HEALTHY
\ ENTILATION. For sleeping apartments, offices, hospitals, lodge and smoking rooms, factories, halls, public
buildings, enclosed vehicles, schools, churches, &c., the invention is invaluable. It can be fitted to the window or
brickwork without disfiguring the original structure, and cost is so moderate as to bring the invention within reach
of the householder of most modest means. VENTILATE AND THUS REMOVE ONE OF THE PRINCIPAL
CAUSES OF LOST HEALTH AND PREMATURELY LOST LIVES. Particulars on application.
The Pullman Automatic Ventilator Co. of Australasia, Ltd.,

George Reid, at Zeehan (Tas.), on the
hatter episode again :
It’s a remarkable thing, but Australians are ready to go
to South Africa and shed their blood for the old country,
and yet won’t let a half-dozen of the same men land in
Australia.
The same mean old lie. The hatters did land in
Australia, and are there now. This weary howl
about the “ shutting out” of the men who came
in, and the “exclusion” of the men who weren’t
excluded, and the “rejection” of the men who
are here now, and the “ imprisonment ” of the
men who were never imprisoned, is an interesting
example of how the dull, mechanical, dogged
repetition of one dull, wooden lie can influence
public opinion.

Australasia. Not genuine without the signature.

Martin, pharmaceutical chemist, Southampton,
England.*
A letter from Tamworth (N.S.W.) :
The noisome condition of Tamworth, now exercising
pulpit and press, is irremediable, and it is too late to act
upon the advice of the Board of Health, or- evade the
probable consequences plainly indicated in the monitions
of Dr. Ashburton Thompson. Twelve years ago, writer,
then residing in Tamworth, publicly drew attention to a
prevalent practice of burying night-soil in back yards.
The service for removal of contents of dry-earth pans
must have been inefficient ; the contractor must have
been dilatory, or the charges above the means of poorer
ratepayers, or something. Anyhow, the nefarious bury-
ing dodge was resorted to. Writer knows houses the
yards attached to which have a subsoil composed of the
accumulated filth of years. Tenants leaving of course
fail to disclose aught touching the state of the yard
beneath the surface, and the incoming tenant proceeds
to dig, as did his predecessor, unconscious, until he
happens on a patch. Tamworth municipality is a whited
sepulchre. There are good buildings, and broad, tree-
shaded streets ; bad water, filth slung about promiscu-
ously, consequent malodor, and insanitary pestilential
result. Dr. Ashburton Thompson’s philosophic “let-’em-
do-as-they-dern-please ” attitude is due to the hopeless-
ness of the case ; for the accumulation of filth and its
disposition is such that it would be cheaper to shift the
town than to cart away the noxious matter. All the
Board of Health can do is to get the new cemetery ready
for the anticipated rush, and, if possible, rig up a sana-
torium on the hills towards Nundle or Moonbi.

Rain Record (same day).
“Jason” sings:—
Once more my gallant barque bestrides the seas,
And walks the waters like a thing of life;
Once more I trim my sails to catch the breeze,
Once more I sharpen boarding-axe and knife.
Here on my vessel’s deck I proudly stand,
And sing the Song of Sigurd as I sail;
Below me, at the oars, my trusty band
Urge on the vessel with the favoring gale
(Excuse me—this is only parenthetical —
I find the waves annoyingly emetical!)
A Viking and an Argonaut am I;
Yet seek not Oolchi, nor the Western Isle ;
No fiat of the gods do I defy,
Wherefore no kindly goddess on me smiles,
I lack Achilles’ thunder-welded shield
To turn the naked sword or whistling dart;
No blade like bright Excalibur I wield,
Nor spear of Bradamant or Britomart.
(But I have found neat classical allusion
Puts unread enemies to much confusion !)
No ! We have dared the wide and stormy main,
To know the meaning of an ancient Rune,
Which tells of a mysterious Cockaigne—
“ A land where it is always afternoon ”
The Country of the Rainbow and the Gleam,
Where water does not wet, nor fire burn ;
Where no one needs to go to sleep to dream,
And everyone may spend what others earn.
(I will not speak of “ lubberlands delectable,”
Because that phrase is really not respectable.)
Land of Cockaigne : Our fathers sought you long;
And we, too, seek—my bard, and dog, and I.
Though waves roll high and gales blow wild and strong,
The Mystic Rune will keep us warm and dry.
Even now the Fates are kind—for never rain
Falls where we sail: a floating Avalon 1
■Argo, good dog ! Lift up thy voice again
And lick my hand, for we must travel on.
(By Jove ! This animal is far too loving —
Get out of it, you brute ! Where are you shoving ?)
(Left travelling round the Commonwealth, look-
ing for the Land of Cockaigne.)

of windows. This is interesting as it goes to prove that there can be no risk in smoking GUINEA GOLD CIGARETTES.

Cardinal Moran (in a semi-religious newspaper)
on the visit of the King of England to the Pope
of Rome: —
The last king that made a vow to visit Rome was
Edward the Confessor, and when through State occupa-
tion he was unable to fill his promise, he erected the
grand Cathedral of Westminster, which is a monument
of his vow and a monument of his devotion to the Holy
See. But it is a strange thing that the first King Edward
of England made the vow, and now the best Edward
since then on the English throne has fulfilled it.
It is by such sycophantic nonsense that the clergy
are always exposing the true worldliness of their
calling, and the falsehood of their spiritual pro-
fessions. If Cardinal Moran believes, as he pro-
fesses to believe, that the R.C. church, with the
Pope at its head, is the earthly representative of
a Supreme Being who despised kings and rich men
and glorified a meek and lowly heart, why this wild
joy because King Ned patronises the Pope of Rome
with a visit ? And if Cardinal Moran believes,
as he professes to believe, that the “best man ” is
the good R.C. who doesn’t run after many girls,
or drink too much, or eat meat on Fridays, why
should he exalt cheerful, worldly Heretic Ned—
who has had his share of the sweet forbidden
fruit of life—over, say, Roman Catholic Edward
V., who died at the age of 13, presumably sinless
according to the Cardinal’s ideas? Why Aus-
tralian men don’t go to church much is easily
explainable since a church seems to be only a
church when there is a row or a collection to be
made, and at other times is a mere human lick-
spittle abject, crawling in the houses of kings and
rich men. Even the Cardinal’s history is con-
temptible unless he is shamefully ill-reported.
The Confessor was the third Edward —not the
first.
A Sydney shop sign reads—“ Sandwiches,
late F. Condy.” Cannibalism, or what?
“Alcohol is the chloroform which enables
the working classes to undergo the severe opera-
tion of living.’’

] )ear Reader.
A little while since, we were favored with your
instructions to forward Catalogue of Phonographs,
etc., and this was in due course sent to you. We
do not know whether you have since purchased
one, but if not, we would like to interest you as
much as possible with that end in view.
You can hardly conceive the enormous amount
of pleasure such an inexpensive instrument can
give you. Life in many Australian parts is very
dull and monotonous, and in our opinion, and in
that of all those who have them, the Phonograph
comes as a most welcome relief. What does it
do ? It sings all the old popular melodies or the
most up-to-date songs ! It plays with full band
effect all the most beautiful music ! It talks to
you, or laughs so heartily that you cannot resist
joining in ! It will play a waltz or a polka, a set
of quadrilles or lancers, and loud enough for a
whole roomful to join in merry tripping.
It not only does this, but it takes records too.
That means, that you talk to it and it talks back
and repeats your words in exactly the same
manner as uttered. If you play any instrument,
if only the mouth organ, it will truly and faith-
fully return the compliment and play back to you
in exactly the same manner. Sing to it, it sings
to you ; play to it, it plays to you ; talk to it, it
talks to you ! ! !
The next point is its simplicity ; wind it up and
that is all. It requires no skill ; anyone who can
wind a clock can work a Phonograph. There is
nothing to get out of order ; a little careful hand-
ling and a drop of oil occasionally is all the Phono-
graph asks for all the delight it can afford you.
The last (and not an unimportant) point is the
cost, and we will deal with the different machines
in their rotation of cost.
First: “The Majestic,” at £1 Is., an instru-
ment that is satisfactory in every way ; it does
everything we have spoken of, except take records,
and though the price is remarkably small, it must
not for a moment be thought that it is a toy. It
plays well and loudly, but naturally not so well as
the higher-priced instruments. But still “half a
loaf is better than none at all,’’ and if you are not
prepared just now for a more expensive instru-
ment, “ The Majestic ” will return you a hundred-
fold in pleasure the money expended on it.
Next comes “The New Gem, ’’Edison’s Machine,
a gem in every respect, cost £3, a grand little
instrument for the house This takes records as
well as playing, singing, etc., to you. It plays
two records (that is, sings two songs or plays two
band selections) with one winding. Note.—We
would specially recommend a larger sized horn
than that provided with the machine, although it
is of course not essential; but the cost is only ss.
more, and the difference in volume of sound is
very marked.
Next, “ The Standard,” £5, which is fitted with
the new speaker, the most marvellous improve-
ment made in the phonograph, and which was
only brought out last year; and unless you have
heard such an instrument play, you can hardly
believe it possible to get such sweet music. This
machine plays 4 records with one winding.
Finally, “The Home Phonograph” £7 10s., the
daddy of them all. The best made machine, the
finest results, and Edison’s climax of perfection.
This plays 6 to 8 records with one winding.
Now, we have tried to interest you and hope we
have succeeded and not wearied you, for although
we sell Phonographs, as we do dozens of other
lines in our business, still at the same time we
consider that their mission in Australia is not yet
accomplished, and that there are hundreds of
homes which would be made much more cheerful
and happy, did the heads of them but know how
much pleasure they were missing for the sake of
sucli a small expenditure.
Things are dull and times are bad, but the
Phonograph will help to dispel the gloom and
make you take a much more hopeful view of things
in general. We wish you good luck, and hope
we shall hear from you again.
Yours truly,
393 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY,
and 213 QUEEN ST., BRISBANE.
P.S.—Records are not included in prices quoted,
le’se all cost the same, 2s. each, £1 Is. a dozen,
sts are always available.

Need to Despair
You may have been suffering for years from Rheumatism,
Gout, Neuralgia, Persistent Headache, Lum-
bago, Sciatica, Backache, Anaemia, Blood
Disorders, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles,
or some other of the many ills, pains and symptoms of
URIC ACID POISONING,
but, if you will consent to assist Nature, yon can yet be cured. The source of all such troubles is
defective action of the kidneys. When the kidneys are healthily performing their functions none of
these disorders can exist, any more than a pebble in a shoe can hurt your foot after the pebble is
removed. The kidneys filter from the blood of a healthy person about three pints of water every day.
In this water are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains in weight of uric acid, and
other animal and mineral matter, varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the
kidneys, from any cause, are unable to do their work properly, then some of this solid matter remains
in the system and becomes actively poisonous. Minute crystals of uric acid form in the blood and are
deposited in the joints and tissues of the body, causing the acute pain experienced when we have
Rheumatism. Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, &c. Sometimes the uric acid crystals
aggregate together in the kidneys and form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic and
Irritation and Inflammation of the Bladder. In fact, nearly all the aches and pains to which
the human body is subject are uric in their origin. It will be easily realised, therefore, how important
it is that we should
WATCH THE KIDNEYS,
and make sure that they are properly doing their work of removing these urinary poisons from the
system, as it necessarily follows that the absence of the poisons means absence of the pains they cause
The secret of the success which has attended the treatment of uric ailments by
Warner’s Safe Cure
is that the medicine acts in a scientific manner. It is a specific cure for all diseases of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER.
So intimate is the relation between the functions of the kidneys and those of the liver that it is
necessary, in order to cure any disorder of either of those organs, to employ an agent which possesses
an equally curative action on each. In other words, when the kidneys are sick, the liver is sick also,
and the contrary is likewise true. Warner’s Safe Cure is the only known remedy which possesses the
remarkable property of being of equal efficacy in cases of diseases of the kidneys and diseases of the
liver. By establishing a healthy and vigorous action of both the kidneys and the liver, Warner’s
Safe Cure cures not only all diseases due to uric poisoning, but Indigestion, Biliousness,
Jam ice, Sick Headache, Anaemia, and .Debility, which are all caused by the liver failing to
deal efficiently with the biliary waste products of the used-up tissues in the body.
Mrs. A. J. L. Barnett, Brighton Road, Glenelg, S.A., says:—“l suffered greatly from my kid-
neys and liver, and after consulting several doctors and getting no relief I had given up all hope of
recovery when I heard of Warner’s Safe Cure. After taking one bottle I was much relieved, and
after taking four bottles, I am pleased to say, was entirely cured.”
The commonest first syn p l om of kidney disease is Fain in the back. A surer test is to place
some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and let it stand until next
morning, if it is then cloudy or there is a brick-dust like sediment or if particles float about in it,
then the kidneys are not healthy and Warner’s Safe Cure' should be taken immediately or Brights’
Disease or some serious illness will result.
A treatise containing full particulars relating to Warner’s Safe Cure, and the diseases it cures,
together with many accounts of remarkable recoveries, will be sent free on application by H. H.
Warner and Co., Limited, Australasian Branch, Melbourne.

Honorary Repentance.
Kipling, the bounding bard of blood, has perpe-
trated a new “poerr' *n a minor key. It is a
poem of friendliness and honorary repentance —
not the repentance that hands back the loot, but
the kind that is sorry, and keeps the loot, and
puts 3d. in the plate to square matters with God.
It addresses the Boer in such phrases as : “I will
repair the wrong that was done to the living and
the dead,” and “ I will lay my hand in my neigh-
bor’s hand and together we will atone.” This is
the language of the well-dined alderman who
wants to know why the rest of the world isn’t
happy—the fat, beery commonplaces that mean
nothing, barring an affable gorged feeling. They
don’t signify that the stolen republics will be
restored ; or even that any reasonable measure of
self-government will be given to the conquered.
They don’t imply that the project of flooding and
desolating the land with hordes of leprous
Chinese will be abandoned. They don’t convey
the news that the yoke of the German Jews at
Jewhannesburg will be lightened in the least.
They are nothing more than a maudlin, beery-
friendly invitation to the vanquished to
look happy on general principles. An immense
amount of the Britisher’s reputation for gene-
rosity in large State matters is built on this same
kind of honorary repentance. Many other
nations would have taken Warren Hastings’
merciless looting of India as a matter of
course; Britain denounced it as a national
disgrace, and left it at that. No nation
ever spoke of itself with more unanimous bitter-
ness than Britain did after it had waged the
hideously unjust opium war against China, but it
hung on to the advantages gained just like any
common, ungenerous, unrepentant State. No
nation ever agreed more largely and generally
about, its own sinfulness than Britain did over
the seizure of the Diamond Fields, while hanging
on to the Diamond Fields just like the unre-
generate buccaneer any other nation might have
been. And now its bounding bard of blood strikes
the first official note of repentance for the care-
fully engineered war with the Transvaal, and
in later years the feeling will spread,
and . a calm dispassionate Britain will
admit in the dust of grief and the ashes
of sorrow that it did wrong—and keep the
plunder. Britain is the land of honorary
repentance. No other community on earth
speaks more severely, while hanging on to
the profits, about the way it acquired them. And
this honorary repentance seems to pay, as well as
make the community feel good. As a general
thing Britain steals more soil (and repents of it
afterwards) than any other six Powers that know
no repentance. It speaks in bitter reprobation
about itself for prigging ten square miles of terri-
tory, while unabashed and conscienceless rival
looters have great difficulty in prigging one mile.
Britain even does odd jobs of repentance for
other countries. When it isn’t being sorry in an
honorary way over the manner in which it gained
much of its own property, it has time to be sorry
at the sinful fashion in which other States (which
are not at all sorry themselves) remove their
neighbor’s landmark. Britain is probably the
most religious of the Great Powers, but a great
imperial steal can’t be built on religious princi-
ples, so it compromises with its conscience by the
cheap sorrow that comes after the profits have
been gathered in, and abases itself in seven verses
by Kipling. It is a beautiful thing to repent in
seven verses by Kipling.

Victorian Justice Hodges on the Privy Council
(the jurisdiction of which in Australian appeals
the Federal Constitution attempted to abolish—
an attempt foiled by Chamberlain with the assis-
tance of the foreign-trade party in Australia):
Men who had occupied positions of judges in Crown
colonies, where they have spent the best part of their
working lives in dealing with laws which may or may not
he British, but which certainly are in some instances
Roman-Dutch, and in deciding upon various Eastern
customs, are appointed to seats in the Privy Council.
These men have had their mental and bodily powers
worn out by exhausting climates, and have gone to reside
in England after they have earned their retiring allow-
ances ; and yet they may be called on, as they were in a
recent Maoriland case, to consider a course of legislation
on a particular subject. Such men were not mentally
fresh enough nor vigorous enough to investigate masses
of legislation in some colony where the circumstances
were altogether strange to them, and where changes of
conditions were rapid.
Had Australia shown a firm front on the ques-
tion, the Privy Council might have lost its power
of reviewing Australian law; but the curse of
this continent is its foreign-trade party —the
freetrade section, which is, to a large extent,
German-Jew in blood and altogether Hinglish in
sentiment, which holds that this mere colonial
community exists only to use up the shoddy of
Europe, and must as little dare to have manufac-
tures as final appeal courts of its own.
“Less than the brawny individual who prefers
pick to pen.” Thus Sydney Evening News in a
column of twaddle on the “ Disadvantages of
Education.” The man who gets less is the city
clerk or shopman, assumed to be educated. The
“ brawny individual ” who gets more is the navvy
or unskilled laborer, assumed to be ignorant. But
the most manifestly ignorant person of all is the
writer of the article. Sum up the unemployed of
any Australian State and 70 per cent, at least will
be of the class known as unskilled workers. The
city clerk looms big because he hangs about
the city, but in N.S.W. to-day there are
ten swagmen out of work for one clerk.
As to wages, the average bush rate is 15s a week
and tucker, which pans out less than 4s. a day.
Ten hours at least in every day. No Saturday
afternoon, not much Sunday, and Xmas the only
holiday in the year. A tip-top tradesman may
make £150 a year, but he is nailed down to that
for the term of his natural life. Let any clerk
display knowledge, industry, steadiness equal to
that of the first-class tradesman, and it is hard to
tell where he will stop. He may become a Teeck
or M‘Millan. There is an awful lot of rot talked
about the men of brawn. Most of the talkers
would crack up in half-a-day if they took on
Brawn’s job.
+
Concerning a crying grievance :
“Your Honor; whether it pleases you or not, I’ve
got a word to say, and I mean to be heard. The word is :
Just you mind your business and-we’ll mind ours. Also,
we want no back talk or impertinence.” What would
happen if a foreman, or even a common juryman, got up
and addressed a meddling judge in that way? The roof
wouldn’t fall and the sky wouldn’t crack. Nobody, in
fact, could molest that juryman, because he would he
strictly within his rights. It is time jurymen jumped
for their rights, even if they jumped on to a half-dozen
judges. For the impertinence and the arrogance, and
also, and not infrequently, the ignorance of judges, are
as hard to be borne as their results are painful to be
endured. Judge Docker (N.S.W.) is a sinner by continu-
ous and immemorial usage in all these ways. In a recent
case the jury had considered the case for 21 hours and
could not agree. Then his Honor flew round something
like this. “ But why can’t you agree ? The case is plain
as a pikestaff to me, and I’ve explained it to you, so that,
if ordinarily intelligent, you cannot fail to understand.
Look at the cost of a new trial if you fail to agree. I tell
you, you ought to agree.” Then they went back and
agreed, and the prisoner got twelve months.

Q. opal fields, far out West, have been floefifW.
The gougers have been doing fairly well lately ; their
greatest drawback is want of a regular market, as there
are no resident buyers. Why they don’t co-operate to
establish a selling agency in the city is a mystery.
The fact re Etheridge (N.Q.) is that nothing
definite is known about the deep, or even comparatively
shallow, ground. The half dozen or so shafts sunk 400 ft.
or more, don’t prove 1200 square miles of country. Unfor-
tunately, such data as they do supply are unfavorable,
and payable ground of any extent has not been found
below 300 ft. The Cumberland, out of the upper levels of
which O’Brien, Hunt, and Steele made a pile, is the only
show on the field which has been extensively and intelli-
gently prospected below 400 ft. (down to over 1000); the
result was that the plant was sold and the mine aban-
doned. Still, as the mine is on the extreme edge of the
gold-bearing country, that is not much of a guide.
Etheridge is a fair enough gamble, but it is as well to
bear in mind that, although well over a million has been
put into the field during the last 25 years, last year’s
gold from 78 mines (including Woolgar) was only 66820 z.
Chillagoe (N.Q.) smelting costs, which were up
as high as 285., have been brought dow 7 n to 17s. lOd.
Shepherd, formerly constructing engineer at Lyell, is now
in charge. The April return (from Chillagoe ore only)
was, approximately, 144 7 3 tons copper, 97140 z. silver,
and 21 tons lead from 2997 tons ore ; this is equal to 4‘B
per cent, copper, 3Joz. silver, and under 1 per cent, lead
* * * *
Before the Chillagoe slump Ruddigore Mining
and Investment Co. (capital £1000) took up a lot of
position blocks to float subsidiary concerns. The boom
expected with the opening of the railway and the
smelters did not eventuate, and the Ruddigore Co.
passed out as an active concern. Then a local speculator,
E. B. Torpy, took the Consols, one of its shows, on
tribute, and since October has sent to smelters 2500 tons
high’g'rade carbonate ; he has paid £l5OO in royalty, and
put something in his own till.
* • * *
Yet another Q. syndicate has failed to toe the
financial scratch. Last session a Bill was engineered
through the State Houses giving a large concession to
the Glasgow-Gympie co. Objects as stated were to test
the deep ground, and inter alia to provide lots of toil for
the wurrikin’ man ; but, for some murky reason, the de-
posit of £5OOO has never been paid, although the stipu-
lated time has passed. Evidently the attempt to sell the
concession to a big company didn’t come off.
* » • *
The Arltunga gold boom has resulted in some
nibbling at the great land-grant railway steal. One
Capt. Barclay, an alleged “ expert,” recently arrived in
Adelaide to tender for the railway, and claims to repre-
sent a powerful London S3 7 ndicate "(memo., the Adelaide
dailies said Snow had a “ powerful company” behind him
when the tramways steal was being boomed, but nary a
co. can be found now). Barclay is, of course, incidentally
inquiring about the 90,000,000 acres of land, including
minerals, which the Jenkins Iscariot Ministry offers as a
bonus to the co. making the line. In fact, Barclay’s co.
may be more anxious about the 90,000,000 acres than the
mere detail of finding £6,000,000 to make the railway.
Preparations are being made at the Burra (S. A.)
for the restarting of the mine on the tribute system.
Before the mine can start work thousands of gallons of
water will have to be pumped out of the main shaft.
Tenders have been called for the carting of the ore which
will have to be cleared out of the drain (running from the
main shaft to the Burra Creek) before pumping can
begin.
* • * »
Bull-Cohen, who holds a big swag of Briseis
(Tas.) shares, and w 7 ho got most of them during the
Briseis boom, is fearing a reconstruction of that co. It
may, however, pull through without it, though it is just
now carrying about £40,000 of debt.
* ♦ » *
The unfortunate Sulphide Corporation (Broken
Hill) finds, after all, that it would be unprofitable to use
the lately-purchased Extended shaft, just outside its own
mine-boundary, as a main working shaft. Yet, so unsafe
would it be to rely upon the Kintore shaft, which may be
corkscrewed any day, that another shaft is to be sunk
east of the lode—that is, on the footwall side. Another
£30,000, or so, gone.

How tenderly does Proputty, in the shape of
the average municipal council, tread on its own corns !
It doesn’t tax itself more than it can help, but sucks the
central Govt, for regular endowments and special sub-
sidies ; and when it wants a park planted or a bridge
repaired it gets Carruthers, or some other conspicuous
patriot, to bail up the Treasurer for the money. And
even the little tax which it does strike it doesn’t usually
enforce. It doesn’t say, as the central Govt, has to—
“ The rate for 1920 is due and payable at the Town Hall
on May 1, and if not paid with in 30 days from that date,
10 per cent, extra will be charged.” It hires, instead, a
collector or two to wait on the alleged taxpayer, and
encourages him to think that it’s some sort of charity
| that is being touted for. If the alleged taxpayer doesn’t
pay promptly he isn’t spoken to with any harshness ; and
if he doesn’t pay at all it doesn’t matter much. The
comparatively small Lane Cove (Sydney) council, which
has no business to have any separate existence at all, has
just owned up to being close upon £6OOO in debt ; yet it
has over £lOOO of outstanding rates, some of them due
for 23 years ! The local council thinks that an “ earnest
appeal ” would bring in part of this—which exactly
illustrates the remark that Proputty is encouraged to
regard the local-Govt. concern as some sort of charity
turn-cut.
“ Chass ” : Re molasses cure for cancer.
I can recommend molasses as one of the best blood
purifiers going. Years ago I was troubled with
sores on my hands which would not heal. Was
advised to eat treacle, and in a short time the
sores all healed. These sore hands were very
common in northern Yic. during the late summer,
but by using a little treacle occasionally I have
always been free from them since.
Victorian railway items (from Melbourne
papers) before the strike :
The reduced rate for carriage of starving stock and
fodder for same is to continue until 31st May, being a
further extension of one month.
Mr. Shiels is confident that the railway deficit will
be £150,000, although the railway officials’ estimate is
£BO,OOO.
The firewood merchants cannot get sufficient railway
trucks for their business to the great detriment of their
trade.’
Shiels is by nature a raven, and seems firmly
resolved to make his miserable prognostications
come true. The extra £70,000 deficit which he
has promised, if it can’t be got any other way, is to
be acquired by not providing trucks for the pay-
able traffic (the firewood business is a highly pay-
able one, but when no trucks are provided to carry
firewood the business stops) and by extending,
month after month, the non-paying traffic of
carrying the stock of wealthy squatters at reduced
rates — i.e., at a dead loss.
Writes a Vic. correspondent :
The State is the Law. Employees of the State, having
a grievance, should submit it to the calm judgment of
the highest court, which is the People. To strike a blow
at the comfort, convenience and pockets of the People is
a mere attempt to intimidate the Supreme Court.
Quite true. Therefore, let the railway difficulty
be submitted to the people by a referendum or
even a general election. The railway men are
more than willing. The people have never been
consulted yeb _ They returned a majority to
support Irvine —but then Irvine didn’t tell them
that he and Bent were going to do this iniquity.
In this matter they got their job by false pre-
tences. And Irvine won’t give the people, who
own the railways, a chance to say whether they
want their property run in this idiotic fashion or
not. The people are the Supreme Court, but the
Supreme Court is shut, and Bent and Irvine squat
toad-fashion on the front step and refuse to open
it.
At Poverty Point.
The two big- Australian circuses—FitzGerald’s and
Wirth’s—had early struggles in the backblocks. About
’B2, the Wirth family struck a small township on the
Castlereagh at the annual races ; they were knocking
out a very precarious living as a travelling brass band.
Later they ran a small acrobatic side-show in opposition
to the late Jimmy Ashton’s well-known circus. The
Fitz Gerald co. struck a hard time along the Namoi about
’B7, and were compelled to lay up for repairs a few miles
from Gunnedah (N.S.W.), where their well-known pony
stallion Commodore stood the season.
Alice Crawford was “approached” by more than one
London manager during Wilson Barrett’s short season in
the Big Smoke ; so before the co. started its provincial
tour W.B. sent for the bonnie Bendigo girl and announced
a rise in her salary, and hoped she didn’t think of leaving
him. Miss Crawford has lost the massiveness that
threatened to mar her theatrical future, and is even told
by the other girls (probably in envy) that she is now too
thin.
“ Melb.” : A friend of Amy Castles’ father informs me
that he is a Welshman, and that Mrs. Castles furnishes all
the Hibernian blood. I met Mr. Castles at Camberwell
not long ago. He did not impress me as being Irish. An
unpretending little fellow, with a black moustache, who
looks quite young with his hat on and spectacles off.
Anyhow, he furnished the music, being enthusiastic that
way. I have seen it in print that his people were Wes-
leyan, and that Amy Castles’ grandmother had charge of
the Kilmore Wesleyan Church.
The Melbourne Shakspeare Birthday Festival evoked
four lady aspirants for the Stage. Carrie Haase bro'ke
the ice as Cleopatra, in full rig. A somewhat gaunt,
aquilinear young party, high enough ; and with the
fallacious grease-paints and proper artificial lighting of
the stage, she could look the Serpent of Old Nile. Lady
Macbeth, Hermione, and Constance in “King John”
might be more in her line, but where are such people
going to find the openings in these days ot Florence
Youngs and Carrie Moores? One, Maggie Neill, hit hard
in the queer witchlike part of Queen Margaret, in
Richard 111. Charlotte Hemming essayed Beatrice and
Rosalind, and seemed a rather better Janet Waldorf.
Florence Champion went in for Portia, with agreeable
effect.
“ The Worst Woman in London ” (about the most pre"
posterous of all the penny-dreadful dramas introduced to
Australia by Anderson) is promising to make a fortune
for an unsuspecting manager who staged it at the
Adelphi, London, as a serious melodrama. Previously 7
the “ Worst Woman ” had only prospered moderately on
its shocking demerits in Shoreditch and other places
where the average theatre goer is an illiterate consumer
of beer and nuts during the performance. Even at the
Adelphi, the traditional home of somewhat reasonable
shockers, this lurid work was but a gallery 7 success for
the first few nights. But somehow its extreme absurdity
got talked about in Society, and Percy and Algernon took
Lady Gwendoline to see it, and they all shrieked at the
situation and dialogues. A less awful drama might have
escaped the British aristocwaey’s sense of humor. “ The
Worst Woman’’ couldn’t fail to tickle the most tired
understanding. Result, a rush of weary 7 triflers to the
Adelphi, and stalls booked a month in advance.
“ Matchbox ” : Have just arrived from a trip through
western district of Victoria with a show, and can vouch
for “ Ludo’s ” statement that mummers are as plentiful
there as starving stock, and mostly in a worse condition.
Gave a show at Condah one night. Four miles away at
the Swamp was another troupe. At Wallacedale another
company of five was holding a rapt audience worth about
£2. Up at Branxholme was another co. Met three
troupes in one day’s travelling from Branxholme to
Hamilton. The toughest game that I have tackled so
far. Working for Bent is luxury to it.
“H.P.“ : I was much disappointed to learn, when he
died the other day, that the proprietor of one-time
“ St. Leon’s ” Circus was, in real life, only Jones. It
requires a lot of imagination to connect Jones with any-
thing romantic. St. Leon’s circus was a power in the
land during my school-days of 20 odd years ago. It
travelled the backblocks townships far beyond the rail-
road’s reach, and its cavalcade of horses, and team of
waggons, headed by the glittering band carriage, was
quite an event in our dead old township when it
arrived once a year or so. We school kids con-
sidered it excuse enough for a half-holiday, and were so
unanimous on the point that the school had to be closed
for the afternoon. There was great joy amongst us when
“ The Boneless Bounder of Bohemia” plugged our school
bully in the eye so effectually as to put it in mourning
for a week after. St. Leon was a name to awaken many
pleasant memories. And after all it was—Jones 1

Boyd, M.L.A., the Scotch egotist who
has been engineering the Gippsland coal trouble
on behalf of capital, has been less than half-a*
dozen years a straight-out Tory. One time he
acted as chairman for little Dr. Maloney during
an election, but Boyd got his eve on the Melb.
City seat, and apparently recognised that it was
necessary to wear a different kind of coat if he
wished to represent Flinders-lane and the shipping
offices. He is now the most platitudinous Con-
servative in the Assembly.
Golden-haired Mrs. Fraser, who persuaded
a Melbourne jury to find her not guilty of wilfully
shooting her husband, Dr. Fraser, is now reported
to have obtained an American divorce from that
viotim of matrimony. Mrs. Fraser, for a while,
ran a Melbourne pub., on the strength of her
notoriety, and also got some advt. by apply-
ing to be sent as a soout to S’Africa.
At the trial, the unfortunate Fraser made
little or no protest against the fairy tales of
his voluble wife and her counsel. He now lies bed-
ridden in lodgings at a Melbourne suburb, with
Mrs. Fraser’s bullet still imbedded in his brain.
Dr. Fraser pleaded hard to be let alone when
the surgeons were treating him as an obstinate
fool, who couldn’t live 48 hours with the lead in
his brain. After long and fruitless efforts to dis-
lodge the bullet, they left him alone to put them
to confusion by living on.
