The Diplomacy of Caroline.
[FOR THE BULETIN.]
“Ever heard how Caroline got) Professor Bart-
mer’s pupils?” The woman spoken to breathed
a humble “No !” and suspected weird complica-
tions in the incident.
“Promise never to tell, and Pll let you into the
secret of the Professor’s great kindness to our
friend.”
With a mental reservation in favor of her hus-
band and sundry female friends who wouldn’t
matter, the lady with the Mount Mellick work
promised.
“Well, then, I don’t mind telling. Caroline,
you know, was an old school-friend of mine in
Bungaroo, and when her father died, leaving her
and a young brother to the care of an unamiable
stepmother, it seemed natural enough that she
should come to the city to earn her living and
apply to me for assistance in that object. She
had been a clever girl at school ; I expected that
she would do well enough, till I met her—grown-
up.
“Then I knew that she wouldn’t. It was not
because she had changed from her school-girl self,
but because she hadn’t. She wore her pretty hair
strained back in a way that didn’t suit her, and
whioh accentuated that arch of the eyebrows
which still gives her a ridioulous expression of
surprised innocence. Also her manners were
abrupt, and I saw in five minutes that she hadn’t
developed the slightest tact.
‘ ‘ Now, you know the unwritten law of the world
regarding tact ; a man says what he pleases ; so
does a woman—what he pleases.
“I had a long talk with Caroline over her plans.
She told me she decided to be a journalist because
she could write. I thought the reason inadequate,
but took her round to some of the newspaper
offices.
“ When I saw her next she had left off writing
because she had discovered with indignation that
some of the newspaper chiefs were not always
quite truthful. When I told her that Editor
Ananias must • simply be matched by Lady
Journalist Sapphira, she made some remarks
about her ancestors. Her forefathers were
Caroline’s strong point. She said she didn’t
know who they were, and therefore could live up to
them better, never having heard any unpleasant
stories about them, as was the ease in more
aristocratic families.
“I put her into a little millinery business with
another lady; she advised Potts Point not to wear
certain hats because its complexions couldn’t
stand them. When her money was all but gone, I
got her into a tea-room, where a married man
made love to her and was repulsed with indigna-
tion.
“ Now a girl CSft turn an adventure of this sort
to advantage, if she can make it clear to Don Juan
that she repudiates him from holiness, and that if
circumstances had been different, etc., etc. In
reality the holiness is a sort of understudy motive
that might do good work if it got a chance, only
it never does. The average girl repulses the
sinful married man because she wouldn’t listen
to him under any circumstances. But if she is
worldly-wise she gets a little kudos by having a
‘moral struggle.’
“ Then sometime's- he helps her a little —not so
much as he would otherwise have done ; but still,
something. Do you think Caroline could do this ?
She hadn’t enough sense.
“Fortunately about this time I discovered that
Caroline could play very well. When I told her
that she must make her living as a pianiste, she
inquired with some sarcasm if musical proficiency
would fit her for that profession, or must she be a
sycophant and a hypocrite as well. I got quite
despondent about Caroline at this time.
“Anyway, I got hertotake finishinglessons from
Professor Bartmer, who meditated a year’s stay
in England. I was working to get his pupils for
her, but there was another candidate in the field.
She couldn’t play as well as Carry, but she had
tact.
‘ ‘ She used to besiege the Professor with flowers,
or requests for him to write in her birthday book,
etc. Caroline, of course, because she wanted
something from him, was as chilly to him as
possible.
“ Yet, if anyone needed the Professor’s pupils,
Caroline did. These finishing lessons had brought
her to the end of her resources. If successful,
however, she would be able, not only to support
herself, but to send for her little brother, who
lived unhappily with his stepmother. His name
was Ernest, and she used to behave sentimentally
over his photograph at night.
“About a month before the Professor left he
gave a farewell concert. At this entertainment
Carry and the other girl, Eva Something or other,
were important performers. Somehow, it was
understood by those in the know, that that even-
ing would decide which of the two ladies should
be the Professor’s successor.
“ For a wonder Caroline was liervous. When I
pulled her hair out of its stiffness in the dressing-
room she submitted without an argument. That
was a great sign of meekness in Caroline. Eva,
of course, was gorgeous. She talked to the Pro-
fessor as long as she could, and he looked at her
with mild approval in his gentle German eyes.
Sho had already done my friend one bad turn,
having contrived that the Professor should over-
hear Caroline say that she didn’t think him at all
good-looking.
“Caroline played her first solo well, while I
stood at the side and said nice things about her to
the Professor. I did not allude to her financial
difficulties, of course. Money troubles in a woman
seldom arouse sympathy in the average man;
they generally make her seem sordid to him,
somehow.
“ But I saw soon it was no good. You see they
were pretty evenly matched; so it was only
natural that he should give the preference to the
one he liked best, and who seemed to like him
best —a qualification still more pleasing to the
rnan of sentiment, as differentiated from the man
of passion.
“ Carry’s last item came on towards the end of
the programme. Her trembling fingers made
several mistakes, but I reflected it didn’t matter
now if she played the cat’s cradle.
“But I was mistaken. Suddenly there was a
little crash from the piano, and Carry almost tot-
tered from the platform. I caught her, supported
her to the dressing-rcom, and guided her to the
sofa, where she fainted.
“She didn’t look a bit interesting or impressive,
or anything, and the Professor was only politely
anxious till she opened her eyes and murmured
brokenly as if in a dream :
“ ‘Oh ! Ernest, and I love you so ; and now I
shall have lost you, my dear one—l 11 never be
with you any more.’
“ Then the remembrance that the Professor’s
Christian name was Ernest flashed before me and
I realised that Caroline had shown tact in spite of
herself.
“ That’s the story of Caroline. She’s getting on
well now, as you know ; having got plenty of
pupils for herself when Professor Ernest Bartmer
came back to resume his. No ! he never proposed
to her, but he helps her a good deal, and keeps
sorrowfully away from her, so as not to make her
anguish greater. He sees himself enshrined in
her maiden heart, and doesn’t know that she was
only worrying about a freckled-faced boy, more
im P or tant to her than all the men in creation.
“ Of course, Caroline would be mad if she knew.
I should like to see Caroline mad ; that’s why Pve
told you all about it—in the strictest confidence.”
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
The Herbalist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Op all the latter-day plagues, the itinerant med.
cine-man is easily the worst. You find him
everywhere; but his favorite haunt is the small
village, where he holds forth in the blacksmith’s
shop, or the parlor of the “ hotel.”
‘‘This ’ere,” he says to the blacksmith, "is my
’erbal liver mixture, made out of ’leven diff’rent
’erbs, from a reseet given me by the owner on his
dyin’ bed. I’ve got up to fifteen bob a bottle for
it, but I’m lettin’ it go now for two ’arf-crowns.”
The blacksmith, resting on the handle of his
bellows, replies: ‘‘There’s nothing the matter
with me, old chap ; liver nor nothing else.”
The hawker is a disreputable-looking but keen
old man, with a ragged, grey beard straggling
round the outskirts of his red face, which resembles
a neglected geranium garden within an ill-kept
hedge. His clothes are hung on odd corners of
his anatomy, and he wears neither vest nor collar.
“ Why don’t you get your horse something to
lean against?” inquires the blacksmith’s boy, who
has been inspecting the villainous turn-out at the
door.
“When you’ve done as much work as’im, sonny,
you'll need somethin’ to lean against,” replies the
hawker, sharply. “That ’orse,” he continues,
turning to the blacksmith, “is as ’ard as Pharaoh’s
’eart, an’ as good as gold. But ’ow about these
’ere patent medsuns ? ’Ere’s my patent tooth-
ache ”
“Never had a tooth-ache,” interrupts the black-
smith.
“ Well, my ’ead-ache cure ”
" Never had a head-ache.”
“ At any rate, it’s a handy thing to ’ave about,”
says the desperate vendor, “you never know
when you might ’ave ’em. They’re only a bob.”
“No, they’re out of my line altogether,” replies
the smith, turning to his fire.
“Will anny of you gentlemen,” resumes the
hawker, as a group of dairymen enter the shop,
“try my patent ’erbal mixtures. They’re the
rage wherever I’ve been. I took £l2 out of
W’racknabeal, and a bit more out of Col’raine.
Wait till I get my book of gen’wine testimonials
out of the buggy.”
He dives out to the trap and returns with a
horribly greasy exercise-book. “This,” turning
the leaves rapidly over till he finds the desired
page—“ this is an extraek copied from the Col’-
raine Albyn. Read it for yourself, sir.”
This is what, in crabbed handwriting, with
watery ink, meets the reader’s eye :
Quite a sensashun has been caused in the town by the
arrival of Professer Mixem with his famus erbel remedys.
As is well known they cure consumsion, sore eyes, gout,
paralises, hydates.
“Is that the way it appeared in the paper?
inquires the blacksmith’s boy, who has been
looking over the shoulder of the reader.
“Yes, word for word,” is the prompt reply.
“ Perhaps you ain’t aware that it’s seven years in
quod for perjury if any of them testimonials ain’t
true ? ” This was added as a clincher.
“Well,” replied the boy, pretending to feel in
his pocket, “ here’s half-a-crown to buy the
Albion people a dictionary.”
Disdaining to notice the remark, the medicine-
man continues: “I don’t s’pose you gen’lemen
know that the toff doctors fills yer up with
minerals. What do they know about ’erbs?
Never even ’erd of ’em. They just shoves bottles
full of strjcknum, beller-donny, quinine ”
“Gam !” says the irrepressible boy, who only
left school a month ago. “Garni who told you
quinine was a mineral? I don’t believe, bella-
donna is either.”
“When you start in the medsun business your-
self, sonny, I’ll be pleased to teach yer a thing or
two,” replies the hawker, with deep sarcasm.
“ Till then, I’ll thank yer to ’ang a weight on yer
tongue.”
“ Now, gen’lemen,” he resumes pleasantly,
“ ’ow about a bottle of my cramp cure—only two
bob a bottle. Just look at it”—producing a
lemonade bottle, without k label, secured with
a broken cork, and containing a thick, black
mixture.
“Queer looking,” comments a farmer.
“ 1 s’pose you mean the get-up,” is the reply
“well, you don’t ’ave to pay for a fancy label, a
flash bottle, and a red seal, and the rest of the
fakements the chemists charge you double for. I
put all the money in the mixture.”
“Well, I don’t mind if I have a bottle,” remarks
a farmer, passing over a florin, and a second
follows suit.
It is incomprehensible that men who can spot a
blind teat on a cowchains away, and whom it would
be impossible to swindle in a horse deal, will
swallow the tale of the medicine hawker with
actual relish. The magical bait is the word
“herbal,” which has the same effect on present-
day men as the word Mesopotamia had on the
pious old woman. The man who travels on
“mineral” remedies is unknown, while the
“herbalists” are legion. It is possible to con-
ceive of a time when Flemington will be deserted
on the first Tuesday in November, and there may
come a day when test cricket matches will be pro-
hibited by law; but when both of these calamities
shall have smitten us, the voice of the wandering
medicine-man will still be heard loudly in the
land, and the world “ herbal ” will still float con-
vincingly in the breeze.
F. R.
A Melbourne horse-dealer, returned
from Capetown, told this the other day: “I
landed at Durban and couldn't get trucks
for my horses, try as I might. At last I
greased the palm of a certain high official—£2
per truck—and then had no difficulty. At
Johannesburg I paid £2 a day for a room at
Heath’s Hotel, and hardly dared go out in the
street for fear of meeting old friends who were
hard up. I knew them as good, honest chaps in
Australia, and it was terrible to see them abso-
lutely stony broke. In ten days I paid away in
oans only, over £50 —a fi\er here, two quid there,
dso on. Then I got on the train and left.”

\
Is Caused by
Sick
Kidneys."
Everyone knows the purpose of
a water filter.—to filter the im-
purities from water.
Have you ever reflected for a
moment on the fact that you have
filters in your system a thousand
times more intricate and delicate
than the water filter—a thousand
times more important, too 1
These filters are the kidneys.
It is their duty to filter from the
blood the impurities which cause disease,
and to pass them from the system.
But sometimes the kidneys get sick through a fall or
strain, exposure, excessive use of liquor, perhaps, or one
of many other causes. Then they become clogged.
It’s no use to try to filter water with a clogged filter; before the water
can be purified the filter must be cleansed. It should always be kept clean.
The same, exactly, with your kidneys. When they are clogged they
cannot filter the blood properly ; then certain impurities are left in the system
that poison every nerve and organ. These poisons, this unfiltered blood,
cause Anaemia, Jaundice, Eczema, Pimples, Boils, Blotches, Piles, Varicose
Veins, Sallow Complexion, as well as Female Troubles, Neuralgia, Headaches,
Melancholia, and a hundred other ills.
A tonic or purgative medicine will often give temporary relief in cases of
this kind, but the trouble soon returns. It is NO USE to doctor the symp-
toms if you want a lasting cure—you must go to the root of the trouble and
doctor that.
A gardener who is weeding does not clip off the body of the weed, but
pulls it out by the root. The kidneys are the root of troubles brought about
by impure blood, and

fail to gently aid and soothe the kidneys, and to cleanse them from im-
purities. They cure the root of your trouble, not merely the symptoms.
Entirely vegetable in their composition, they do not injure the system, nor
purge.

stranger living in a distant town, but you are asked to place confidence

BUT BE SURE THEY ARE DOAN’S
My Old Proprietor.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Readers ! in historic pages,
In dim annals of past age 3,
You have learnt how kings and sages
Came to grief—
Caesar stabbed his Roman shawl in,
Great Darius fallen, fallen,
Hemlocked Socrates—and all m
Fashion brief.
These days, too, with all their glory,
Could tell many a saddened story —
Like a grim memento mori,
So to say :
Recently, in King street walking,
I was with a Fatman talking,
When I saw a spectre stalking
Down the way.
He was thin, yea, he was thinner
Than the lost soul of a sinner,
Than the ghost of a dead dinner,
Than a rake —
Yet I had a curious fancy
That I knew that shape unchancy,
And I said, “ It’s Mr. Clancy,
No mistake ! ”
His stiff hair was thickly frosted,
And he seemed with thirst exhausted,
So that Spectre I accosted
With a wink.
I said, “Pardon this intrusion,”
And he stared in dumb confusion
Till I made a slight allusion
To a drink.
Then his eyes, that had seemed dreaming,
Like a chemist’s globes were gleaming,
And he walked with visage beaming
To the bar.
“ Once again the Land of Goshen,”
Said he, trembling with emotion,
And he told the girl his lotion
Was Three Star.
So by alcoholomancy
I made sure that it was Clancy,
But he did not take my fancy
As of yore;
For he looked a dead-broke draper,
Not the man who used to caper
Round when we brought out his paper
» Years before.
Then he used to ride a thorough-
Bred like blazes round Gingurra,
And the town of Turramurra
Painted red ;
And you’d think, to see the hearty
Way he’d drink with Black M'Cartv,
Life was just a whisky-party
Then to bed.
Well, he said, a reptile rival,
A silurian survival,
Had, by some obscene contrival,
Crushed his hope—
Sold him up in fashion thorough,
And he had to leave Gingurra,
And was ploughing a hard furrow
Hawking soap.
Now I think it is a pity
That while soulless, fat banditti
Can go swelling round the city
On their cheek,
One who was an earnest heaver
Of the Archimedean lever
Should be left without a steever,
So to speak.
It was truth and not romance he
Told, for better men than Clancy
Tramp the streets in fact, not fancy—
Sad to see.
But the cold world is too busy
For great minds with high thoughts dizzy-
Look at Francis of Assisi !
Look at Me !
N.S.W.

The Contemplations of Casey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
IFocr a.in., and a howling westerly gale whipping
the sedate English elms of Collins-street into an
indecent suggestion of a delirious dance in the
native quarters of Port Said. There emerged
from the sheltering depths of a portico a voice
murmuring in monotone:
“ Six-fut-wan uv me, an’ here I am, stuck on a
bate loike this, spoilin' for divarsion, wanderin’
roun’ like a pig at a Donegal wake. ’Tis a dog’s
life, an’ few bones.”
“ Mornin’, constable ! ’’
“ Mother av Mary, an’ who are you ? Sure an’
I mistuk ye for the sargint. ’Tis about your size
he is, but better-looking maybe—at night.”
“ Thanks ; I’m grateful! ”
‘‘You wouldn’t be if ye knew him fer a week.
Have ye a match on you ? ”
“ I have, an’ some good weed. Try it ? ”
“ An’ why not?”
Two minutes of solid pulling, and he of the hel-
met broke forth again:
“Sure, an’ ’tis enough to drive a man to the
military. Here am I—divil a wan in Eussell-
street can give me a start in a row, and they plant
me here to waste. ’Tis all influence what does it.
There’s Holden, now. Him do they sind to
Antonio’s, the bull-fighter’s, where throuble’s as
thick as heads at a fair. Stricklan’, too, wanderin’
up and down Swanston-street, servin’ eviction
notices on thim Oitalian flower-sellers. Up to
his neck in glory is he all day. ’Tis a bad thing,
is influence, whin ’tis on the side of the other
malm.”
“ But surely there are always drunks?
“ Divil a drunk but in the Gardens; that’s the
place. ’Twas a polis magistrate, no less, I dis-
covered wan night. On the bend had he been, an’
’deed it must have been a foine wan. Whin Oi
come along, there he was on the footpath, asleep
an’ ondressed. His clothes were hung toidy loike,
on the pickets. Only his underpants and a singlet
had he on. Whin I woke him, ‘ ’Tis in bed, Oi
am,’ he sez, ‘ an’ a damn hard wan at that. Sure
at Menzies,’ he sez, ‘ ’tis a spring mattress at the
laste you expect for your money.’
“’Twas a wake later I met him, an’ slippin’ a
note into me hand, ‘Don’t forget, constable,’ he
sez, ‘to deny that outrajious report that Oi slep’
in a tree last wake.’ ‘’Deed sor,’l sez, ‘’n you
did not. ’Twas at the Ground Hotel, yer wor.’
“ Another wan do I remember coming down
Spring-street at daybreak. Drunk ! ’Twas worth
a wake’s pinsion to look at his breath,
livenin’ dress he wore. Where his hat had bin
was wan av’ thim pitcher hats, cocked over his eye
most ondacent. In his hand, do you moind ’twere
Christmas, wor a goose. He held it by the neck
at arm’s length, wid its bake pointed at him, and
whin I remonstrated wid him for not carrying his
liquor betther, he swore he hadn’t smelt it for
months. ’Twas the burrd, he said, had mismirised
him. Called himself the anshint mariner, and
the goose, he sez, ’twor some kind of a harbor
trus’ roosting on a gold ridge. Oi convinced him
he wor wrong, an’ be my sowl, he started to tell
me how in South America ’twor a game the boys
had o’ berrying a turkey in the groun’ with its
head alone above warter; thin on horseback
phwat does they do but gallop up and try an’ tear
the burrd from its moorings. The bloodthirsty
body-snatchers! ;
“‘I will enlighten your ignorance, constable,’
he sez, and, wid that, what does he do but lift a
gratin' in the strate, put the head av his goose
through it, throw his leg ‘cross a crooked stick he
had wid him, an’ prance along like a horse-guard,
no less. A grand foight he put up, an’ forty bob
it cost him for farin’ me uniform.
“Wan noight mesilf and Big Mac. had mit, by
way of divarshun, whin along comes the sargint
an’ old Dr. Shields. The doc. took wan luk at
Mac., an’ ‘Me mahn,’ sez he, ‘what the devil do
you mane by bein’ on duty in that state ? Report
yerself to me in the mornin,’ he sez. ‘ ’Tis dis-
graceful.’
“ ‘ Is it drunk he is, docther ? ’ sez the sargmL
“‘Drunk, be damned. The mahn's dyin’.
Nivir did Oi see such a case of ilifantus in me
career,’ he sez, an’ wid that he walked away wid
the sargint.
“ ‘Good Lord, Mac.,” I sez, ‘’tis terrible to
think how ye must be sufferin’. Is it catchin’ ? ’
I sez. .
“ ‘ You bet your loife ! ’ sez Mac., whin he had
done laughin’, an’ wid that he pulls out a bottle
what had slipped down from under his tunic, an’
was in the knee av his pants. ’Twas good beer,
an’ welcome.
“There was an inshpector that I moind av.
’Twas at Flinders-street, an’ there wus an old
harridan av a woman had bin makin’ loife onaisy
for me the night through. I wud shift her off me
bate to O’Meara’s, an’ whin O’Meara come back
he would shoo her on to moine agin.
“ Well, we played ping-pong wid her till along
comes the inshpector, an’ ‘ Take her to the cells,’
sez he. So Oi called a cab an’ left her to the
tinder mercies av the two of thim. ’Twas per-
suasion they tried, an’ thin argymint. Divil an
inch would she budge. O’Meara he climbed in
the keb an’ got a grip av her an’ pulled. How
that mahn wasn’t a dintist, I dunno at all.
“ ’Twas no use at all; so what does Mr. Inshpec-
tor do but drop his dignity an’ his bit av a cane.
Gettin’ a hould av the feet av her, he tries to
shove her in. She resinted this at wanst, an’,
d rawin’ up her knees till they was touchin’ her
chin, she let drive wid both fate the wanst. ’Twas
on the chist she caught him. Faith! and he
dropped as though he’d bin slugged, no less; an’,
to save himself, he made a grab at the skirts av
her. Something bruke, an’ there he was, siftin’
on the tram-track wid his arms full av female
clothes, while the colleen, dressed in a blouse an’
a pair av No. 9 boots, fled down the sthreet like a
deer.
“Well, Oi must be movin’, for ’tis a wicked
city, an’ full av bad characters in the shape av
inshpectors, sargints, an’ the loike.”
MAYBON.
“W.”: The Salvarmy lassie’s uniform is
ugly enough, goodness knows; but the fashion
of wearing the back hair done like a lorry horse’s
tail in a trades’ procession is positively unseemly.
No religion that wears its back hair like that can
be permanent.
While shunting operations were in pro-
gress at the Euroa (Vic.) railway station on a
recent evening, a schoolboy sat on the points to
view boss Bent’s minions at work. Getting the
signal to make the road, the stationmaster pulled
the lever, bringing together the points on which
the boy was sitting. Excruciating howls caused
the danger signal to be raised, when the slack of
the boy’s pants and a fair proportion of his nether
portion were found squeezed between the rails.
Had the guard and porter not been at hand, he
would assuredly have been run over, unless he
had sacrificed a portion of himself by main force
to save the remainder.
“ Vul ” on a complicated electric subject :
The recent narrow escape of several firemen from
serious injury or death through coming in contact with
telegraph and telephone wires, which had fallen foul of
the electric trolly- wires during a fire outbreak in King-
street, Sydney, has brought under the notice of the
average citizen the hourly danger he runs of being suddenly
and finally extinguished by electricity. In the case of fire-
men and others, there is always the possibility of coming
into contact with the wires whilst they are on ladders or
roofs, and receiving a fatal shock and then their re-
mains dropping on the crowd below and killing some-
body. In the event of the wires breaking and falling
amongst a crowd listening to a political orator or watch-
ing a dog fight the casualty list in the press next morning
would probably read like that of a first-class railway col-
lision. Except for the sake of cheapness, and through the
hankering to do in a hurry at the last things that have
been leisurely thought over for half-a-century, there is
absolutely nothing in favor of the overhead system as
against the underground method. In the latter case the
wires are placed similarly to the cable system, the arm,
which at present rises from the car roof, being placed and
controlled like the grip in the cable car. The whole
thing is safer, more convenient, and much more sightly
than the present overhead system. But, seeing that the
latter system is with us and is likely to remain for the
present, the authorities should take steps to see that the
wires are properly protected. The British Board of
Trade has issued strict regulations that no franchise
shall be granted to any co. for the use of electric traction
with overhead systems unless guard wires are fixed.
The same regulations are in force on the Continent. This
method of protection by guard wires is at present used in
Glasgow, and has proved most efficient. It consists of
an iron wire, supported by a span wire, placed 2ft. above
the trolly wire and Bin. to the outside, and is so arranged
that should the telephone and telegraph wires fall across
the trolly wire, they become dead and are quite harmless
to firemen or others handling them. But with such a
system of wire protecting wire, with another wire pro-
tecting the protector, the city streets will ultimately
become roofed in with wires, and the citizen will have to
take a trip to the country to have a look at the sky. The
most sensible method is to put the ’phone, telegraph, and
other wires underground ; and seeing that it will have to
be done sooner or later, it would be best to do it sooner.

“Floradora” was produced in Paris as an American
production, written and composed by Yanks, and coming
straight from New York. Nothing admittedly English
could become popular in Paris these times, the French
being a chivalrous and broad-minded people only at
Intervals. So a number of the flower-pickers on the Isle
of Floradora were represented as coons to give American
color to the scene.
“ J. 8.” : Florence St. John, in her autobiography in
‘‘M.A.P.” recently, relates her up* and downs since she
commenced her theatrical career. “ Married,” she says,
“ when only fourteen years and seven months old.” In
her account of her billets they ranged from 15s. to £2O a
week. Once her salary dropped from £l7 to £8 per
week, because she was recalled from the provinces to the
“ Big Smoke.” That was in’79. The rule works on the
reverse hinge nowadays. H. B, Farnie taught her more
about acting than anyone else. With all her powers of
mimicry she only laughed in earnest once on the stage.
That was when Farnie, watching her out of the wings,
accidentally shed his false teeth.
Since the inauguration of the Willoughby-Gbach co.
hardly a day hag passed without applications being re-
ceived from girls and women desirous of going upon the
stage. Mothers with talented daughters, aun ts with
talented nieces, pressmen with talented lady friends,
young girls, old girls, fat girls, thin girls, sad girls, jolly
girls, dark girls, fair girls, solemn girls, funny girls, nice
girls, and other girls, Government House girls, and ser-
vant girls—all stage-struck, all bitten by the virus of
public admiration —besiege Manager Geach, week in,
week out, for a chance to go upon the styge. Every new
manager is struck this way. The great unanimous desire
of the great aggregate Girl to go on the stage seems over-
whelming.
Although Charles Santley became very devout late in
life, he did not also become an ascetic. When, some
years ago, he was at Broken Hill, the mayor of the place,
delighted with his conviviality, slapped him on the back,
saying, “ You’re a jolly good sort, Santley ! I’ll conre
and hear you to-night; I shall, damme, if it costs me five
bob! ”
A fearful thing has happened in London. The Times—
the Times! has had the door of the Garrick Theatre
slammed in its face. A day or two afterwards Henry
Arthur Jones, whose “Whitewashing of Julia” was
“ on,” wanted to explain. The Lord High Editor, who
hadn’t publicly observed the slam, said Jones might do so
if first he got down upon his knees. Jones thereupon sent
along two columns, which the Times didn’t print, but
which half the other London papers did. “The notices
of my plays in the Times,” Jones wrote in two columns,
which the Times wouldn't print, “can scarcely be called
criticism at all. They have been largely composed of
personalities and allusions to my habits and expenses . .
Why, then, should we send a card to Balaam, and ask
him to drop in and curse us?” And the‘Times went on
not noticing the whitewashing of either Jones or Julia.
“N.”: Anent the great scandal of 1847 in a British
regiment—the 46th, if I remember aright— The Bulletin
enlightens many readers as to what it was all about. In
the course of the court-martial one question and answer
became so frequent as to be turned into a byeword. It
was “ Don’t you remember ? ” “No, I don’t recollect.”
This was a gag all over London. One gentleman, for-
merly a member of the regiment, came to Melbourne
about 1856, and appeared at the Theatre Royal in
“ London Assurance,” but did not take.
, A London musical critic complains that it was a mistake
for composer Strauss to accept certain verses by one Ritter,
as the true explanation of his remarkable symphonic
poems entitled “Death and Transfiguration.” Ritter
described the opening of the symphony as the picture of
“ a narrow sordid garret, lighted by one spent rushlight’s
gleam.” But the critic prefers to think that the music
reveals “ a desolate moor on a moonlight night.” Aus-
tralian musical cranks may be advised to remember this
difference of opinion next time they read the gorgeous
word-pictures with which Prof. Marshall-Hall explains a
Beethoven sonata. When the Professor states, on the
programme, that a prolonged movement for the clarionets
and trombones signifies a fight to a finish between angel
Gabriel and Beelzebub, it may be that the conflict onl>
means that Bodger, the baker, has come home in an
nusober state, and is getting violently abused by Mrs.
Badger.
Alfred Dampier, still struggling gamely with an occa-
sional “Shakespearian revival” in the Vic. provinces,
recalls that his first appearance as a pro. was at Stratford-
on-Avon, where Shakespeare was born. Many worse
things than this have happened to the Bard. Dampier, at
least, has a reverence for the old author and doesn t call
him Bacon.

“He has the making* of a great actor.’’ Toole was tht
indispensable Artful Dodger. Kssley used to be immens<
as Sairey Gamp. Seymour Hicks lately did well a

a snufflesome voice, has passed out from consumption
in his 56th year. The Cog-ills, Charles and Harry, firs
came to Australia with Billy Emerson’s co. some 19 year
ago, as far as present scribe can remember. Whe
Emerson went back to ’Murka the pair of burnt-cor
brethren remained and set up in business on their ow>
—in competition, and once in conjunction, with Fran I
Clark, also Harry Rickards, who was then only a period
cal visitor to Australia. For a while .John Gourlav wa?
associated with the Cogill Bros.’ Minstrel and Burlesqu
co., and that was probably their most prosperous time
Of later years they struggled hard to hold their groom
against the permanent Rickards show, but weight o
metal was too much for them When last heard of it
this country, Charlie Cogill was running a lit tle vaude
ville co. in out of the way places. Sydney and Jlelbourn<
lost sight of him. He went back to America, and died it
San Francisco.
How “ Alba ” was shaved :
Beware of the shallow-pated barber who takes his
Corelli and his Bible seriously. Entered a hair-saloon
recently and was immediately approached by a cadaver-
ous flesh-hacker, who rammed me into a chair with the
force of a garrotter. It was a sweltering day, and as I
looked into the razor-man’s face I saw certain small sky-
rockets moving across the pupils of his eyes. Aftei
blinding me with lather, he proceeded to glare at me like
a long-lost tiger. “ There was no shaving in Judea while
Herod ruled the Israelites,” he said, bitterly. “The
apostles never shaved.” He dragged his razor across my
throat with brutal violence. My protesting voice was
hardly strong enough to pierce the lather he had walled
around me. I said I never knew an apostle who was game
enough to be shaved. They preferred to die in prison
By way of answer he dug his knuckles into- my throat
and scarified the flesh under my left ear, and before he
had reached my chin he again flew off the handle.
“The multitude liberated Barrabas and sacrificed
Christ,” he said, grimly. I replied that, owing to the
scarcity of firewood in Israel, it had been deemed advis
able not to waste a tree on such an uncleanly thief,
also explained through the lather how the ancient robbe
had gone to Egypt about a.d. 40, and had become Owe
Barrabas, Minister for Works at Alexandria. Whereupoi
I looked into the barber’s eye and saw that I was jesting
with a lunatic. With slow, iron fingers, he twisted thi
•having-cloth about my throat, and then reached for a
longer and more murderous-looking razor than the one
he was using. I saw that my just deserts were hurrying
up. The towel almost strangled me ; hitherto I had always
pictured my end as happening in the South Seas —the
schooner’s ribs hammering the reef, my naked body flung
beachward, head to the sun, heels to the sea, with the
gull* drowsing in the trade-wind above-and here I wa-
about to die, surrounded by hair-wash. I made a mental
resolve that if I escaped from the chair alive I would lead
a better life and go to picnics like other men. The boss
barber had been watching us for some time. He came
behind my chair silently and took my man very gently
by the wrist. “You can knock off and pack up your
tools, Seth,” he said, somewhat sorrowfully ; “ I’ve been
watching you all day.” Seth dropped his razor and
gaped like a man shaken from his sleep. “ I haven't done
anything. What’s the matter?” he asked, stupidly.
“ Nothing,” said the boss. “Git, and take your Bibit
with you.” Last time I saw Seth he was hurryinj.
towards Dill Macky’s printing office, a razor-case in one
hand and a large, wobblesome bag in the other. Tin
bag fascinated me. It was of the kind much used by the
muffled officers of the Inquisition. It always turns up at
a big murder trial. And as Seth the barber jogged along
it trembled and shook as though it held the head of an
atheist or a priest. Has anyone seen the Cardinal lately :
